Only God Is Pre-eternal by Essence: Distinguishing Temporal Pre-eternity
States the creedal claim and elaborates on temporal pre-eternity in different schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between pre-eternity by essence (qadim bi al-dhat) and pre-eternity by time (qadim bi al-zaman), and how different schools apply these terms.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity belongs to God alone by essence; everything else is either temporally pre-eternal or originated.

This passage by Sanusi establishes a foundational creedal claim: only God is pre-eternal by essence. However, the term 'pre-eternal' can also be used in a temporal sense, and different theological schools apply it differently. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion when reading later proofs about God's oneness and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the distinction between 'pre-eternal by essence' and 'pre-eternal by time'.
The passage uses two different Arabic phrases: قديم بالذات and قديم بالزمان.
2
Identify the three groups mentioned
The passage discusses philosophers, theologians, and the Mu'tazila. Notice what each group affirms or denies regarding pre-eternity.
Philosophers apply temporal pre-eternity to many contingent beings; theologians apply it only to God's attributes; Mu'tazila are compelled to accept states (ahwal) in pre-eternity.
3
Reflect on the Mu'tazila's position
Consider why the author says the Mu'tazila are 'compelled' (lazimahum) because they posited states. How does this relate to the denial of eternal attributes?
The states (ahwal) are subsistent with the essence, leading to multiple pre-eternal entities if not careful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : لا قديم بالذات (۲) سوى الله تعالى (۳) ، وأمـا بـالـزمـان (٤) فزادت الفلاسفة كثيراً من الممكنات (٥) ، والمتكلمون صفات الله تعالى (٦)، ولزم المعتزلة حيث جعلوا العالمية ،والقادرية والحيية والموجدية، أحوالاً ثابتة في الأزل مع الذات، ولا نعني بالوجود إلا ما عنوا بالثبوت) .
لما سيأتي من أدلة توحيد الواجب، وما وقع في عبارة بعضهم، من (۷) أن صفات الله تعالى واجبة أو قديمة بالذات فمعناه بذات الواجب بمعنى أنها لا تفتقر إلى غير الذات. وأما القديم بالزمان فجعله الفلاسفة شاملاً لكثير من الممكنات كالمجردات والأفلاك وغير ذلك على ما سيأتي . والمتكلمون منا لصفات الله تعالى فقط، حيث بينوا أن ما سوى ذات الله تعالى وصفاته حادث بالزمان
He said: There is no pre-eternal by essence (2) except God Most High (3). As for pre-eternal by time (4), the philosophers added many contingent beings (5), and the theologians [added] the attributes of God Most High (6). And the Mu'tazila were compelled because they made the states of being-knowing, being-powerful, being-living, and being-causing-to-exist as states subsisting in pre-eternity together with the essence, and we do not mean by existence except what they meant by subsistence). 
Because the proofs of the oneness of the Necessary Being will come later, and what occurred in the expression of some of them (7) that the attributes of God Most High are necessary or pre-eternal by essence is meant [as] by the essence of the Necessary Being, meaning that they are not in need of other than the essence. As for the pre-eternal by time, the philosophers made it include many contingent beings, such as the immaterial intellects, the spheres, and other than that, as will come later. And the theologians among us [apply it] only to the attributes of God Most High, where they explained that everything other than the essence of God Most High and His attributes is temporally originated.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage presents the central theological claim that only God is pre-eternal by essence (qadim bi al-dhat), meaning His existence has no beginning and is not preceded by any cause. The author then distinguishes temporal pre-eternity (qadim bi al-zaman), which is applied by philosophers to contingent beings like immaterial intellects and celestial spheres, and by theologians (mutakallimun) to God's attributes, since attributes are not temporally originated but depend on the essence. The Mu'tazila are said to be compelled (lazimahum) because in denying eternal attributes, they posited states (ahwal) that are subsisting in pre-eternity with the essence, leading to multiple pre-eternal entities. The commentary clarifies that when some scholars say attributes are 'pre-eternal by essence', they mean by the essence of the Necessary Being, not independently. The passage concludes by reaffirming that only God's essence and attributes are not temporally originated; everything else is hadith bi al-zaman (temporally originated).

Key Terms
قديم بالذات
قديم بالزمان
صفات الله
أحوال
المعتزلة
الفلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pre-eternal by time applies to God's essence in the same way as pre-eternal by essence.
Correction: Only God is pre-eternal by essence; attributes are pre-eternal by time, meaning they are not originated but depend on the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, to what do the theologians ('mutakallimun') apply the term 'pre-eternal by time' (qadim bi al-zaman)?
A) The essence of God
B) The attributes of God
C) The contingent beings of philosophers
D) The states (ahwal) of the Mu'tazila

Key Takeaway
Only God is pre-eternal by essence; attributes are pre-eternal by time, and all contingent beings are temporally originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life have you been treating something other than God as if it were pre-eternal—something you rely on as if it has always been there and will always be there? What would it mean for you to truly internalize that only God is pre-eternal by essence?
?
You affirm that God's attributes are pre-eternal, but how does that change your daily relationship with His names like 'the Living'? Are you living with the awareness that His life sustains you at every moment, or do you treat it as an abstract concept?
?
The Mu'tazila were compelled into a problematic position because they rejected attributes. Are there any beliefs you hold that, if fully examined, lead to a logical inconsistency about God's nature? Are you willing to abandon them for the truth, even if it challenges your comfort?

Philosophers' True Position on Divine Freedom and Eternal Act
Al-Taftazani clarifies the philosophers' true doctrine on divine agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' actual position on divine power and choice, and how they differ from those who deny it outright.

Core Idea
The philosophers affirm God's power and choice but deny that they cause multiplicity in His essence.

In this passage, al-Taftazani clarifies the philosophers' true stance, citing Sharh al-Isharat. He shows that they do not reject divine freedom; rather, they reinterpret it to avoid any multiplicity in the divine essence. This distinction is crucial for understanding the debate between theologians and philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the philosophers' claim. Notice the negation and affirmation structure.
2
Identify the key affirmations
List what philosophers affirm: power and choice, no multiplicity in essence, agency unlike created beings, eternal perfect agency, world eternal and dependent.
3
Understand the precaution
Recognize that this position is a precaution (ihtiraz) against the disgrace of denying divine attributes.
4
Compare with other views
Think about how this differs from the Ash'ari view and from outright denial of divine freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم. صرح في شرح الإشارات بأن الفلاسفة لم يذهبوا إلى أن القديم يمتنع أن يكون فعلا لفاعل مختار، ولا إلى أن المبدأ الأول ليس بقادر مختارا، بل إلى أن قدرته واختياره لا يوجبان كثرة في ذاته، وأن فاعليته ليست كفاعلية المختارين من الحيوان ولا كفاعلية المحبورين من ذوي الطبائع الجسمانية، وإلى أنه أزلي تام في الفاعلية وأن العالم أزلي مستند إليه، وأنت خبير بأن هذا احتراز عن شناعة نفي القدرة، والاختيار عن الصانع.
Yes. He stated clearly in Sharh al-Isharat that the philosophers did not hold that the eternal is impossible to be an act of a free agent, nor that the First Principle is not powerful and free-choosing, but rather that His power and choice do not necessitate multiplicity in His essence, and that His agency is not like the agency of free-choosers among animals nor like the agency of compelleds among those with corporeal natures, and that He is eternal, perfect in agency, and that the world is eternal, dependent upon Him. And you are aware that this is a precaution against the disgrace of denying power and choice to the Maker.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage offers a clarification about the philosophers' actual doctrine, citing Sharh al-Isharat. The author asserts that philosophers do not deny that the eternal can be an act of a free agent, nor do they deny that the First Principle is powerful and free-choosing. Rather, they hold that divine power and choice do not entail any multiplicity in the essence, and divine agency is unlike that of animals (who act by choice) or of compelled beings (like natural agents). They affirm that God is eternal and perfect in agency and that the world is eternal and dependent on Him. The author notes that this position is a precaution (ihtiraz) against the disgraceful accusation of denying power and choice to the Creator. Thus, the philosophers' view is distinguished from both the Ash'ari position and the charge of outright denial of divine freedom.

Key Terms
فاعل مختار
قادر مختار
كثرة في الذات
فاعلية
أزلي
احتزاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That philosophers deny divine freedom and power.
Correction: They affirm God's power and choice but reinterpret them to avoid multiplicity and anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint
According to the clarification in Sharh al-Isharat, what is the distinctive view of the philosophers regarding divine agency?
A) They deny that God is a free agent
B) They affirm God's power and choice but say they do not necessitate multiplicity in the essence
C) They consider God's agency identical to that of animals
D) They claim the world is not eternal

Key Takeaway
Philosophers maintain that God is powerful and free but His agency does not entail multiplicity or likeness to created agents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God's 'choice' or 'will,' does it naturally feel like a human kind of choice – involving deliberation, change, or desire? How does this passage challenge you to purify your concept of divine agency from any taint of multiplicity or resemblance to creatures? What specific change in your mental image will you make starting today?
?
This passage warns that denying God's power and choice is a 'disgrace.' Honestly, have you ever spoken or thought in a way that implicitly reduces God's influence to a mere philosophical necessity or natural law? If so, what practical step can you take to consistently affirm His active, free agency without slipping into anthropomorphism?
?
The philosophers affirm God's power and choice but insist they do not cause multiplicity in His essence. Can you accept that God's attributes are in no way like ours – even when we use the same words? Where in your heart do you still resist that complete transcendence? To help yourself, write down one specific area of life where you must actively remember that God's agency is unlike anything you experience.

Divine Attributes

Defending Eternal Attributes: The Necessity of Concomitants
Defends eternal attributes against necessitarianism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash‘arī theology defends the eternal attributes of God from the charge of necessitarianism, specifically by distinguishing the basis of causal need (ḥudūth vs. imkān) and by explaining attributes as necessary concomitants (lawāzim) of the essence.

Core Idea
God's attributes are not necessitated effects but essential concomitants.

A philosophical objection threatens to collapse the Ash‘arī view of God's eternal attributes into the necessitarian system of the philosophers. The author responds by clarifying the very foundation of causal dependency: it is origination, not mere possibility, that requires a cause. Moreover, attributes are not separate effects but necessary concomitants of the essence, with no real distinction to allow causal interaction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first statement: 'If it is said...' The opponent argues that if attributes are eternal, they cannot be produced by choice, so they must be necessitated. Note the key term 'ījāb' (necessitation).
This objection reflects the philosopher's view that from the One only one can proceed necessarily.
2
Grasp the key distinction
Focus on the author's first response: 'The cause of need for an efficient cause in our view is temporal origination, not possibility.' This shifts the entire framework. Understand why this rebuts the objection.
For the Ash‘arī, eternal things do not need a cause; only originated ones do.
3
See the argument about concomitants
Examine the second point: attributes are not effects (āthār) but necessary concomitants (lawāzim) of the essence. Also note the argument that there is no real distinction between God and His attributes for causal interaction.
The phrase 'لو سلم' indicates a hypothetical concession—even if they were effects, the causal relation would not hold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: صفات الواجب عندكم موجودات قديمة، فيمتنع استناده إليه بطريق الاختيار ويتعين الإيجاب. قلنا: علة الاحتياج إلى المؤثر عندنا الحدوث لا الإمكان، وصفات الواجب وإن كانت مفتقرة إلى ذاته لا تكون آثاراً له البتة، وإنما يمتنع عدمها لكونها من لوازم الذات. ولو سلم، فالتأثير والتأثر إنما يكونان بين المتغايرين ولا تغاير ههنا وسيجيء لهذا زيادة بيان.
If it is said: The attributes of the Necessary, in your view, are eternal existents, so it is impossible that they are attributed to Him by way of choice, and necessitation (ījāb) is necessitated. We say: The cause of need for an efficient cause (mu’aththir) in our view is temporal origination (ḥudūth), not possibility (imkān). And the attributes of the Necessary, even if they are dependent on His essence, are not effects (āthār) of Him at all; rather, their non-existence is impossible because they are necessary concomitants (lawāzim) of the essence. And even if it is granted, then affecting and being affected are only between two distinct things, and there is no distinction here, and for this there will come further clarification.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the Ash‘arī position and its answer. The objector argues: Since the Ash‘arites hold that God's attributes (like knowledge, power) are eternal entities distinct from the essence, they cannot be produced by God's choice (because choice involves temporality); they must be necessitated (ījāb) from the essence, which would lead to the philosophical view of emanation. The author answers: In Ash‘arī theology, the need for an efficient cause (mu’aththir) is due to temporal origination (ḥudūth), not possibility (imkān). Attributes, while dependent on the essence, are not 'effects' (āthār) of God; they are necessary concomitants (lawāzim) of the essence. Their impossibility of non-existence comes from being essential concomitants. Even if one were to concede that they are effects, then affecting and being affected require two distinct entities, but between God and His attributes there is no real distinction (since attributes are not separate entities in the way creatures are). The author promises further clarification later.

Key Terms
صفات الواجب
إيجاب
حدوث
إمكان
لوازم الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That eternal attributes must be necessitated effects of the divine essence.
Correction: The passage explains that attributes are not effects but necessary concomitants, and the need for a cause is based on origination, not mere possibility.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for needing a cause according to the author in this passage?
A) Possibility (imkān)
B) Temporal origination (ḥudūth)
C) Eternity (qidam)
D) Essential necessity

Key Takeaway
God's attributes are not effects needing a cause; they are necessary concomitants of His essence, and the need for a cause is based on temporal origination, not possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's attributes like power or knowledge, do you ever imagine them as separate 'things' that God could choose to have or not have? How does this lesson's insistence that they are 'necessary concomitants' change the way you relate to God—will you now see Him as inseparably perfect, and how will that affect your worship today?
?
The objection assumes that whatever is eternal must be a necessitated effect. Deep down, do you ever feel that your own eternal destiny is 'necessitated'—that your fate is already sealed by your past or circumstances? How does the Ash‘arī emphasis on choice and non-necessitation challenge that feeling? What specific action will you take today to live as if your choices truly matter to God?
?
Consider the phrase 'attributes are not effects.' Have you ever treated God's attributes as if they were just actions or tools He uses, rather than who He essentially is? How might your prayer change if you realized you are addressing the very essence that is powerful, merciful, and just without any separation? Will you commit to pausing before each prayer to remind yourself of this inseparable reality?

Reply to the Objection on Divine Attributes and Compulsion
Objection and answer about divine attributes and divine choice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response to the objection that divine attributes necessitate compulsion hinges on the distinction between origination and contingency and on the principle that cause-effect requires real distinction.

Core Idea
The attributes of God are not His effects but His necessary concomitants.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary addresses a critical objection against the Ash'ari position on divine attributes. The objector argues that if the attributes are eternal, then God's relation to them is necessary, not voluntary. Sanusi's reply introduces two key principles: the need for an effecting agent arises from temporal origination, not mere contingency, and causal interaction requires distinct entities. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the Ash'ari conception of divine agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by a two-part reply.
The objector assumes that eternal attributes imply necessity. The author rejects this.
2
Identify the two key points in the reply
Find where the author gives the first reason (the need for an effecting agent is due to origination, not contingency) and the second reason (causality requires distinct things).
These two points dismantle the objection from different angles.
3
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this defense preserves both divine unity and free choice. The attributes are not separate effects but intrinsic necessary concomitants.
This is a classic Ash'ari move against necessitarian views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : صفات الواجب عندكم موجودات قديمة، فيمتنع استناده إليه بطريق الاختيار ويتعين الإيجاب.
قلنا : علة الاحتياج إلى المؤثر عندنا الحدوث لا الإمكان، وصفات الواجب وإن كانت مفتقرة إلى ذاته لا تكون آثاراً له البتة (١) ، وإنما يمتنع عـدمهـا لكونها من لوازم الذات. ولو سلم (۲) ، فالتأثير والتأثر إنما يكونان بين المتغايرين ولا تغاير ههنا وسيجيء لهذا زيادة بيان.
If it is said: The attributes of the Necessary Being according to you are pre-eternal existents, so it is impossible for it to be attributed to Him by way of choice, and necessity is determined. We say: The cause of the need for an effecting agent according to us is origination (ḥudūth), not contingency (imkān). And the attributes of the Necessary Being, even though they are dependent on His Essence, are not effects of Him at all (al-batta). Rather, their non-existence is impossible because they are necessary concomitants of the Essence. And even if it is granted, then effect and being effected only occur between two distinct things, and there is no distinction here. And further clarification of this will come.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair concerning the divine attributes and the problem of divine choice. The objector argues that if the attributes of God are eternal existing entities, then God cannot be said to act by choice in relation to them; rather, they would be necessary outcomes. The author responds by distinguishing the basis for needing an efficient cause: according to the theologians (mutakallimūn), the need is due to temporal origination (ḥudūth), not mere contingency (imkān). The attributes, though existing, are not originated; they are necessary concomitants of the divine essence and thus not 'effects' of God in the sense of produced acts. Even if one were to concede the point, the objector's reasoning fails because cause and effect require two distinct entities, but here there is no distinction between God and His attributes—they are identical in essence. The author promises further elaboration later.

الحدوث
الإمكان

لوازم الذات
التأثير
التأثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence of attributes as distinct entities undermines divine unity and necessity.
Correction: The author clarifies that attributes are not effects but inseparable concomitants, and they are not distinct from the Essence in reality.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the cause of the need for an efficient cause?
A) Contingency
B) Origination
C) Possibility
D) Necessity

Key Takeaway
The author defends divine choice by showing that divine attributes are necessary concomitants of the Essence, not produced effects, and that causality requires real distinction, which is absent between God and His attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your own attributes—like knowledge or power—are simply 'yours' but somehow external to you? How does this lesson's insistence that God's attributes are not effects but necessary concomitants change the way you think about His closeness to His own perfection?
?
If you truly believed that everything about God is a necessary concomitant of His Essence, would you still struggle with trust in His will when life feels random? What would it mean to rest in the fact that His attributes are not forced upon Him but are Him?
?
The objection claims divine necessity contradicts free choice. But the author says necessity here is not compulsion but inherent nature. Where in your life do you confuse 'how things must be' with 'how things are because God is who He is'? Will you let this lesson shift your perspective from suspicion to surrender?

Multiplicity of Attributes Despite Denial of Difference
The author argues that denying difference does not solve the problem of multiple eternal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying difference (taghayyur) between the divine attributes and the essence does not eliminate the multiplicity of eternal attributes.

Core Idea
Multiplicity does not require difference; the attributes are many even if not distinct.

The Ash'ari theologians affirmed that God's attributes are eternal. This raised a problem: if there are multiple eternal entities, does that imply multiple gods? They attempted to solve this by denying any real difference between the attributes and the essence. However, as the author points out, this only avoids saying something eternal is 'other than' God, but it does not remove the fact that the attributes are many. The key is to understand that multiplicity and difference are not the same.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's argument structure: affirmation of attributes leads to multiplicity, attempt to escape by denying difference, then critique that this only avoids otherness, not multiplicity.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the core distinction
Highlight the distinction the author makes between 'eternal other than God' and 'multiplicity of eternal entities'. Denying difference only addresses the former.
3
Understand multiplicity vs. difference
Consider the example given: part and whole are two existents even if not two different things. This illustrates how multiplicity can exist without difference.
This is crucial for the Ash'ari doctrine of attributes.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Ask yourself: why is this distinction important for Islamic theology? How does it preserve tawhid while affirming attributes?
The Ash'aris maintain that attributes are not identical to the essence nor separate from it, a middle position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : واعلم يريد أن مشايخنا لما قالوا بوجود الصفات القديمة لزمهم القول بتعدد القدماء وبإثبات قديم غير الله تعالى، فحاولوا التقصي عن ذلك بنفي المغايرة بين الصفات وكذا بين الصفة والذات . والظاهر أن هذا إنما يدفع قدم غير الله تعالى لا تعدد القدماء وتكثرها، لأن مع الصفة، وكذا الصفات بعضها مع البعض، وإن لم تكن متغايرة لكنها متعددة متكثرة قطعاً، إذ التعدد إنما يقابل الوحدة، ولذا صرحوا بأن الصفات سبعة أو ثمانية وبأن الجزء مع الكل اثنان (۱) وشيئان وموجودان وإن لم يكونا غيرين .
He said: Know that our masters, when they affirmed the existence of the eternal attributes, were compelled to affirm the multiplicity of eternal entities and to affirm something eternal other than God, so they attempted to escape that by denying difference (taghayyur) between the attributes and likewise between the attribute and the essence. And it is apparent that this only repels the eternity of other-than-God, not the multiplicity and abundance of eternal entities, because the attribute together with the essence, and likewise attributes together with each other, even if they are not different, they are certainly multiple and abundant, since multiplicity is only the opposite of unity. For this reason they explicitly stated that the attributes are seven or eight, and that the part with the whole are two (1), two things, and two existents, even if they are not two different things.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the Ash'ari position on the attributes. The author says that when the Ash'ari scholars affirmed the existence of eternal attributes, they had to accept multiple eternal entities. To avoid affirming something eternal other than God, they denied difference (taghayyur) between the attributes and between attributes and essence. The author points out that this only avoids affirming a separate eternal being other than God, but does not avoid multiplicity of eternal entities. Multiplicity is distinct from difference; even if the attributes are not different from each other or from the essence, they are still multiple, because multiplicity is the opposite of unity. The Ash'aris themselves say the attributes are seven or eight, and they say that the part and whole are two things, two existents, even if they are not two different things. So denying difference does not solve the problem of multiplicity.

Key Terms
صفات
قديم
تعدد
مغايرة
وحدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying difference between attributes and essence solves the problem of multiple eternal entities.
Correction: Denying difference only avoids saying there is an eternal being other than God, but multiplicity remains because the attributes are many in number.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue is the consequence of denying difference between the attributes and the essence?
A) It solves the problem of multiple eternal entities.
B) It only avoids affirming something eternal other than God, but does not negate multiplicity.
C) It proves that all attributes are identical to the essence.
D) It is insufficient to establish unity.

Key Takeaway
Denying difference between attributes and essence does not resolve the problem of multiplicity; the attributes are many even if they are not different.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a mental shortcut or denial to avoid a difficult theological implication, rather than facing the full logical weight of your beliefs? How does this passage challenge you to accept the consequences of your positions, even when uncomfortable?
?
The Ash'ari masters attempted to protect tawhid by denying difference, yet the author shows this doesn't solve the problem. Is there an area in your own understanding of Allah's attributes where you might be relying on a similar conceptual 'quick fix' rather than grappling with the full reality? What would it take for you to honestly confront that tension?
?
This passage forces you to choose: either affirm divine attributes and accept multiplicity, or abandon attributes for strict simplicity. Which path is closer to your heart, and why? Are you willing to let go of a cherished theological comfort zone if the proof leads elsewhere?

Multiplicity Without Otherness in Divine Attributes
Al-Taftazani analyzes an objection and answer about multiplicity and otherness among divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari distinction between multiplicity (ta'addud) and otherness (mukhalafa) in the context of divine attributes, and why denying otherness does not entail denying multiplicity.

Core Idea
Multiplicity does not require otherness; attributes can be many without being other.

A key objection against the Ash'ari doctrine of eternal attributes is that it seems to require multiple eternal entities, implying polytheism. Al-Taftazani here presents the Ash'ari response and then critiques its limits. This passage clarifies a subtle distinction between numeric multiplicity and ontological otherness, which is essential for understanding the unity of God in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. The objector claims that positing eternal attributes forces belief in multiple eternal beings. The answer distinguishes between the subject of relation and the composite. Notice the technical terms: ma'rud al-idafa, taddud al-qudama', mukhalafa.
2
Identify the distinction made
The author argues that denying otherness (mukhalafa) between attributes and essence only repels the idea of an eternal being other than God, but does not remove multiplicity (ta'addud). Understand the difference: ta'addud means multiple in number; mukhalafa means distinct in essence.
This is the core conceptual move.
3
See the evidence from Ash'ari statements
The author cites that Ash'aris themselves affirm that attributes are seven or eight, and that part and whole are two existents, even if they are not 'others' (ghayrayn). This shows they accept multiplicity without otherness.
This grounds the argument in their own texts.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it preserves God's oneness while allowing real attributes. The lesson guards against the misconception that multiplicity in God necessarily implies polytheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجيب بأن الكلام في معروض الإضافة من حيث إنه معروض، لا في المجموع المركب من المعروض والمعارض . قال : واعلم يريد أن مشايخنا لما قالوا بوجود الصفات القديمة لزمهم القول بتعدد القدماء وبإثبات قديم غير الله تعالى، فحاولوا التقصي عن ذلك بنفي المغايرة بين الصفات وكذا بين الصفة والذات . والظاهر أن هذا إنما يدفع قدم غير الله تعالى لا تعدد القدماء وتكثرها، لأن مع الصفة، وكذا الصفات بعضها مع البعض، وإن لم تكن متغايرة لكنها متعددة متكثرة قطعاً، إذ التعدد إنما يقابل الوحدة، ولذا صرحوا بأن الصفات سبعة أو ثمانية وبأن الجزء مع الكل اثنان وشيئان وموجودان وإن لم يكونا غيرين .
It is answered that the discussion is about the subject of the relation (ma'rud al-idafa) inasmuch as it is a subject, not about the composite of subject and relation. He said: And know that he means: when our shaykhs affirmed the existence of the eternal attributes, they were obliged to affirm the multiplicity of eternal entities (ta'addud al-qudama') and to affirm an eternal other than God, so they attempted to escape from this by negating otherness (mukhalafa) between the attributes and likewise between the attribute and the essence. And it is apparent that this only repels the eternity of something other than God, not the multiplicity and abundance of eternal entities, because the attribute together with the essence, and likewise the attributes together with one another, even if they are not other (mutaghayira), are nevertheless multiple and numerous without doubt, since multiplicity is opposed to unity. Hence they explicitly stated that the attributes are seven or eight, and that the part together with the whole are two, and two things, and two existents, even if they are not two others (ghayrayn).
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer concerning the Ash'ari position on the divine attributes. The objector claims that positing eternal attributes forces one to assert multiple eternal entities (taddud al-qudama') and something eternal besides God. The answer distinguishes between the discussion being about the subject of the relation (the essence considered as a subject for attributes) versus the composite of subject and relation. The Ash'ari response is to deny otherness (mukhalafa) between the attributes and the essence, and among the attributes themselves. However, the author notes that this only repels the charge of an eternal being other than God, but does not avoid multiplicity of eternal entities, because multiplicity (ta'addud) is distinct from otherness (mukhalafa). Multiplicity is the opposite of unity, and the Ash'aris themselves affirm that the attributes are many (seven or eight) and that the part and whole are two existents, even if they are not 'others' (ghayrayn). Thus, the objection is partially resolved: there is no eternal other than God, but there is multiplicity within the divine essence.

Key Terms
تعدد القدماء
مغايرة
تعدد
وحدة
صفات
ذات
معروض الإضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if attributes are multiple, they must be distinct entities separate from God, leading to polytheism.
Correction: Multiplicity (ta'addud) does not entail otherness (mukhalafa). Attributes can be many without being ontologically separate from the essence or from each other.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the Ash'ari denial of otherness (mukhalafa) between attributes and essence succeed in repelling?
A) The multiplicity of eternal entities (taddud al-qudama')
B) The eternity of something other than God (qidam ghayr Allah)
C) The existence of seven or eight attributes
D) The composite of subject and relation

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari denial of otherness between attributes and essence repels the charge of an eternal other than God, but it does not deny multiplicity of attributes—multiplicity without otherness is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Condition of Difference and Impossibility of Two Similars Coexisting
Sanusi states the dispute over taghayyur and argues against two similar accidents coexisting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the condition of difference (taghayyur) is disputed in kalam and the proof that two similar accidents cannot coexist in one locus.

Core Idea
Two similar accidents cannot occupy the same locus because they are individuated by locus.

Sanusi begins by noting that the requirement of difference for similarity is a disputed matter among theologians. He then presents a key argument: two similar accidents cannot coexist in one locus, as they are only distinguished by their locus of inherence. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation to grasp the flow. Notice the initial statement of dispute and the subsequent argument.
The passage includes a refutation and an example.
2
Identify the disputed condition
The first sentence states that the condition of taghayyur (difference) is disputed. Ask yourself: what exactly is the condition?
This sets the stage for the rest.
3
Analyze the argument against coexistence
The passage provides a proof: differentiation of individual accidents is only via the locus, so two similars cannot share one locus. Consider the refutation (al-man') and the blackness example.
The example shows that intensity is not multiple coexisting blacknesses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشتراط التغاير مختلف فيه (قال : أما اشتراط التغاير فمختلف فيه، ويمتنع اجتماع المثلين لأنه لا يعقل تمايز أفراد نوع من الأعراض إلا بالمحل . ورد بالمنع . وليس شدة سواد الجسم باجتماع سوادين وأكثر، بل السوادات المتفاوتة بالشدة والضعف أنواع متخالفة تتعاقب على الجسم . قال الآمدي : وأما الصفات فقد اختلفت أصحابنا فيها، فمنهم من قال : ليست متماثلة ولا متخالفة لأن التماثل والاختلاف بين الشيئين يستدعي مغايرة بينهما وصفات الله تعالى غير متغايرة . وقال القاضي أبو بكر بالاختلاف نظراً إلى ما اختص به كل صفة من الصفات النفسية من غير التفات إلى وصف الغيرية وهذا ظاهر في أن القاضي لا يشترط في التخالف الغيرية، ففي التماثل أولى، وقد يتوهم من ظاهر عبارة المواقف أن التغاير شرط في التماثل والاختلاف البتة فمن يصف الصفات به يصفها بهما، ومن لا فلا. قال : ويمتنع اجتماع المثلين يعني أن المثلين إذا كانا من قبيل الأعراض يمتنع اجتماعهما في محل واحد، خلافاً للمعتزلة لنا أن العرضين إذا اشتركا في
The condition of difference is disputed. He said: As for the condition of difference, it is disputed. And it is impossible for two similars to coexist because the differentiation of individuals of a type of accidents is not conceivable except by the locus. And it was refuted by denial. And the intensity of blackness of a body is not due to the coexistence of two or more blacknesses; rather, the blacknesses that vary in intensity and weakness are different types that succeed one another on the body. Al-Amidi said: As for the attributes, our companions differed concerning them. Some said: they are neither similar nor different, because similarity and difference between two things require diversity between them, and the attributes of God Almighty are not diverse. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr said they are different, considering what each attribute is characterized by from psychological attributes, without regard to the description of otherness. This shows that al-Qadi does not require otherness for difference, so it is even more so for similarity. And it might be misunderstood from the apparent wording of al-Mawaqif that difference is an absolute condition for similarity and difference, so whoever describes the attributes with it describes them with both, and whoever does not, does not. He said: And it is impossible for two similars to coexist, meaning that if two similars are of the type of accidents, their coexistence in one locus is impossible, contrary to the Mu'tazila. For us, if two accidents share in
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition of taghayyur (difference) for similarity and whether two similars can coexist. It begins by stating that the requirement of difference between two similars is a matter of dispute. Then it presents an argument for the impossibility of two similars (specifically accidents) coexisting in one locus: the differentiation of individuals of a type of accident is only conceivable by their locus (place of inherence), so two individuals of the same accident cannot occupy the same locus simultaneously. This argument is refuted by denial (al-man')—i.e., it is not necessarily true that the only individuator is the locus. The author then gives an example: the intensity of blackness in a body is not due to multiple blacknesses coexisting; rather, varying intensities are different types that succeed one another. 

Next, the passage cites al-Amidi on the attributes of God. There is disagreement among the Ash'aris: some say God's attributes are neither similar nor different because similarity and difference require diversity (taghayyur) between the two things, but God's attributes are not diverse (since they are not existents separate from the essence). Al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani holds that they are different, considering the specific psychological attributes of each attribute, without requiring otherness in external existence. This implies that for him, difference does not require otherness, so even more so similarity may not require it. The author warns against a misunderstanding of al-Mawaqif that suggests difference is a condition for both similarity and difference absolutely. 

Finally, the passage returns to the general principle: it is impossible for two similars (if they are accidents) to coexist in one locus, contrary to the Mu'tazila. The argument for this is that if two accidents share in... (the text breaks off).

اجتماع المثلين
الأعراض
المحلة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two similar accidents can coexist in one locus.
Correction: The passage argues they cannot, because they are individuated by locus; the example of blackness shows that apparent coexistence is actually succession of different types.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason given for the impossibility of two similar accidents coexisting?
A) They would merge into one.
B) They cannot be differentiated without different loci.
C) They cause a contradiction in the substance.
D) They are mental constructs.

Key Takeaway
The condition of difference is disputed, but the impossibility of two similar accidents coexisting in one locus is argued based on individuation by locus, with an example from blackness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Are you willing to subject it to the same rigorous scrutiny as the scholars did with accident individuation? What step will you take today to investigate one such belief?

Ash'ari Disagreement on Divine Attributes
Al-Amidi quotes Ash'ari disagreement on similarity/difference of divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari disagreement on whether divine attributes are similar or different, and the role of otherness in that determination.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are neither similar nor different because they lack otherness, according to some Ash'aris.

Al-Amidi reports a disagreement among Ash'ari scholars regarding the similarity and difference of God's attributes. Some hold that the attributes cannot be described as similar or different because they are not distinct existences. Others, like al-Qadi Abu Bakr, argue that they are different based on their specific meanings. This lesson clarifies these positions.

It highlights the subtlety of the condition.

The Debate on Similarity and Difference of Divine Attributes
Reports Al-Amidi and Al-Qadi Abu Bakr's views on similarity/difference of divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate among Ash'arite scholars about whether divine attributes are similar, different, or neither, and the role of mutual distinction as a condition.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are neither similar nor different because they lack mutual distinction—or so argues one group.

In this passage, Al-Amidi reports a key theological disagreement: are God's attributes similar, different, or neither? The debate hinges on whether 'otherness' is a necessary condition for applying similarity and difference. This lesson unpacks the positions and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation to grasp the basic flow of the debate.
Notice that the passage begins with Al-Amidi's report before presenting Al-Qadi's view.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the two positions: one group says attributes are neither similar nor different; Al-Qadi says they are different. Note the reasoning for each.
The first group requires mutual distinction (mughāyara) which they say is absent among attributes.
3
Understand the condition of otherness
The passage explains that Al-Qadi does not require otherness (al-ghayriyya) for difference, and by extension not for similarity. Contrast this with the first group's requirement.
The passage also warns against misinterpreting al-Mawaqif as making distinction an absolute condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الآمدي : وأما الصفات فقد اختلفت أصحابنا فيها، فمنهم من قال : ليست متماثلة ولا متخالفة لأن التماثل والاختلاف بين الشيئين يستدعي مغايرة بينهما وصفات الله تعالى غير متغايرة. وقال القاضي أبو بكر بالاختلاف نظراً إلى ما اختص به كل صفة من الصفات النفسية من غير التفات إلى وصف الغيرية وهذا ظاهر في أن القاضي لا يشترط في التخالف الغيرية، ففي التماثل أولى. وقد يتوهم من ظاهر عبارة المواقف أن التغاير شرط في التماثل والاختلاف البتة فمن يصف الصفات به يصفها بهما، ومن لا فلا.
Al-Amidi said: As for the attributes, our companions differed concerning them. Some of them said: They are neither similar nor different because similarity and difference between two things necessitate mutual distinction, and the attributes of God Almighty are not mutually distinct. And Al-Qadi Abu Bakr held [they are] different, considering what each attribute is specified with from among the essential attributes, without regard to the description of otherness. This makes it apparent that Al-Qadi does not require otherness for difference, so for similarity it is more appropriate. And one might misunderstand from the apparent wording of al-Mawaqif that distinction is an absolute condition for similarity and difference; so whoever describes the attributes by it describes them by both, and whoever does not, does not.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage reports a theological debate about the similarity (tamāthul) and difference (takhāluf) of God's attributes. Al-Amidi notes that among Ash'arite scholars there is disagreement. One group argues that divine attributes are neither similar nor different because similarity and difference require a mutual distinction (mughāyara) between two things, but God's attributes are not distinct from each other (since they are not separate entities). Al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani, on the other hand, holds that the attributes are different, judging by what is specific to each attribute from among the essential attributes (al-ṣifāt al-nafsiyya), without considering the notion of otherness. This shows that al-Qadi does not require 'otherness' for difference; and if so, for similarity it is even more likely that he also does not require otherness. The passage then cautions that one might misinterpret the text of al-Mawaqif (by al-Iji) as making distinction an absolute condition for similarity and difference—so one's stance on whether attributes are similar or different will depend on whether one accepts that condition.

Key Terms
الصفات
التماثل
التخالف
التغاير
الصفات النفسية
الغيرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine attributes must be either similar or different, with no third possibility.
Correction: The passage shows a valid Ash'arite opinion that attributes are neither similar nor different because the conditions for those judgments (mutual distinction) are absent.

Checkpoint
Why do some Ash'arite scholars say that divine attributes are neither similar nor different?
A) Because they are multiple and distinct.
B) Because similarity and difference require mutual distinction, but God's attributes are not distinct from each other.
C) Because they are all identical.
D) Because they are accidents.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'arite debate on divine attributes shows that whether one applies similarity/difference depends on accepting mutual distinction as a necessary condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God's attributes like mercy and justice, do you naturally treat them as separate or as a single reality? How might the debate in this lesson challenge you to unify your understanding of God's nature in worship?
?
Is there an area of your faith where you, like the scholars here, struggle with categories of 'same' or 'different'? How can accepting mystery—that some truths about God are beyond human categorization—transform your humility before Him?

Two Arguments for Multiplicity from the One
Al-Taftazani enumerates two arguments for multiplicity from the One: dialectical and demonstrative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of arguments (dialectical and demonstrative) that al-Taftazani uses to support the possibility of multiplicity issuing from the One.

Core Idea
The possibility of multiplicity from the One is defended by shifting the burden of proof and by promising a demonstrative proof.

Al-Taftazani presents two distinct approaches to defend the permissibility of many things emanating from the single Necessary Existent. The first approach relies on a dialectical principle: what is not impossible is possible, so the burden of proof lies with the denier. The second approach is a promissory note for a rigorous demonstrative proof. This passage sets the stage for a deeper discussion on the relationship between the One and multiplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the author's own words and rhythm.
Notice the concise style of al-Taftazani.
2
Identify the two aspects
Find the two arguments: one called iqna'i (dialectical) and the other tahqiqi (demonstrative). Understand how they are distinguished.
3
Understand the dialectical argument
Reflect on why the intellect finding no impossibility shifts the burden of proof to the one claiming impossibility.
This is a key principle in kalam: possibility is the default.
4
Note the promissory demonstrative argument
The second argument is not fully given here; it is a promise of a later demonstration. Consider why al-Taftazani might include it.
5
Reflect on the complementary nature
Think about how these two arguments work together: one is a logical default, the other a rigorous proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جواز صدور الكثرة عن الواحد الحقيقي قال : وأما الثاني فلأن الأصل هو الإمكان ما لم يمتنع البرهان سنبين ولأن استناد الكل إلى الواجب ابتداء يعني جواز صدور الكثير عن الواحد فلوجهين أحدهما: إقناعي وهو أن العقل إذا لاحظ هذا الحكم لم تجد فيه امتناعاً لا لذاته ولا لغيره فمن ادعى الامتناع فعليه البرهان وثانيهما: تحقيقي وهو إقامة البرهان على صدور الممكنات كلها عن الواجب تعالى على ما سيأتي
The permissibility of multiplicity issuing from the true One. He said: As for the second [argument], it is because the principle is possibility (al-imkan) unless demonstration prevents it; we will show [later] that the attribution of all to the Necessary Existent is directly. That is, the permissibility of the many issuing from the One has two aspects: first, dialectical (iqna'i): the intellect, when considering this judgment, does not find any impossibility in it by itself or by another; so whoever claims impossibility must provide proof. Second, demonstrative (tahqiqi): it is to provide demonstration that all contingent things issue from the Necessary Existent, as will come.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents al-Taftazani's own arguments for the possibility that the one God can directly create multiplicity. The first argument is based on the principle that possibility is the default unless demonstration proves otherwise. He uses a dialectical (iqna'i) approach: since reason does not see any impossibility in the claim, the burden of proof lies on the one who denies it. The second argument is a promissory note for a demonstrative (tahqiqi) proof that all contingent beings issue from God, to be given later. This sets up the framework for the subsequent discussion.

Key Terms
الإمكان (al-imkan, possibility)
إقناعي (dialectical)
تحقيقي (demonstrative)
الواجب تعالى (the Necessary Existent)
صدور (issuing)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the dialectical argument is insufficient to prove the claim.
Correction: Al-Taftazani uses the dialectical argument not to prove the claim definitively, but to shift the burden of proof to the denier. The definitive proof is reserved for the demonstrative argument.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the first (dialectical) argument for multiplicity from the One?
A) The intellect finds no impossibility in it.
B) The philosophers have proven it.
C) Demonstration shows it is necessary.
D) The nature of God requires it.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani defends the permissibility of multiplicity from the One through two complementary arguments: a dialectical one based on lack of impossibility, and a demonstrative one to be detailed later.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a claim about God that seems impossible to your mind, do you immediately reject it, or do you pause and consider that your own perception might be limited? How can al-Taftazani's dialectical method expose your intellectual arrogance and call you to humble inquiry instead?
?
The principle 'possibility unless demonstration prevents' places a heavy burden on the one who denies. In your own faith journey, have you ever dismissed an Islamic teaching without seriously examining the proofs? Will you now commit to investigating such teachings before rejecting them?
?
Al-Taftazani promises a demonstrative proof later, showing that trust in the Islamic intellectual tradition is part of the process. Do you have the patience and humility to trust that deeper proofs exist even when you have not yet encountered them? How does this lesson challenge your desire for instant certainty?

First Argument for 'From One Only One Proceeds' and Its Refutation
Taftazani presents the first argument and responds to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first philosophical argument for the principle that from one only one proceeds, and how Taftazani refutes it by exposing its arbitrary assumptions and internal inconsistency.

Core Idea
Multiplicity of effects does not force real multiplicity in the cause.

Taftazani begins by presenting a common argument used to defend the principle 'from one only one proceeds'. This argument claims that if multiple effects arise from a single cause, the cause must itself be multiple in some way. After summarizing the argument, Taftazani rejects it as arbitrary and exposes its inconsistency with the very principles its proponents hold.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Taftazani uses a very compressed argumentative style.
2
Identify the argument's structure
The argument defines the cause as a particularity that necessitates the effect. It then reasons: if the effect is multiple, the cause must be multiple (either by essence or by a state). Trace this logical move.
The argument attempts to chain multiplicity of effect to multiplicity of cause.
3
Understand the objection and Taftazani's response
An objector clarifies that the multiplication is only by aspectuality, not real. Taftazani responds that this demolishes the opponents' own foundation because mental aspects do not harm real unity.
Taftazani's refutation turns the objection against the opponents.
4
Reflect on the key insight
Ask yourself: If mental aspects did imply real multiplicity, then even a single effect could not proceed from a simple cause. This shows the flaw in the argument.
This insight is the heart of Taftazani's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلة بحيث يصدر عنها المعلول. أعني خصوصية بحسبها يجب المعلول. فإن تعدد المعلول فهو متعدد وإلا فواحد وحينئذ إن كانت العلة علة لذاتها، فهو ذات العلة، وإلا فحالة تعرض لها فلزوم تعدد الجهات، إنما يكون عند صدور الكثير دون الواحد. قلنا : تحكمات لا يقتضي بها شبهة. فإن قيل : مرادهم أنه كلما تكثر المعلول تكثر الفاعل ولو بالحيثية ضرورة أن فاعليته لهذا اعتبار مغاير لفاعليته لذلك، ويلزم أنه كلما لم يكن تكثر في الفاعل، ولو بالحيثية اتحد المعلول. قلنا : كلام خال عن التحصيل هادم أساس قواعدهم المبنية على امتناع تعدد أثر البسيط. فإن تعدد الحيثيات العقلية لا يقدح في الوحدة الحقيقية، وإلا لما أمكن أن يصدر عنه الواحد أيضاً لأن مصدريته له اعتبار مغاير له بحسب العقل ضرورة.
The cause is that from which the effect proceeds. I mean a particularity in virtue of which the effect is necessary. So if the effect is multiple, then [the cause] is multiple, otherwise it is one. And then if the cause is a cause by its essence, then it is the essence of the cause, otherwise it is a state that occurs to it. So the necessity of multiple aspects only occurs when the many proceeds, not the one. We said: Arbitrary assertions that do not warrant a doubt. For if it is said: Their meaning is that whenever the effect is multiplied, the agent is multiplied even by way of aspectuality, necessarily because his agency for this is a consideration different from his agency for that, and it follows that whenever there is no multiplication in the agent, even by way of aspectuality, the effect is one. We said: Speech devoid of attainment, demolishing the foundation of their rules built on the impossibility of the simple having multiple effects. For the multiplicity of mental aspects does not harm real unity, otherwise it would not be possible for the one to produce [anything] also, because his source-ness for it is a consideration different from him mentally necessarily.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument for the principle 'from one only one proceeds' and includes an objection and response. The argument begins by defining the cause as a particularity that necessitates the effect. If the effect is multiple, the cause must be multiple; if the effect is one, the cause is one. If the cause is a cause by its essence, the cause's essence itself suffices; otherwise, a state supervenes, requiring multiple aspects, but only when many effects proceed, not when a single effect proceeds. The author (Taftazani) then dismisses this argument as sheer asserting without proof ('تحكمات'). An objector then clarifies that the argument's proponents mean: whenever the effect is multiplied, the agent is multiplied, even if only by aspectuality, because agency for this effect is conceptually distinct from agency for that effect, so without that conceptual multiplicity the effect would be one. The author replies that this objection is empty and undermines the opponents' own principles, which are based on the impossibility of a simple entity having multiple effects. He argues that multiplicity of mental considerations does not negate real unity; if it did, even a single effect could not proceed, because the source-ness for that effect is mentally distinct from the agent's essence.

Key Terms
العلة (the cause)
المعلول (the effect)
الخصوصية (particularity)
الحيثية (aspectuality)
الاعتبار (consideration)
الوحدة الحقيقية (real unity)
مصدريته (source-ness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That mental aspects necessarily imply real multiplicity in the agent.
Correction: Taftazani shows that multiplicity of mental considerations does not impair real unity; otherwise, even a single effect could not proceed from a simple cause.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument presented, what follows if the effect from a single cause is multiple?
A) The cause must be multiple in reality.
B) The cause must have multiple aspects, even if only mentally.
C) The cause must be a state, not an essence.
D) The effect must be simple.

Key Takeaway
The first argument for 'from one only one proceeds' is rejected by Taftazani as arbitrary and self-undermining.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever mentally separate God's actions—creating, providing, judging—as if they were different 'parts' of Him, even though you know He is One? How does this lesson's emphasis on mental aspects not harming real unity confront that tendency in your own worship?
?
When you read the objection that God's agency for each effect is a 'different consideration,' do you feel a subtle doubt creep in about His absolute simplicity? What would you need to change in your heart to truly rest in the fact that His unity is untouched by your analytical mind?
?
Taftazani calls the argument 'arbitrary assertions that do not warrant a doubt.' Are there areas of your faith where you hold onto comfortable theological formulations without letting them be tested by deeper reflection? Will you commit to examining one such belief this week, not to destroy it, but to see if it stands on the foundation of God's real unity?

Second Argument for 'From One Only One Proceeds' and Its Refutation
Taftazani presents the second argument and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for the principle that from one only one proceeds, and how it is refuted by distinguishing between existence and truth.

Core Idea
The contradictory of 'A proceeds' is 'A does not proceed', not 'B proceeds'.

This passage presents a nuanced theological argument about causality and divine simplicity. The second argument attempts to prove that a single source can only produce one effect, but the refutation reveals a subtle logical fallacy: conflating the contradictory of an event with the occurrence of a different event.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall structure.
Pay attention to the flow of the argument and its rebuttal.
2
Identify the two parts
Note where the argument ends and the refutation begins. The argument claims that if two different effects proceed, contradictories would be combined.
3
Understand the logical distinction
The refutation centers on the difference between the contradictory of an event (non-occurrence) and a different event. The contradictory of 'A proceeds' is 'A does not proceed', not 'B proceeds'.
This distinction is subtle but crucial.
4
Apply the insight
Consider why this distinction is important for discussions of divine causality. It shows that multiple effects do not entail contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنه إذا صدر عنه ( أ ) فلو صدر عنه (ب) وهو ليس (أ) اجتمع النقيضان بخلاف ما إذا تعددت الجهة، فإن كلا يستند إلى جهة . ورد بأن صدور (أ) لا يناقض صدور ما ليس (أ) بل عدم صدور (أ) وهو وإن صدق على صدور ما ليس (أ) لكن لا امتناع في اجتماع الشيء، وما يصدق عليه نقيضه ، إذا كان بحسب الوجود دون الصدق، وإنما الممتنع إن يصدق عليه ، أنه يصدر عنه (أ) ولا يصدر عنه (أ)
The second: That if (A) proceeds from it, then if (B) proceeds from it—and (B) is not (A)—the two contradictories would come together, unlike if the aspect is multiple, for each is then attributed to an aspect. It is refuted by saying: The proceeding of (A) does not contradict the proceeding of what is not (A); rather, it is the non-proceeding of (A) [that contradicts it], and although this is true of the proceeding of what is not (A), there is no impossibility in the combination of a thing and that which is affirmed as its contradictory, when this is according to existence rather than truth. The impossible is only that it be truly said of it that (A) proceeds from it and (A) does not proceed from it.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the principle that from one only one proceeds. The argument claims: if from a single source (A) proceeds, and then (B) proceeds, where (B) is not (A), then the two contradictories—namely the proceeding of (A) and the proceeding of what is not (A)—would be combined in the same subject, which is impossible. The only escape is if the source has multiple aspects, so each effect is attributed to a different aspect. The refutation responds that the supposed contradiction is not real: the proceeding of (A) does not contradict the proceeding of what is not (A); rather, the contradictory of the proceeding of (A) is the non-proceeding of (A). The phrase 'proceeding of what is not (A)' is true of (B), but there is no contradiction in a thing and what is affirmed as its contradictory when considered from the perspective of existence (واقع) rather than truth-value (صدق). The only truly impossible case would be if it were simultaneously true that (A) proceeds and (A) does not proceed from the same source.

Key Terms
النقيضان (the two contradictories)
الصدور (proceeding)
الجهة (aspect)
الوجود (existence)
الصدق (truth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the occurrence of a different event (B) contradicts the occurrence of event (A) from the same source.
Correction: The contradictory of 'A proceeds' is 'A does not proceed', not 'B proceeds'. Two different events can coexist without logical contradiction.

Checkpoint
What is the key point of the refutation of the second argument?
A) The two effects cannot coexist because they are opposites.
B) The contradictory of proceeding (A) is not proceeding (B) but rather the non-proceeding of (A).
C) Multiple aspects are impossible in a simple being.
D) The argument relies on a false premise about existence.

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that the contradictory of 'A proceeds' is 'A does not proceed', not the proceeding of a different effect, thus the argument fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assume that God cannot bring about two different things at once unless He has multiple 'aspects,' are you projecting your own mental limitations onto Him? In what area of your life do you currently limit God's power with human logic?
?
The argument mistakenly treats 'the proceeding of B' as if it were the contradictory of 'A proceeds'. In your own relationship with God, have you ever confused His not doing something with Him doing something different? How might this confuse your understanding of His will?
?
This refutation forces you to distinguish between logical impossibility and actual existence. In your daily submission to God, do you sometimes demand logical explanations where you should instead accept His decree? Are you willing to let God's actions be 'illogical' to you?

The First Proof: Composition or Regress
Taftazani states the first proof against multiple effects from one agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that if two effects proceed from a single simple agent, then the agent's source-ness for each effect must either be part of its essence (causing composition) or external and thus lead to an infinite regress of source-nesses.

Core Idea
A simple cause cannot have two distinct effects without losing its simplicity.

This lesson presents the first proof against the possibility of multiple effects emanating from a single simple cause. The argument proceeds by analyzing the concept of 'source-ness' and showing that it leads to either composition or regress. Read carefully to follow the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic and English translation of the proof. Notice how the argument sets up a dilemma.
2
Identify the two horns
The proof gives two possibilities: either source-ness is included in the agent (composition) or it is external (leading to regress). Mark these.
3
Understand why both are impossible
Composition contradicts simplicity; regress is impossible because it would be an infinite series. The proof concludes that multiple effects cannot proceed from one simple agent.
4
Reflect on the scope
Consider that this proof applies to any simple agent, including the Necessary Existent. This is a key argument in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على امتناع صدور الكثير عن الواحد بوجوه : الأول : أنه لو صدر عنه شيئان لكان مصدريته ،لهذا، أو مصدريته لذاك، مفهومين متغايرين فلا يكونان نفسه بل يكون أحدهما أو كلاهما داخلاً فيه، فيلزم تركيبه، هذا خلف. أو خارجاً عنه لازماً له، فيكون له صدور عنه . وينقل الكلام إلى مصدريته له ، وتسلسل المصدريات مع كونها محصورة من بين حاصرين والاعتراض عليه من وجوه . ١ - أن المصدرية أمر اعتباري لا تحقق له في الأعيان، فلا يلزم أن يكون جزءاً من الفاعل، أو عارضاً له معلولاً . ۲ - أنه إن أريد بتغاير مصدرية هذه لمصدرية ذاك تغايرهما بحسب الخارج فممنوع ، أو بحسب الذهن فلا ينافي كونهما نفس الفاعل بحسب الخارج. ٣ - أن المصدرية لو كانت متحققة في الخارج لم يكن الفاعل واحداً محضاً في شيء من الصور لأنه إذا صدر عنه شيء فقد تحققت هناك مصدرية مغايرة له منافية لوحدته الحقيقة . ٤ - أن المصدرية على تقدير تحققها، وعدم دخولها في الفاعل لا يلزم أن تكون معلولاً له لجواز أن تكون معلولاً لأمر آخر ، اللهم إلا إذا كان الفاعل الواحد هو الواجب تعالى وحينئذ لا تتم الدعوى كلية .
On the impossibility of the many proceeding from the one, through several arguments: The first: That if two things proceeded from it, then its source-ness for this, or its source-ness for that, would be two distinct concepts. So they would not be itself, but rather either one of them or both would be included in it, thus entailing its composition—this is a contradiction—or [they would be] outside it as necessary concomitants of it, and thus there would be a proceeding from it. Then the discussion is transferred to its source-ness for it and the regress of source-nesses while they are finite between two finite extremes. And the objection to it is from several aspects: 1. That source-ness is a mental consideration with no realization in extramental existence, so it does not entail being a part of the agent or an accident of it that is an effect. 2. That if by the difference of source-ness of this from source-ness of that is meant their difference in extramental existence, it is denied; or by mental [difference], it does not conflict with their being the same agent extramentally. 3. That if source-ness were realized extramentally, the agent would not be a pure one in any case, because if something proceeds from it, then a source-ness different from it would be realized, conflicting with its true unity. 4. That source-ness, assuming its realization and its non-entering into the agent, does not necessarily entail being an effect of it, because it is permissible that it be an effect of something else, unless the one agent is the Necessary Exalted, and then the claim is not universally established.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents a new proof for the impossibility of multiple effects from a single cause and then raises four objections against it. The proof argues: if two things proceed from one agent, then the agent's 'source-ness' (مصدرية) for each effect are two distinct concepts. If these concepts are identical to the agent's essence, they would be either included in the essence (causing composition) or external to it but necessary concomitants (which would then themselves require a source, leading to infinite regress). Thus the agent cannot be simple. The four objections refute this: (1) Source-ness is a mental consideration (اعتبار), not a real entity; therefore it does not imply composition or accidents. (2) The distinction between source-nesses is only mental, not extramental, so they can still refer to the same agent. (3) If source-ness were extramental, even a single effect would entail a distinct source-ness, compromising the agent's unity. (4) Even if source-ness were real and not part of the agent, it could be caused by something else; only in the case of the Necessary Existent would this regress be problematic, but then the proof fails as a universal claim.

Key Terms
المصدرية (source-ness)
التركيب (composition)
التسلسل (regress)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if one agent produces many effects, the source-ness is a real attribute that does not affect the agent's simplicity.
Correction: The proof shows that if source-ness were real, it would either make the agent composite or lead to an infinite regress. Thus, the agent's simplicity is compromised.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what are the two possible relationships of source-ness to the agent?
A) It is identical to the agent or distinct from the agent.
B) It is included in the agent (composition) or external and necessary (regress).
C) It is eternal or created.
D) It is mental or real.

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that if two effects come from one simple agent, the agent's source-ness must either cause composition or lead to infinite regress, both impossible, so multiple effects cannot proceed from one simple cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that God is absolutely simple. Yet, when you think of His mercy and His justice, do you unconsciously picture two different 'parts' of God? How does this proof expose that tendency as a form of shirk in your heart? What will you do to retrain your mind to see all His attributes as one undivided reality?
?
This proof argues that multiple effects from one simple cause lead to contradiction. Consider your own life: do you sometimes treat God's actions as if they come from different 'sources' within Him—blessings from His mercy, trials from His justice—rather than from a single, simple will? How does this realization challenge you to see every event in your life as coming from the same simple, wise God? Commit to reminding yourself of this when you face difficulty.
?
The proof forces you to either accept composition in God or face an infinite regress. But you cannot accept either. Are you content to simply 'know' this intellectually, or does this truth demand a deeper reverence in your worship? How will you actively cultivate an awe of God's oneness in your daily prayers and supplications, so that your tongue matches the purity of your creed?
