Objection 1: Source-ness as Mental Consideration
First objection: source-ness is a mere mental consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection that source-ness (المصدرية) is merely a mental consideration without extramental reality, and thus does not entail composition or accidents in the agent.

Core Idea
Source-ness is just a mental tag, not a real attribute.

The first objection challenges the proof's assumption that source-ness is a real entity. If it is only a mental consideration, then it cannot be part of the agent or an accident of it. This undermines the dilemma posed by the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection text
Read the Arabic and translation of the first objection. Note the key term 'اعتبار' (consideration).
2
Understand the claim
The objection states that source-ness has no extramental existence; it is merely a mental concept. Therefore, it cannot be a real part of the agent or a real accident.
3
See how it refutes the proof
The proof treated source-ness as something real enough to cause composition or regress. If it is only mental, those consequences do not follow.
4
Reflect on the implication
This objection allows that a single agent can have multiple effects without compromising its simplicity, because the distinctions between source-nesses are only in the mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على امتناع صدور الكثير عن الواحد بوجوه : الأول : أنه لو صدر عنه شيئان لكان مصدريته ،لهذا، أو مصدريته لذاك، مفهومين متغايرين فلا يكونان نفسه بل يكون أحدهما أو كلاهما داخلاً فيه، فيلزم تركيبه، هذا خلف. أو خارجاً عنه لازماً له، فيكون له صدور عنه . وينقل الكلام إلى مصدريته له ، وتسلسل المصدريات مع كونها محصورة من بين حاصرين والاعتراض عليه من وجوه . ١ - أن المصدرية أمر اعتباري لا تحقق له في الأعيان، فلا يلزم أن يكون جزءاً من الفاعل، أو عارضاً له معلولاً . ۲ - أنه إن أريد بتغاير مصدرية هذه لمصدرية ذاك تغايرهما بحسب الخارج فممنوع ، أو بحسب الذهن فلا ينافي كونهما نفس الفاعل بحسب الخارج. ٣ - أن المصدرية لو كانت متحققة في الخارج لم يكن الفاعل واحداً محضاً في شيء من الصور لأنه إذا صدر عنه شيء فقد تحققت هناك مصدرية مغايرة له منافية لوحدته الحقيقة . ٤ - أن المصدرية على تقدير تحققها، وعدم دخولها في الفاعل لا يلزم أن تكون معلولاً له لجواز أن تكون معلولاً لأمر آخر ، اللهم إلا إذا كان الفاعل الواحد هو الواجب تعالى وحينئذ لا تتم الدعوى كلية .
On the impossibility of the many proceeding from the one, through several arguments: The first: That if two things proceeded from it, then its source-ness for this, or its source-ness for that, would be two distinct concepts. So they would not be itself, but rather either one of them or both would be included in it, thus entailing its composition—this is a contradiction—or [they would be] outside it as necessary concomitants of it, and thus there would be a proceeding from it. Then the discussion is transferred to its source-ness for it and the regress of source-nesses while they are finite between two finite extremes. And the objection to it is from several aspects: 1. That source-ness is a mental consideration with no realization in extramental existence, so it does not entail being a part of the agent or an accident of it that is an effect. 2. That if by the difference of source-ness of this from source-ness of that is meant their difference in extramental existence, it is denied; or by mental [difference], it does not conflict with their being the same agent extramentally. 3. That if source-ness were realized extramentally, the agent would not be a pure one in any case, because if something proceeds from it, then a source-ness different from it would be realized, conflicting with its true unity. 4. That source-ness, assuming its realization and its non-entering into the agent, does not necessarily entail being an effect of it, because it is permissible that it be an effect of something else, unless the one agent is the Necessary Exalted, and then the claim is not universally established.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents a new proof for the impossibility of multiple effects from a single cause and then raises four objections against it. The proof argues: if two things proceed from one agent, then the agent's 'source-ness' (مصدرية) for each effect are two distinct concepts. If these concepts are identical to the agent's essence, they would be either included in the essence (causing composition) or external to it but necessary concomitants (which would then themselves require a source, leading to infinite regress). Thus the agent cannot be simple. The four objections refute this: (1) Source-ness is a mental consideration (اعتبار), not a real entity; therefore it does not imply composition or accidents. (2) The distinction between source-nesses is only mental, not extramental, so they can still refer to the same agent. (3) If source-ness were extramental, even a single effect would entail a distinct source-ness, compromising the agent's unity. (4) Even if source-ness were real and not part of the agent, it could be caused by something else; only in the case of the Necessary Existent would this regress be problematic, but then the proof fails as a universal claim.

Key Terms
المصدرية (source-ness)
الاعتبار (consideration)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming source-ness is a real attribute that exists in the agent.
Correction: The objection states it is only a mental consideration, not extramentally real.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, what is source-ness (المصدرية)?
A) A real attribute that exists in the agent.
B) A mental consideration with no extramental reality.
C) An effect that must proceed from the agent.
D) A necessary concomitant of the agent.

Key Takeaway
Source-ness is purely mental, so it cannot cause composition or regress in the agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you mentally attribute actions or qualities to Allah, do you often slip into thinking of them as separate real 'parts' or 'sources' within Him? How does knowing that source-ness is only a mental distinction change the way you think about Allah's oneness? What will you do to train yourself to see all attributes as pointing to a single, simple essence?
?
Reflect on your own experience: you frequently label yourself as the 'source' of different actions (e.g., 'I the writer', 'I the speaker'). Are these labels real parts of you, or mental tags? How does the lesson's insight help you avoid pride or fragmentation in self-perception? Will you commit to reminding yourself that your 'source-ness' for each action is just a mental consideration, and your true essence is one?

Objection 2: Distinction is Only Mental
Second objection: distinction of source-nesses is mental, not extramental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that the distinction between source-nesses is only mental, not extramental, so they do not conflict with the agent being one in external reality.

Core Idea
Differences in source-ness are in the mind, not in the world.

The second objection clarifies that when we say source-nesses are distinct, that distinction is only in the mind. If someone insists they are distinct in external reality, that is denied. If they are distinct only mentally, then the agent remains one extramentally. Thus the proof fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection text
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two alternatives: external vs mental distinction.
2
Analyze the alternatives
The objection gives two possibilities: if the distinction is extramental, it is denied; if mental, it does not affect the agent's unity.
3
See the refutation
The proof relied on the distinction being real to cause problems. Here it is shown that the distinction is only mental, so no real composition or regress follows.
4
Think about the implications
This objection reinforces that mental concepts do not impose real multiplicity on the agent. A single agent can be mentally associated with many effects.

Conceptual distinctions do not create real multiplicity in the agent.

Objection 3: Source-ness Would Compromise Unity Even for One Effect
Third objection: real source-ness would break unity even with one effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection that if source-ness were real, even a single effect would introduce a distinct source-ness, compromising the agent's pure unity regardless of the number of effects.

Core Idea
If source-ness were real, one effect would already break simplicity.

The third objection turns the proof on its head: if source-ness were a real thing, then even a single effect would produce a source-ness distinct from the agent, violating its unity. So the proof's assumption that source-ness is real would itself contradict the agent's simplicity, making the argument self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection text
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the conditional 'if source-ness were realized extramentally'.
2
Understand the logical point
The objection says: if source-ness is real, then for any effect, there is a source-ness different from the agent. That already destroys the agent's true unity, even with one effect.
3
Connect to the proof
The proof assumed that source-ness being real would cause problems only with two effects. But this objection shows that even with one effect, the problem arises.
4
Reflect on the implication
This objection suggests that the proof's notion of source-ness as real is incompatible with divine simplicity from the start. Therefore, the proof's conclusion is not needed; the flaw is in the premise.

?
This objection demands that you choose: either God's unity is absolute and attributes like source-ness are mental, or you risk dividing God in your heart. Which do you actually believe, and are you willing to let go of any mental picture that treats God's attributes as separate parts?

Objection 4: Source-ness Could Be Caused by Something Else
Fourth objection: source-ness need not be caused by the agent it belongs to.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection that even if source-ness were real and not part of the agent, it need not be caused by that agent; it could be caused by another, unless the agent is the Necessary Existent, which limits the proof's universality.

Core Idea
Real source-ness does not have to be caused by the same agent.

The fourth objection challenges the regress argument. Assuming source-ness is real and not part of the agent, it does not automatically follow that it must be an effect of that same agent. It could be caused by something else. Only in the case of the Necessary Existent would the regress be problematic, but then the proof is not universal. This objection limits the scope of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection text
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the conditional and the exception at the end.
2
Understand the main point
The objection says: even if source-ness is real and external to the agent, it does not have to be caused by that agent. It could be caused by another cause.
3
See the exception
The exception is when the agent is the Necessary Existent (الواجب تعالى). In that case, there is no other cause, so the regress would indeed follow. But then the proof is not general.
4
Reflect on the implication
This objection shows that the proof's regress argument only works if we assume the agent is uncaused and all effects originate from it. For other agents, the source-ness could be caused by something external.

?

This lesson shows that only for the Necessary Existent is source-ness necessarily self-caused. In your daily life, do you act as if you are the ultimate source of your achievements, or do you consciously attribute all causation to God? What practical step can you take today to remember that only God is the Necessary Causer?

First Objection: Counterexamples from Numbers, Knowledge, and Motion
Al-Taftazani records the first objection with three counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first objection uses three counterexamples to challenge the proof of application by showing it would force the finitude of things known to be infinite.

Core Idea
The proof of application is refuted by showing it would make the infinite finite.

Al-Taftazani now presents the first of two objections against the proof of application. This objection targets the premise of the proof by arguing that if the proof were sound, it would lead to absurd conclusions that contradict established truths. The objector provides three counterexamples from numbers, divine knowledge, and celestial motions, each demonstrating that the proof's logic would incorrectly imply finitude where infinitude is accepted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the objection's structure.
Note the three distinct counterexamples introduced by 'وأن تكون'.
2
Identify the three counterexamples
List the three things the objection claims the proof would force to be finite: numbers, God's knowables, and celestial motions. For each, note why finitude is considered false.
The groups that reject finitude are mentioned: consensus for numbers, mutakallimun for divine knowledge, philosophers for celestial motions.
3
Understand the method of refutation
Recognize that this objection is a 'naqd'—it shows the proof would yield absurd consequences, thus refuting its premise. Ask yourself how each example uses the idea of applying two series to show contradiction.
The objection does not disprove the proof directly but shows its implications are untenable.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this objection is important. It forces theologians to refine or defend the proof. How might a supporter of the proof respond?
This is a common strategy in kalam: testing arguments by their consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما : نقض أصل الدليل بأنه لو صح لزم أن تكون الأعداد متناهية ، لأنا نفرض جملة من الواحد إلى غير النهاية، وأخرى من الاثنين إلى غير النهاية ، ثم نطبق بينهما ، وتناهي الأعداد باطل بالاتفاق، وأن تكون معلومات الله تعالى متناهية للتطبيق بين الكامل (۷) وبين الناقص منه بواحد وتناهيها باطل عند المتكلمين وأن تكون الحركات الفلكية متناهية للتطبيق بين سلسلة من هذه الدورة وأخرى من الدورة (۱) التي قبلها ، وتناهيها باطل عند الفلاسفة (٢)
The first: refuting the premise of the proof by saying that if it were sound, then numbers would have to be finite, because we suppose a set from one to infinity and another from two to infinity, then we apply between them; and the finitude of numbers is invalid by agreement. And that God's knowables would be finite by the application between the complete and the deficient from it by one, and their finitude is invalid according to the mutakallimun. And that celestial motions would be finite by the application between a series of this cycle and another of the cycle before it, and their finitude is invalid according to the philosophers.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two objections against the proof of application. It is a refutation (naqd) of the premise of the proof. The objector argues that if the proof were sound, it would force the finitude of numbers (by comparing natural numbers and evens), God's knowables (by comparing complete and deficient sets), and celestial motions (by comparing orbits of different cycles). These conclusions are all false: numbers are infinite by consensus, divine knowables are infinite according to theology, and celestial motions are infinite according to philosophy. Thus the proof is invalid.

Key Terms
نقض
أعداد
معلومات الله
حركات فلكية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the objection is simply a rejection of the proof without argument.
Correction: The objection demonstrates that the proof's logic would lead to false conclusions, thus it is a rational refutation (naqd) of the proof's premise.

Checkpoint
What is the main strategy of the first objection?
A) It provides a direct counterexample to the conclusion of the proof.
B) It shows the proof would imply the finitude of things known to be infinite.
C) It argues the proof relies on an infinite regress.
D) It claims the proof is circular.

Key Takeaway
The first objection refutes the proof of application by demonstrating that its logic would force the finitude of numbers, divine knowledge, and celestial motions—all of which are accepted as infinite—thus the proof is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector uses logical consistency to challenge a proof. Have you ever faced a logical objection to your faith that shook you? What does this lesson teach you about the proper role of reason in defending truth?
?
God's knowledge is infinite and includes every detail of your life. If you truly believed that, what one decision today would you change because He knows it?
?
The mutakallimun did not shy away from difficult objections. Is there a question about your faith you have been avoiding? What step will you take today to confront it with the tools of knowledge?

Second Objection: Infinity Does Not Imply Finitude
Al-Taftazani records the second objection with examples of unequal infinites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second objection uses examples of unequal infinites to challenge the premise that deficiency entails finiteness.

Core Idea
One infinite can be smaller than another without being finite.

The first objection attacked the premise directly. This second objection provides concrete examples to show that deficiency does not force finiteness. The objector argues that you can have two infinite sets where one is clearly less than the other yet both are infinite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: a claim, three examples, and a logical conclusion.
2
Identify the three examples
Find the examples: doubling one vs two, God's powers vs knowledge, Saturn vs moon orbits. Each shows a smaller infinity.
The examples cover mathematical, theological, and cosmological domains.
3
Understand the logical structure
The objection states that correspondence between parts does not imply equality. Infinity can produce a mapping without finitude.
This directly refutes the premise that deficiency forces finiteness.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this objection matters for the proof of the world's temporality. It shows that the opponent's reasoning is not airtight.
It exemplifies a key kalām method: using counterexamples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما: نقض المقدمة القائلة بأن إحدى الجملتين إذا كانت أنقص (۳) من الأخرى لزم انقطاعها بأن الحاصل من تضعيف الواحد مراراً غير متناهية أقل من تضعيف الاثنين مراراً غير متناهية مع لا تناهيها اتفاقاً، ومقدورات الله تعالى أقل من معلوماته لاختصاصه) بالممكنات وشمول العلم للممتنعات أيضاً مع لا تناهي المقدورات عندنا ودروات زحل أقل من دورات القمر ضرورة مع لا تناهيها عند الفلاسفة وحاصل الاعتراض أنا نختار أنه يقع بإزاء كل جزء من الناقصة، ولا نسلم لزوم تساويهما. فإن ذلك كما يكون للتساوي . فقد يكون لعدم التناهي، وإن سمى مجرد ذلك تساوياً. فلا نسلم إستحالة ذلك فيما بين التامة والناقصة بمعنى نقصان شيء من جانبها المتناهي، وإنما يستحيل ذلك في الزائدة والناقصة بمعنى كون عدد(ه) إحداهما فوق عدد الأخرى، وهو ليس بلازم فيما بين غير المتناهيين وإن نقصت ألوف.
And the second: refuting the premise that if one of the two sets is less (deficient) than the other, then it must be finite: because the result of doubling one infinitely many times is less than doubling two infinitely many times, while both are infinite by agreement. And the powers of God are less than His knowables because [power] is specific to possibles while knowledge encompasses impossibles as well, while the powers are infinite according to us. And the orbits of Saturn are less than the orbits of the moon necessarily, while they are infinite according to the philosophers. The gist of the objection is that we choose that there occurs opposite every part of the deficient set, and we do not accept that they must be equal. For that [correspondence] can be due to lack of finitude, not necessarily equality. And if they call that mere correspondence equality, we do not accept its impossibility between the complete and deficient in the sense of a deficiency in the finite side; rather, it is impossible only in the case where one is numerically greater, which is not necessary between two infinites even if one is deficient by thousands.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection, which targets the premise that if one set is deficient compared to another, it must be finite. The objector gives examples: doubling one infinitely yields a smaller infinity than doubling two, yet both are infinite. Similarly, God's powers are less than His knowledge because power only covers possibles while knowledge covers impossibles too, yet both are infinite. Celestial orbits of Saturn are fewer than those of the moon, yet both are infinite according to philosophers. The objector concludes that one can accept that every part of the deficient set has a counterpart in the complete set without implying equality; such correspondence can occur due to infinity, not necessarily because the deficient set is finite. Hence the proof's claim of impossibility is rejected.

Key Terms
تضعيف
مقدورات
دورات
غير متناهيين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If one set is less than another, it must be finite.
Correction: Infinite sets can have different sizes (e.g., doubling one vs two, powers vs knowledge) while both remain infinite.

Checkpoint
What is the second objection's argument regarding God's powers and knowledge?
A) They are equal
B) Powers are less than knowledge yet both infinite
C) Powers are finite because they are only for possibles
D) Knowledge is finite because it includes impossibles

Key Takeaway
The objection demonstrates that infinite sets can be of different sizes without either being finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life do you subconsciously equate 'less' with 'finite' or 'deficient' when thinking about God's attributes? How can you train your mind to accept that God's power, though infinite, can be meaningfully distinguished from His knowledge without being any less perfect? Write one specific thought pattern you will change today.
?
The objection shows that mathematical logic can help us understand divine realities, yet it also warns against over-applying human reasoning. Are you ever tempted to force God into the limits of your own logic? Pause and reflect: In what area of your faith do you need to submit your reasoning to revelation, even when it seems to contradict? Commit to seek Quran and Sunnah for clarity.
?
The example of Saturn and moon orbits (both infinite, yet one less) comes from ancient cosmology which we now know is inaccurate. This reminds us that theological principles remain while scientific models change. What is one belief you hold that might be based on outdated science rather than revelation? Are you willing to re-examine it with an open heart, trusting that Allah's words are true and timeless?

Divine Attributes Are Not Under the Ten Categories
Explains why divine attributes are not in the categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine attributes, though possible (mumkin), are not classified under the ten categories of substance and accident.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are eternal, so they escape the substance-accident division.

This passage tackles a potential objection: if God's attributes are possible beings (mumkin), shouldn't they fall under the categories of substance or accident? The author explains why the division applies only to originated (ḥādith) beings, not eternal ones. You'll see how the theologians differ from the philosophers on this point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the contrast between the philosophers and the theologians.
The passage is short but packed with key terminology.
2
Identify the key claim
The author claims that the restriction to substance and accident applies to the originated, not the possible. Find that statement in the text.
3
Understand the consequence
The divine attributes are possible yet eternal, so they are not substances nor accidents. The author says 'there is no problem' with this.
This resolves the apparent inconsistency.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider why someone might think possible attributes must be accidents. The passage gives the correction: accident category only for originated things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : وأما صفات الباري فالفلاسفة لا يثبتونها، ونحن لا نجعل المنحصر في الجوهر والعرض هو الممكن بل الحادث. قال : وأما صفات الباري تعالي يعني أنها لا تقدح في الحصر وإن كانت ممكنة غير داخلة تحت شيء من المقولات العشر إجماعاً أما عند الفلاسفة فلأنهم لا يثبتونها ، وأما عندنا فلأن المنقسم الى الجوهر والعرض هو الحادث، والصفات قديمة، غاية الأمر أنه يلزمنا قديم ليس بواجب لذاته، ولا جوهر ولا عرض ولا إشكال فيه .
He said: As for the attributes of the Creator (ṣifāt al-Bārī), the philosophers do not affirm them, and we do not consider that which is restricted (al-munḥaṣir) to substance (jawhar) and accident (ʻaraḍ) to be the possible (al-mumkin) but rather the originated (al-ḥādith). He said: As for the attributes of the Creator, exalted, meaning that they do not detract from the enumeration, even if they are possible (mumkina) and not included under any of the ten categories by consensus. As for the philosophers, because they do not affirm them; and as for us, because that which is divided into substance and accident is the originated, and the attributes are eternal (qadīma). The upshot is that it entails for us an eternal (qadīm) that is not necessary in itself (wājib bi-dhātihi), and not a substance, nor an accident, and there is no problem in that.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage addresses the divine attributes (ṣifāt al-Bārī) and their relation to the ten categories. The philosophers (falāsifa) do not affirm attributes; but we (the mutakallimūn) do. The author clarifies that the division into substance (jawhar) and accident (ʻaraḍ) does not apply to all possible beings, but only to originated (ḥādith) things. The attributes are eternal (qadīma). Therefore, even though they are possible (mumkina) in themselves, they are not accidents because accidents inhere in a subject that is originated; eternal attributes do not inhere in a contingent subject. They are not substances either. Thus they are outside the categories, and this does not break the enumeration because the enumeration covers only originated beings. The conclusion is that we affirm an eternal being (God) with attributes that are not necessary in themselves (i.e., they are not the essence) but are nevertheless eternal, not substance nor accident, and this is acceptable in our theology.

Key Terms
صفات الباري
قديم
حادث
جوهر
عرض
ممكن
واجب لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Since divine attributes are possible (mumkin), they must be accidents falling under the ten categories.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the substance/accident division applies only to originated beings (ḥādith), not to eternal beings. Divine attributes are eternal, so they are not accidents, even though they are possible.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why are the attributes of God not considered accidents despite being possible?
A) Because they are necessary in themselves.
B) Because they are eternal while the accident category applies only to originated things.
C) Because they are not real.
D) Because they are substances.

Key Takeaway
The divine attributes are possible but eternal, and therefore fall outside the substance-accident classification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever mentally pictured God's attributes as resembling human qualities that come and go? How does knowing that they are eternal and outside our categories change the way you relate to Him? Will you let this truth sink into your heart today?
?
This lesson shows that even the greatest philosophers failed to affirm God's attributes. Are you willing to accept the reality of God's attributes even when your own reasoning cannot fully grasp them? What will you do when your intellect struggles with a revealed truth?
?
The text says the divine attributes are 'possible' but 'eternal'. Does this challenge your comfort zone of wanting everything to be neatly categorized? Are you ready to worship a God whose reality transcends your mental boxes, and to humble your mind before His revelation?

First Objection: Unchanging Things and Time
First objection and its answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection that unchanging things appear to be in time, and Al-Taftazani's answer distinguishing between being in time and with time, introducing dahr and sarmad.

Core Idea
Unchanging things are not in time but with time; time, dahr, and sarmad are distinct relations.

The first objection targets the definition of time as a measure of motion by pointing out that unchanging entities like bodies, rest, and even God are described as being 'yesterday' or 'today'. Al-Taftazani's response introduces a crucial distinction: these entities are not in time but with time, leading to the concepts of dahr and sarmad. This lesson walks through that objection and its answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first objection carefully. Notice that it claims unchanging things are described as being in time, which would contradict time being only a measure of change.
2
Read the answer
Read Al-Taftazani's answer. He says the unchanging is attributed to time by 'occurring with it', not 'in it'. He then distinguishes three relations: time (changeable to changeable), dahr (constant to changeable), and sarmad (constant to constant).
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this salvages the definition of time as a measure of motion. Time only applies directly to changeable things; unchanging things relate to time in a different way.
This shows the sophistication of the kalam view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقض أدلة الفلاسفة قال : ثم عروض بوجوه : الأول: أن غير المتغير كالجسم وسكونه بل الواجب وجميع المجردات يتصف بالكون في الأمس واليوم والغد والحركة من غير فرق، وبهذا يظهر أنه ليس مقداراً للوجود إذ المتغير لا ينطبق على الثابت وبالعكس . الثاني : أن الحركة بمعنى الكون في الوسط ثابت فمقداره لا يكون متغيراً و بمعنى الممتد من المبدأ إلى المنتهي وهمي فمقداره لا يكون موجوداً . الثالث: أن ثبوت العرض مع عدم محله بديهي الاستحالة بخلاف ما نسميه الزمان مع عدم حركة الفلك وبهذا يظهر أنه ليس نفس الفلك ولا حركته . وأجيب عن الأول بأن غير المتغير إنما ينسب إذا نسب إلى الزمان بالحصول معه لا فيه فنسبة المتغير إلى المغير هو الزمان ونسبة الثابت إلى المتغير هو الدهر وإلى الثابت هو السرمد. وعن الثاني بأنه كما لا يجب بل يمتنع في وجود غير القار اجتماع جزءين منه فكذا في وجود مقداره، وعن الثالث بأن مبناه على حكم الوهم والكل ضعيف.
Refutation of the philosophers' proofs. He said: Then there are objections in several aspects: First, that the unchangeable, like a body and its rest, indeed the Necessary and all immaterial beings, are described as being yesterday, today, tomorrow, and in motion without difference, and by this it appears that it is not a measure of existence, since the changeable does not apply to the constant and vice versa. Second, that motion in the sense of being in the middle is constant, so its measure would not be changeable; and in the sense of extension from beginning to end is imaginary, so its measure would not be existent. Third, that the subsistence of an accident without its subject is self-evidently impossible, unlike what we call time without the motion of the sphere, and by this it appears that it is neither the sphere itself nor its motion. And the first was answered: the unchangeable is only attributed when it is attributed to time by occurring with it, not in it; so the relation of the changeable to the changeable is time, and the relation of the constant to the changeable is dahr, and to the constant is sarmad. And the second: just as it is not necessary but impossible for two parts of a non-stationary being to coexist, so also in the existence of its measure. And the third: that it is based on the judgment of imagination, and all are weak.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents three objections to the definition of time as the measure of motion, along with their answers. First objection: Unchanging things (bodies, rest, God, immaterial beings) are described as being in time (yesterday, today), so time cannot be a measure of motion. Answer: The unchanging are not 'in' time but 'with' time; we distinguish time (for changing things), dahr (for unchanging relative to changing), and sarmad (for unchanging relative to unchanging). Second objection: Motion conceived as 'being in the middle' is constant, so its measure would be constant; motion as extension is imaginary, so its measure would be nonexistent. Answer: Just as a non-stationary being cannot have two parts coexisting, so its measure (time) cannot have coexisting parts; thus it is not constant. Third objection: An accident cannot exist without a subject, yet we conceive time without motion. Answer: This is based on imagination, not proof; all these objections are weak.

Key Terms
غير متغير
دهر
سرمد
زمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unchanging things like God are 'in time' just as changing things are.
Correction: Al-Taftazani clarifies that unchanging things are not in time but with time, and no temporal predication implies they exist within time.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, how should we understand the phrase 'yesterday' when applied to God?
A) God was in time yesterday.
B) God is with time, not in time, and the relation is dahr.
C) The phrase is metaphorical and meaningless.
D) Time does not apply to God at all.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between being 'in time' and 'with time' allows time to remain a measure of motion while still applying to unchanging entities through the categories of dahr and sarmad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the passage of time, do you experience anxiety about things changing? This lesson teaches that change is measured by time, but the Unchanging is beyond time. How does this distinction affect your trust in Allah's constancy? Can you identify one area of your life where you are overly attached to changeable things, and commit to shifting that attachment to the Eternal?
?
You live in time, but your Lord does not. Does that make Him distant or near? This lesson shows that the relation of dahr connects the constant to the changing. How can you cultivate a sense of being 'with' Allah despite the temporal flow? What practice can you start today to remind yourself that your moments are measured but your Creator is not bounded by them?
?
The distinction between zaman, dahr, and sarmad is not just intellectual. How does knowing that your relationship to Allah is sarmad (constant to constant) affect your hope for everlasting life? What in your life now reflects the sarmad relation rather than the changing zaman? What would it take for you to treat worship not as a time-bound activity but as a constant connection?

Second Objection: Motion as Constant or Imaginary
Second objection and its answer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that motion can be considered constant (as being in the middle) or imaginary (as extension), and how Al-Taftazani answers that the measure of a non-stationary being cannot have coexisting parts.

Core Idea
If motion is constant, its measure is constant; if imaginary, its measure is nonexistent — but the answer refutes both by analogy.

The second objection attacks the concept of motion itself. If we consider motion as 'being in the middle', it is a constant state, so its measure would be constant, not changing. If we consider motion as an extension from start to end, it is an imaginary construct, so its measure would be imaginary. Al-Taftazani responds by drawing an analogy: just as a non-stationary being cannot have two parts coexist, neither can its measure, time, have coexisting parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection presents two senses of motion: (1) motion as 'being in the middle' (a constant state), and (2) motion as an extended process (imaginary). In both cases, the measure (time) would not be a measure of real change.
2
Read the answer
Al-Taftazani's answer uses the nature of non-stationary existence: just as a moving object cannot have two of its parts coexisting at once, its measure (time) cannot have coexisting parts either. Thus time is not constant.
This rebuts the first sense.
3
Consider the second sense
The answer does not explicitly address the second sense (imaginary motion), but it implies that if motion is imaginary, its measure is also imaginary, which is not a problem for the definition. However, the answer seems to focus on the first sense.
The commentary notes that the second aspect might be answered differently, but the passage only gives this brief answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقض أدلة الفلاسفة قال : ثم عروض بوجوه : الأول: أن غير المتغير كالجسم وسكونه بل الواجب وجميع المجردات يتصف بالكون في الأمس واليوم والغد والحركة من غير فرق، وبهذا يظهر أنه ليس مقداراً للوجود إذ المتغير لا ينطبق على الثابت وبالعكس . الثاني : أن الحركة بمعنى الكون في الوسط ثابت فمقداره لا يكون متغيراً و بمعنى الممتد من المبدأ إلى المنتهي وهمي فمقداره لا يكون موجوداً . الثالث: أن ثبوت العرض مع عدم محله بديهي الاستحالة بخلاف ما نسميه الزمان مع عدم حركة الفلك وبهذا يظهر أنه ليس نفس الفلك ولا حركته . وأجيب عن الأول بأن غير المتغير إنما ينسب إذا نسب إلى الزمان بالحصول معه لا فيه فنسبة المتغير إلى المغير هو الزمان ونسبة الثابت إلى المتغير هو الدهر وإلى الثابت هو السرمد. وعن الثاني بأنه كما لا يجب بل يمتنع في وجود غير القار اجتماع جزءين منه فكذا في وجود مقداره، وعن الثالث بأن مبناه على حكم الوهم والكل ضعيف.
Refutation of the philosophers' proofs. He said: Then there are objections in several aspects: First, that the unchangeable, like a body and its rest, indeed the Necessary and all immaterial beings, are described as being yesterday, today, tomorrow, and in motion without difference, and by this it appears that it is not a measure of existence, since the changeable does not apply to the constant and vice versa. Second, that motion in the sense of being in the middle is constant, so its measure would not be changeable; and in the sense of extension from beginning to end is imaginary, so its measure would not be existent. Third, that the subsistence of an accident without its subject is self-evidently impossible, unlike what we call time without the motion of the sphere, and by this it appears that it is neither the sphere itself nor its motion. And the first was answered: the unchangeable is only attributed when it is attributed to time by occurring with it, not in it; so the relation of the changeable to the changeable is time, and the relation of the constant to the changeable is dahr, and to the constant is sarmad. And the second: just as it is not necessary but impossible for two parts of a non-stationary being to coexist, so also in the existence of its measure. And the third: that it is based on the judgment of imagination, and all are weak.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents three objections to the definition of time as the measure of motion, along with their answers. First objection: Unchanging things (bodies, rest, God, immaterial beings) are described as being in time (yesterday, today), so time cannot be a measure of motion. Answer: The unchanging are not 'in' time but 'with' time; we distinguish time (for changing things), dahr (for unchanging relative to changing), and sarmad (for unchanging relative to unchanging). Second objection: Motion conceived as 'being in the middle' is constant, so its measure would be constant; motion as extension is imaginary, so its measure would be nonexistent. Answer: Just as a non-stationary being cannot have two parts coexisting, so its measure (time) cannot have coexisting parts; thus it is not constant. Third objection: An accident cannot exist without a subject, yet we conceive time without motion. Answer: This is based on imagination, not proof; all these objections are weak.

Key Terms
حركة
وسط
ثابت
وهمي
مقدار
غير قار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion as 'being in the middle' implies a constant state with a constant measure.
Correction: The answer shows that the measure of a non-stationary being cannot itself be stationary or constant; time does not have coexisting parts.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Al-Taftazani use to answer the second objection?
A) Like a stationary object, time has no parts.
B) Just as a non-stationary being cannot have two parts coexist, so its measure cannot.
C) Time is like a line with coexisting points.
D) Motion and time are both imaginary.

Key Takeaway
Even if motion is considered constant in one sense, the measure of a non-stationary entity cannot have coexisting parts, preserving time's nature as a measure of change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You likely think of time as a constant, unchanging backdrop to your life. But this lesson teaches that time is the measure of motion and change. If time itself is not constant, what does that mean for your attachment to the past or your anxiety about the future? How can you live in the present moment knowing it is the only 'part' of time that actually exists?
?
The objector assumed motion could be a constant state. Reflect on your own life: what 'motions' do you treat as permanent habits or fixed routines? Are there aspects of your character that you assume are unchangeable? How does the lesson's emphasis on non-stationary existence challenge you to see your own transformation as possible and ongoing?
?
The lesson hints that one interpretation of motion is imaginary. In what areas of your life do you hold onto imaginary concepts—like an ideal self that never changes? How can you root your self-awareness in the reality of constant change rather than a static image?

Third Objection: Accident Without Subject
Third objection and its answer, concluding remark

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection that an accident cannot exist without a subject, yet we conceive time without motion, and Al-Taftazani's answer that this is based on imagination, not proof.

Core Idea
Conceiving time without motion is an imaginative judgment, not a rational proof.

The third objection argues that an accident (like time) cannot exist without a subject (motion), but we commonly conceive of time passing even if the celestial sphere stopped moving. Al-Taftazani replies that this conception is a judgment of the imagination (wahm), which is not a valid basis for a rational argument. He concludes that all these objections are weak.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection states that an accident cannot subsist without its subject. Since we conceive time without the sphere's motion, time cannot be an accident of motion. Therefore, time is not the measure of motion.
2
Read the answer
Al-Taftazani's answer is brief: the objection is based on the judgment of imagination, not reason. The imagination may conceive time without motion, but that does not prove it is possible in reality.
This dismissal is strong but leaves room for further debate.
3
Understand the final phrase
The author says 'and all are weak', meaning all three objections are weak when properly answered. This reinforces the soundness of the original definition.

Source Text

نقض أدلة الفلاسفة قال : ثم عروض بوجوه : الأول: أن غير المتغير كالجسم وسكونه بل الواجب وجميع المجردات يتصف بالكون في الأمس واليوم والغد والحركة من غير فرق، وبهذا يظهر أنه ليس مقداراً للوجود إذ المتغير لا ينطبق على الثابت وبالعكس . الثاني : أن الحركة بمعنى الكون في الوسط ثابت فمقداره لا يكون متغيراً و بمعنى الممتد من المبدأ إلى المنتهي وهمي فمقداره لا يكون موجوداً . الثالث: أن ثبوت العرض مع عدم محله بديهي الاستحالة بخلاف ما نسميه الزمان مع عدم حركة الفلك وبهذا يظهر أنه ليس نفس الفلك ولا حركته . وأجيب عن الأول بأن غير المتغير إنما ينسب إذا نسب إلى الزمان بالحصول معه لا فيه فنسبة المتغير إلى المغير هو الزمان ونسبة الثابت إلى المتغير هو الدهر وإلى الثابت هو السرمد. وعن الثاني بأنه كما لا يجب بل يمتنع في وجود غير القار اجتماع جزءين منه فكذا في وجود مقداره، وعن الثالث بأن مبناه على حكم الوهم والكل ضعيف.

Is Darkness Existent? A Kalam Dispute
Objection and refutation on the existence of darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational and scriptural arguments regarding the ontological status of darkness and how the Ash'ari response refutes the claim that darkness is existent based on the Qur'anic verse.

Core Idea
Darkness is a specific non-existence that can be 'made,' unlike pure nothingness.

This lesson examines a key theological debate: is darkness a real existent thing or merely an absence of light? The author presents an objection from those who deny darkness is a barrier to sight, then recounts the defenders' appeal to the Qur'anic verse 'He made darkness and light.' Finally, the refutation clarifies that 'making' can apply to specific non-existences, so the verse does not prove darkness is existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the argument progression.
The passage is an objection-and-answer structure.
2
Identify the two positions
Notice the initial objection: darkness is not a barrier to sight; rather, the absence of light is the issue. Then the opposing view: darkness is an existent quality preventing sight, supported by the verse.
3
Analyse the Qur'anic proof
The defenders argue that 'made' (ja'ala) implies existence, so darkness must be existent. Reflect on why this seems plausible.
4
Understand the refutation
The response distinguishes 'specific non-existence' (al-'adam al-khass) from 'pure non-existence' (al-'adam al-sirf). The Maker can 'make' specific privations like blindness, so the verse does not prove existence.
This is a crucial distinction in kalam ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحائل المانع من الإبصار بين أن يكون محيطاً بالرائي والمرئي، أو متوسطاً بينهما، وربما يمنع ذلك بأنه ليس بمانع بل إحاطة الضوء بالمرئي شرط للرؤية وهو منتف في الغار، لكنه لا يتأتى على قولهم الظلمة كيفية مانعة من الإبصار تمسك القائلون بكونها وجودية بقوله تعالى : وجعل الظلمات والنور فإن المجعول لا يكون إلا موجوداً . وأجيب بالمنع ، فإن الجاعل كما يجعل الوجود يجعل العدم الخاص، كالعمى فإنما المنافي للمجعولية هو العدم الصرف.
The barrier preventing sight is either surrounding the seer and the seen, or between them. And perhaps it is prevented by saying that it is not a barrier, rather the surrounding of light around the seen is a condition for vision, which is absent in a cave. But this does not follow from their statement that darkness is a quality preventing sight. Those who hold that it is existent cling to His saying, Exalted: 'And He made darkness and light,' for that which is made cannot be but existent. And it was answered by rejection: for the Maker, just as He makes existence, He makes specific non-existence, like blindness. Indeed, what is incompatible with being made is pure non-existence.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer concerning the nature of darkness. The author reports a dispute: some argue that darkness is not a barrier to sight; rather, the absence of light (the condition for vision) is the issue. Others, who hold darkness to be existent, appeal to the Qur'anic verse 'He made darkness and light'— arguing that 'made' implies existence, so darkness must be existent. The response refutes this: the Maker can also make specific non-existences, like blindness (which is the absence of sight), so the verse does not prove darkness is existent; only pure non-existence (absolute nothing) cannot be made.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if something is said to be 'made' by Allah, it must be an existing entity. They therefore conclude that darkness is an existent quality.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'making' can also refer to specific privations (like blindness), which are not existing things but lacks with a specific cause. Thus, the Qur'anic verse does not necessitate the existence of darkness.

Checkpoint

A) They deny the verse's authenticity.
B) They argue that 'making' can also apply to specific non-existences like blindness.
C) They say darkness is not mentioned in the verse.
D) They claim the verse refers only to light.

Key Takeaway
Darkness is a specific privation that can be attributed to divine action, not pure non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If darkness is a specific privation that God 'makes,' what spiritual darkness in your life have you been ignoring as mere absence? Will you today ask God to expose that area and hold you accountable for allowing it to persist?
?
Blindness is a specific non-existence that God makes — but what about your own blindness to your faults? Where are you refusing to see a deficiency that God has placed before you, calling it 'just the way things are' rather than a privation you must act on?
?
Only pure non-existence cannot be made by God — yet how often do you treat your own sins or shortcomings as if they were pure non-existence, too insignificant to be attributed to your own choices? Will you repent of that self-deception today?

Life is Not Conditioned by Bodily Factors
Al-Taftazani states the majority Ash'ari position and defines key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority mutakallimun view that life is not necessarily dependent on physical conditions, and why divine omnipotence allows its creation in simple substances.

Core Idea
God can create life anywhere, even in the indivisible part.

Theologians debated whether life requires a specific physical constitution. The Ash'arī majority argued that life is a direct creation of God, not bound by material preconditions. This passage from al-Taftazānī presents their position and defines key concepts like 'animal spirit' and 'bodily structure'.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the original Arabic passage and its translation to get the gist. Notice the opening assertion and the supporting argument.
2
Identify the three denied conditions
The passage lists three things that are not conditions for life: balanced temperament, bodily structure, and animal spirit. Write them down.
These are the typical materialist conditions.
3
Understand the proof
The passage gives the proof: God can definitively create life in simple substances. Reflect on what 'simple substances' means (indivisible parts).
This underscores divine omnipotence.
4
Define the key terms
The passage defines 'bodily structure' and 'animal spirit'. Compare these definitions with your own understanding. Notice the physical description of animal spirit.
5
Apply the lesson
Consider how this view impacts our understanding of life and miracles. For example, could a lifeless body be revived? This teaching affirms it is possible.
It relates to the attribute of power (qudra).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتدال المزاج ووجود البنية ليسا شرطاً لإيجاد الحياة
وذهب جمهور المتكلمين إلى أن تحقق المعنى المسمى بالحياة ليس مشروطاً باعتدال المزاج والبنية والروح الحيواني للقطع بإمكان أن يخلقها الله تعالى في البسائط، بل هي في الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، والمراد بالبنية البدن المؤلف من العناصر الأربعة، وبالروح الحيواني جسم لطيف بخاري يتكون لطافة الأخلاط تنبعث من التجويف الأيسر من القلب، ويسرى إلى البدن في عروق نابتة من القلب تسمى بالشرايين.
The moderation of temperament and the existence of bodily structure are not a condition for bringing about life. And the majority of the mutakallimun held that the realization of the meaning called 'life' is not conditioned by balanced temperament, bodily structure, and the animal spirit, due to the definitive possibility that God Most High can create it in simple substances. Rather, it is in the indivisible part. And by 'structure' is meant the body composed of the four elements, and by 'animal spirit' is meant a subtle vaporous body formed from the subtlety of the humors, emanating from the left cavity of the heart, and flowing throughout the body in veins growing from the heart called arteries.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage states the position of the majority of mutakallimun (theologians) on the nature of life (al-hayat). It begins with a heading: 'Moderation of temperament and the existence of bodily structure are not a condition for bringing about life.' The author then explains that the realization of life is not conditioned by three factors: balanced temperament (i'tidal al-mizaj), bodily structure (al-binyah), and the animal spirit (al-ruh al-hayawani). The reason given is the definitive possibility (al-qat' bi-imkan) that God can create life in simple substances (al-basa'it), i.e., in the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazzā). This is a direct affirmation of divine omnipotence and a rejection of materialist conditioning. The passage then defines the two key terms: 'bodily structure' refers to the body composed of the four elements (earth, water, air, fire), and 'animal spirit' is a subtle vaporous body that arises from the subtlety of the bodily humors, originates in the left cavity of the heart, and spreads through arteries.

Key Terms
الحياة
البنية
المزاج
الروح الحيواني
البسائط
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Life cannot exist without a balanced temperament, bodily structure, and animal spirit.
Correction: The majority of mutakallimun hold that God can create life in simple substances, so these conditions are not necessary.

Checkpoint

A) Empirical observation shows life can exist without them.
B) The definitive possibility that God can create life in simple substances.
C) The impossibility of an accident existing in multiple loci.
D) The circularity of conditioning life on structure.

Key Takeaway
Life is a direct creation of Allah, not dependent on material conditions; He can create it in any substance, even the indivisible atom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Objection from One Attribute to Multiple Effects Refuted
Refutes an analogy-based objection against multiple attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that one attribute cannot produce multiple dissimilar effects fails due to the homogeneity of the effects in the example.

Core Idea
Homogeneous effects do not prove that heterogeneous effects are possible from one attribute.

In this brief but sharp passage, Sanusi addresses a potential objection: if one attribute (like knowledge) can cover multiple objects (black and white), then why cannot one attribute produce both knowledge and power? The refutation is simple: the effects in the example are homogeneous, whereas knowledge and power are heterogeneous. This lesson walks through the objection and its decisive answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully.
The objector argues: if one attribute can produce multiple different judgments (like knowledge of black and knowledge of white), then by analogy one attribute could produce both knowledge and power, which would eliminate the need for multiple attributes.
Notice the objector uses similarity between judging diverse objects and judging diverse attribute-types.
2
Identify the answer's key point.
The author responds that the analogy is flawed because the judgments in the example are all homogeneous (they are all knowledge-judgments), whereas knowledge and power are heterogeneous. Therefore, the analogy lacks a common ground.
This is a typical kalam move: showing disanalogy.
3
Reflect on why this matters.
Understand that this defense preserves the distinction between attributes like knowledge and power, which are diverse in essence and effects, while still allowing a single attribute (knowledge) to have multiple homogeneous effects (knowing different objects).
This prevents collapsing divine attributes into one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يستدل بأنه لو جاز كون الصفة الواحدة مبدأ للأحكام المختلفة كالعالمية بالسواد، والعالمية بالبياض لجاز كونها مبدأ للعالمية ،والقادرية ويلرم استغناء (۲) الأشياء عن تعدد الصفات باستناد آثارها إلى صفة واحدة ، ويجاب بأنه تمثيل بلا جامع كيف والأحكام هاهنا متجانسة بخلاف مثل العالمية والقادرية
And it may be argued that if it were permissible for one attribute to be the principle of different judgments, like the knowledge of blackness and the knowledge of whiteness, then it would be permissible for it to be the principle of knowledge and power, and it would necessitate the independence of things from multiplicity of attributes by attributing their effects to one attribute. And it is answered that this is an analogy without a common ground, for the judgments here are homogeneous, unlike the case of knowledge and power.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents a different line of argument. The objector claims that if one knowledge can cover multiple objects (e.g., knowledge of black and knowledge of white), then by analogy one attribute could be the principle for both knowledge and power, leading to the elimination of multiple attributes. The answer is that the analogy is invalid because the judgments (effects) in the case of knowledge are homogeneous (both are knowledge judgments), whereas knowledge and power are heterogeneous. Thus, the analogy fails, and the objection does not hold.

Key Terms
الصفة الواحدة مبدأ للأحكام المختلفة
العالمية بالسواد
العالمية بالبياض
استغناء الأشياء عن تعدد الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that because one attribute (knowledge) can have multiple objects, any attribute can produce effects of a different type.
Correction: The effects in the example are homogeneous (all knowledge), so the analogy does not apply to heterogeneous effects like knowledge and power.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the analogy from knowledge covering multiple objects to one attribute covering both knowledge and power?
A) Because knowledge cannot cover multiple objects.
B) Because the judgments in the case of knowledge are homogeneous, unlike knowledge and power.
C) Because attributes cannot be principles.
D) Because the argument is circular.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because the analogy between homogeneous and heterogeneous effects is invalid; multiple attributes remain necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make analogies about God's attributes, do you carefully check that the comparison truly holds? This lesson shows how a seemingly plausible analogy can be invalid. Will you commit to examining your assumptions about God's nature before drawing conclusions?
?
Do you ever find yourself wanting to simplify God's attributes into one general power, ignoring the distinct and specific nature of each? How can you honor the uniqueness of His knowledge, power, and will in your worship and understanding?
?
The objector in this lesson tried to reduce the multiplicity of attributes to a single source. In your own life, are you tempted to reduce God's complex nature to fit your comfort zone? What specific attribute do you need to meditate on more deeply to avoid that error?

Can One Knowledge Cover Two Separable Objects?
Initial objection and response on separable objects known by one knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial objection and response regarding whether a single knowledge can encompass two objects that are separable by nature.

Core Idea
Separation is always possible by itself, but can be impossible due to an external factor.

The discussion begins with a classic objection: if two objects can be known separately, how can one knowledge cover both? The response clarifies the distinction between intrinsic possibility and impossibility by another. This lesson unpacks the first part of the dialectic, focusing on the nature of separation and the core point of dispute.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the first exchange: 'If it is said... we say...' Notice how the objector uses the permanence of possibility to argue for separation, and how the responder distinguishes impossibility by another.
Pay attention to the concept of 'impossibility by another' (imtina' bil-ghayr).
2
Understand the dialectical shift
The objector then refines the scenario to objects whose separation is conceivable in every way. The responder points out that this is exactly the point of dispute.
This shows both sides agree on the facts but dispute the interpretation.
3
Consider the analogy with attributes
Read the analogy about a single attribute producing different judgments and the response that the analogy fails due to heterogeneity.
The key distinction is between homogeneous and heterogeneous judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الإمكان للممكن دائم فيجوز الانفكاك دائماً وفيه المطلوب. قلنا ،نعم إلا أنه لا ينافي الامتناع بالغير وهو المعلومية بعلم واحد، فإن عند تعلق العلم الواحد بهما جواز الانفكاك بحالة بأن يتعلق بهما علمان. فإن قيل: نفرض الكلام في معلومين يجوز انفكاكهما في التعقل كيف ما علمنا. قلنا: إمكان معلومين بهذه الحيثية نفس المتنازع وقد يستدل بأنه لو جاز كون الصفة الواحدة مبدأ للأحكام المختلفة كالعالمية بالسواد، والعالمية بالبياض لجاز كونها مبدأ للعالمية، والقادرية ويلزم استغناء الأشياء عن تعدد الصفات باستناد آثارها إلى صفة واحدة، ويجاب بأنه تمثيل بلا جامع كيف والأحكام هاهنا متجانسة بخلاف مثل العالمية والقادرية وأما فيما لا يجوز الانفكاك كالمجاورة والمماثلة والمضادة، وغير ذلك، فيجوز أن يتعلق علم واحد بمعلومين، بل ربما يجب كما في العلم بالشيء مع العلم بالعلم به، فإن هناك معلومات غير متناهية لأن العلم بالشيء مستلزم للعلم بالعلم به ضرورة وهو للعلم للعلم للعلم به وهكذا لا إلى نهاية فلو لم يكن عدة من هذه المعلومات معلومة بعلم واحد لزم أن يكون لكل من علم شيئاً ما علوم غير متناهية، وهو ظاهر البطلان . وجوابه منع الاستلزام المذكور لجواز أن يعلم الشيء ولا يلتفت الذهن إلى العلم به . ولو سلم فلا تغاير بين العلم بالشيء، والعلم بالعلم به إلا بحسب الاعتبار فينقطع بانقطاع الاعتبار.
If it is said: Possibility for the possible is permanent, so separation is always possible, and therein is the desired. We say: Yes, but it does not contradict impossibility by another, which is being known by one knowledge, for when one knowledge is attached to them, the possibility of separation remains as a state, such that two knowledges may be attached to them. If it is said: We posit the discussion about two known things whose separation in conception is possible, however we know them. We say: The possibility of two known things in this respect is the very point of dispute. And it may be argued that if it were permissible for a single attribute to be the source of different judgments, like the knowledge of blackness and the knowledge of whiteness, then it would be permissible for it to be the source of knowledge and power, and it would necessitate the independence of things from the multiplicity of attributes by attributing their effects to a single attribute. And it is answered that this is an analogy without a commonality; how so, since the judgments here are homogeneous, unlike knowledge and power. As for what does not permit separation, like adjacency, similarity, opposition, and so on, it is permissible for one knowledge to be attached to two known things, nay, it may even be necessary, as in knowledge of a thing along with knowledge of the knowledge of it, for there are infinite known things because knowledge of a thing necessarily entails knowledge of the knowledge of it, and that entails knowledge of the knowledge of the knowledge of it, and so on ad infinitum. So if several of these known things were not known by one knowledge, then for anyone who knows something there would be infinite knowledges, which is manifestly false. And its answer is to deny the aforementioned entailment, because it is permissible for one to know a thing without the mind attending to the knowledge of it. And even if it is granted, there is no difference between knowledge of a thing and knowledge of the knowledge of it except in terms of consideration, so it ceases when the consideration ceases.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange about whether a single knowledge can encompass two objects that are separable (i.e., can be known independently). The objector argues that since possibility is permanent for the possible, separation is always possible, so a single knowledge cannot cover both. The response clarifies that the impossibility of separation is due to an external factor (the very fact of being known by one knowledge). The discussion then shifts to an argument by analogy: if a single attribute could produce different judgments (like knowing black and white), it could also produce knowledge and power, leading to independence from multiple attributes. The rejoinder is that the analogy fails because the judgments here are homogeneous (both cognitive) whereas knowledge and power are heterogeneous. However, for inseparable objects (like adjacency), one knowledge can cover both, and indeed must in the case of knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge, to avoid an infinite regress of distinct knowledges. The final rebuttal denies the necessity of the regress, allowing that one can know without attending to that knowledge, and even if granted, the difference is merely conceptual.

Key Terms
علم واحد
انفكاك
امتناع بالغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Impossibility of a single knowledge covering two objects is always due to intrinsic nature.
Correction: Impossibility can be due to an external factor (impossibility by another), not the nature of the objects themselves.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction in the responder's initial reply?
A) Possibility is not permanent
B) Impossibility can be by another
C) Separation is impossible
D) The objects are inseparable

Key Takeaway
A single knowledge can cover two separable objects because the impossibility of separation is due to an external factor, not the objects' nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What area of your life feels so contradictory or separate that you doubt God can have a single, unified knowledge of it? How does this lesson challenge that doubt, and what will you entrust to Him today?
?
This passage argues that a single knowledge can cover two separable objects because the impossibility is external, not intrinsic. When you think about God's knowledge of your past mistakes and future hopes—both seemingly separable—are you tempted to think He cannot hold them together? Why or why not? What specific act of faith does this call you to?
?
Consider the rebuttal about homogeneous vs. heterogeneous judgments. Are you ever guilty of making superficial analogies about God's attributes, assuming that because one attribute works a certain way, all must? How can you cultivate a deeper awe for the distinctness of God's knowledge and power?

The Infinite Regress Argument in Knowledge of Knowledge
Infinite regress argument and rebuttal for knowledge of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the infinite regress argument for why a single knowledge must cover multiple objects in the case of knowledge of knowledge, and the rebuttal based on mental attention.

Core Idea
Infinite regress of knowledges is avoided by one knowledge covering multiple objects, or by denying the entailment.

The discussion now turns to objects that are inseparable, such as adjacency and similarity. In such cases, one knowledge can cover both. A stronger argument is made using the case of knowledge of a thing and knowledge of that knowledge, which leads to an infinite regress. This lesson examines that argument and the proposed solutions.

Guided Path
1
Read the case of inseparable objects
Read the part beginning 'As for what does not permit separation...'. Understand why adjacency and similarity require one knowledge.
These are objects that cannot be conceived separately.
2
Follow the infinite regress argument
Read the argument: knowledge of a thing entails knowledge of that knowledge, leading to an infinite chain. If each required a separate knowledge, there would be infinite knowledges, which is absurd.
The absurdity forces the conclusion that one knowledge covers multiple known things.
3
Analyze the rebuttal
The responder denies the entailment: one can know a thing without mental attention to that knowledge. Even if granted, the difference is merely by consideration, so the chain stops.
4
Reflect on the overall lesson
Consider how this relates to the broader topic of divine attributes and the nature of knowledge.
This is a fine point of kalām dialectic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly attend to your knowledge of Allah? When you affirm that He exists and is One, do you ever pause to reflect on that knowledge itself, or do you merely pay lip service? This lesson suggests that knowledge does not require such attention, but is that an excuse for your heedlessness? Will you, today, set aside five minutes to deliberately contemplate your own knowledge of your Creator?
?
Consider a deeply held belief you have about your faith—something you are certain of. Have you ever examined whether that certainty is based on real awareness or just an assumption? The infinite regress argument warns against taking knowledge for granted. Ask yourself: Would your conviction survive if you denied yourself the assumption that you know that you know? Force yourself to articulate the grounds of your certainty without relying on an infinite chain. Write down one belief and its justification in no more than two steps.
?
The lesson shows that conceptual distinctions can stop an infinite regress. But in your life, do you use conceptual distinctions to avoid confronting uncomfortable truths? For example, do you separate 'knowing Islam is true' from 'acting upon it' as if they are only conceptually distinct, while in reality they demand unity? Will you commit to bridging the gap between your knowledge and your actions today?

Opening the Section on Divine Will
Introduces the section on divine will with two investigations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine attribute of will is introduced with two distinct investigations: its definition and its relation to aversion of the opposite.

Core Idea
Divine will has two investigative angles: definition and relation to aversion.

This lesson opens the section on the divine attribute of will. The author immediately clarifies that there are two main investigations to undertake: first, defining what will means, and second, understanding that willing something is equivalent to feeling aversion towards its opposite. This sets the stage for deeper theological discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening passage

The passage includes the page number 335 at the end.
2
Identify the two investigations
The passage lists two investigations: first the definition of will, second that willing a thing is aversion of its opposite. Mark these clearly.
3
Consider the significance
Ask yourself: why does the author present the second point separately from the definition? What does it add? This will be clarified in later commentary.
The second point is not a definition but a relational statement about will.

The author thus sets up the structure for the subsequent arguments.
