Definition of Force (Quwwa) and Its Four Types
Defines quwwa and divides it into four types based on intention and effect diversity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of force (quwwa) as an attribute causing change in another, and the four types based on intention and diversity of effects.

Core Idea
Force is the principle of change in another, but only some forces qualify as power.

The author begins by defining force (quwwa) as an attribute that is the principle of change in another thing. This definition sets the stage for a classification of forces based on whether they are accompanied by intention and whether they produce varied effects. Understanding these types is essential before we can determine what counts as power (qudra).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on force
Read the Arabic text defining quwwa and the four types. Notice the criteria: accompanied by intention or not, and whether effects are varied or uniform.
The four types are animal, celestial, vegetative, and elemental.
2
Understand the exclusion of substances
Note that specific forms and souls are excluded because they are substances, not attributes. This clarifies the scope of discussion.
3
Identify the four types
Match each type to its characteristics: animal (intention + varied), celestial (no intention? but varied), vegetative (no intention, uniform), elemental (no intention, uniform).
The text is brief on celestial, but we can infer from the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ومنها القدرة (۱).
المبحث الأول
تعريف القدرة
وبيانها في مباحث :
،
(۲)
المبحث الأول القوة وهي صفة تكون مبدأ التغير في آخر من حيث هو آخر، إما مقارنة للقصد أولا. وكل منها إما مختلفة الآثار أو لا، فالأولى القوة الحيوانية والثانية الفلكية والثالثة النباتية، والرابعة العنصرية، وليس الكلام في الصور النوعية والنفوس لأنها من قبيل الجواهر والمعتبر في كون القوة قدرة إما مقارنة القصد أو اختلاف الآثار، ولهذا قيل: صفة تؤثر وفق الإرادة، أو يكون مبدأ لأفعال مختلفة، فالمشتملة عليهما قدرة اتفاقاً كالقوة الحيوانية، والخالية عنهما ليست بقدرة اتفاقاً كالقوى العنصرية، والمشتملة على إحداهما

(۱) القدرة هي القوة على الشيء، وهي مرادفة للاستطاعة والفرق بينها وبين القوة أن القوة تضاف إلى العاقل وغير العاقل فتكون طبيعية وعقلية كما في قولنا قوة التيار وقوة الجسم وقوة الخيال على حين أن القدرة لا تضاف إلا إلى الكائنات العاقلة كما في قولنا قدرة المربي، وقدرة الحاكم وقدرة الإرادة. والقدرة في الاصطلاح صفة الإرادة. وقد نفى جهم ابن صفوان كل قدرة عن الإنسان وقال: لا قدرة له أصلاً وهذا غلو في الجبر. أما المعتزلة فيقررون وجود القدرة ويقولون : إنها صفة يتأتى منها الفعل بدلاً من الترك، والترك بدلاً من الفعل. وأما الرازي فإنه يطلق القدرة على مجرد القوة التي هي مبدأ الأفعال الحيوانية المختلفة، أو على القوة الجامعة لشرائط التأثير.
(۲) في (أ) بزيادة لفظ (هو).
He said: Among them is power (qudra) (1). The first section: Definition of power and its explanation in sections: (2) The first section: The force (quwwa) is an attribute that is the principle of change in another thing insofar as it is another, either accompanied by intention or not. And each of them either has varied effects or not. The first is animal force, the second is celestial force, the third is vegetative force, and the fourth is elemental force. And there is no discussion of specific forms and souls because they are of the category of substances. And what is considered for a force to be power is either accompaniment of intention or diversity of effects. Hence it is said: an attribute that influences according to will, or is the principle of diverse actions. That which combines both is power by agreement, like animal force. That which is devoid of both is not power by agreement, like elemental forces. And that which includes one of them... (1) Power is force over a thing, and it is synonymous with ability (istiṭāʿa). The difference between it and force is that force is attributed to both the rational and non-rational, so it is natural and rational, as in our saying: force of current, force of body, and force of imagination, whereas power is attributed only to rational beings, as in our saying: power of the educator, power of the ruler, and power of the will. And power in terminology is an attribute of the will. Jahm ibn Ṣafwān denied all power to man and said: he has no power at all, and this is extremism in predestination (jabr). As for the Muʿtazila, they affirm the existence of power and say: it is an attribute from which action proceeds instead of non-action, and non-action instead of action. As for al-Rāzī, he applies the term power to mere force that is the principle of diverse animal actions, or to force that gathers the conditions of effectiveness. (2) In (A) with an added word 'huwa'.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of power (qudra) and force (quwwa). It begins with the author saying 'Among them is power', indicating that power is one of the attributes under discussion. The first section is devoted to defining power and its explanation in various subsections. The text then defines force (quwwa) as an attribute that is the principle of change in another thing, whether accompanied by intention or not. It divides forces into four types based on whether they have varied effects or not: animal force (with intention and varied effects), celestial force (without intention but with varied effects? Actually the text says 'the second is celestial' but does not specify its characteristics—likely it has effects but no intention), vegetative force (with intention? No, vegetative has no intention, effects are uniform), and elemental force (no intention, uniform effects). The author excludes specific forms and souls because they are substances, not attributes. The key point is that a force is considered power (qudra) if it is either accompanied by intention (qasd) or produces diverse effects. Hence it is said that power is an attribute that influences according to will, or is the principle of diverse actions. The passage then notes that a force combining both intention and diverse effects is agreed upon as power (e.g., animal force), while a force lacking both is agreed not to be power (e.g., elemental forces). The text cuts off mid-sentence while discussing a force that has only one of the two criteria. The first footnote provides a lexical and terminological definition of power: it is force over a thing, synonymous with ability (istiṭāʿa). It distinguishes power from force by noting that force is attributed to both rational and non-rational beings (e.g., current, body, imagination), whereas power is only attributed to rational beings (e.g., educator, ruler, will). In terminology, power is an attribute of the will. The footnote then presents the views of three groups: Jahm ibn Ṣafwān denied all power to humans, which is extremism in jabr (predestination); the Muʿtazila affirm human power as an attribute from which both action and non-action proceed; al-Rāzī applies the term power either to mere force that is the principle of diverse animal actions or to force that meets the conditions of effectiveness. The second footnote is a textual variant noting that one manuscript adds the word 'huwa'.

Key Terms
قوة (quwwa)
قصد (qasd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that all forces are the same and can be called power.
Correction: Not all forces are powers; only those with intention or diverse effects qualify as power, as will be seen.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which force is characterized by being accompanied by intention and having diverse effects?
A) Celestial force
B) Elemental force
C) Animal force
D) Vegetative force

Key Takeaway
Force is the principle of change in another, and it is divided into four types based on the presence of intention and diversity of effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the four types of force. Which type characterizes your daily actions? Do you act with intention and purpose (animal force), or passively like elemental force? What steps can you take to ensure your actions become more intentional and meaningful as an act of worship?
?
The lesson teaches that God's power (qudra) is intentional quwwa. How does this truth change the way you understand the events in your life, especially trials? Can you trust that every change He brings about has a purpose, unlike the blind forces of nature?

Criteria for Power (Qudra) and Theological Views
States criteria for qudra and discusses schools' views on human power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two criteria that make a force qualify as power (qudra): accompaniment of intention and diversity of effects, and the positions of Jahm, the Muʿtazila, and al-Rāzī on human power.

Core Idea
A force becomes power only if it involves intention or produces diverse effects; human power is affirmed against extreme jabr.

After defining force, the author lays out the criteria for a force to be considered power: it must either be accompanied by intention (qasd) or produce diverse effects. The passage then discusses the combination of these criteria and gives examples. Additionally, the footnote clarifies the lexical distinction between qudra and quwwa, and presents the positions of Jahm, the Muʿtazila, and al-Rāzī on human power.

Guided Path
1
Learn the two criteria
Read the part starting from 'والمعتبر في كون القوة قدرة' to understand that a force is power if it has intention or diverse effects.
The text says 'إما مقارنة القصد أو اختلاف الآثار'.
2
See the classification
Note the three categories: forces with both criteria (power by agreement), forces with neither (not power by agreement), and forces with only one (disputed).
The text cuts off, but the logic is clear.
3
Understand the lexical distinction
Read the footnote: qudra is for rational beings, quwwa can be for non-rational. Also learn the terminological definition: qudra is an attribute of the will.
4
Learn the theological positions
Study the views of Jahm (denies human power, extreme jabr), Muʿtazila (affirms power as ability for both action and non-action), and al-Rāzī (defines power as force with conditions).

?

?

Are you living like a rational being with agency, or like an elemental force being pushed around? Defining Power and Distinguishing Tremor from Force
Author defines power and distinguishes involuntary from voluntary movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of power as a quality of weighting, specifying, and effecting, and how it is distinguished from involuntary movements like tremor.

Core Idea
Power is a quality that weights and specifies, not just ability.

The author begins by establishing the existence of a quality whose function is to weight, specify, and effect. This quality is power (qudra). He clarifies that the dispute over whether it is called power even without actual effect is merely verbal. Then, he draws a necessary distinction between two types of movement: tremor (involuntary) and force (voluntary), which is crucial for understanding the nature of power.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of power
Focus on the opening statement: 'we are certain of the existence of a quality whose function is weighting, specifying, and effecting.' Understand that power is not just any ability, but a specific quality with these functions.
The Arabic uses 'tarjih', 'takhsis', 'ta'thir' – key terms.
2
Examine the verbal dispute
Notice that the author says the dispute about naming power without actual effect is verbal. This means the core reality is agreed upon; the disagreement is terminological.
3
Distinguish tremor from force
Contrast the voluntary movement of force (batash) with involuntary tremor (ra'sha). Inner sense (wijdan) testifies to a quality that enables voluntary movement and is distinct from temperament and bodily soundness.
This distinction is essential for the Ash'ari proof of power.

مع ابن صفوان (١) الصعود والنزول والحاصل أنا قاطعون بوجود صفة شأنها الترجيح والتخصيص والتأثير، ولا امتناع في أن لا يؤثر بالفعل لمانع والنزاع في أنها بدون التأثير بالفعل هل تسمى قدرة لفظي، والقول بقدم قدرة الله تعالى مع حدوث المقدورات على ما هو رأينا وبثبوت القدرة الحادثة قبل الفعل على ما هو رأي المعتزلة يؤيد ما ذكرنا . الفرق الضروري بين حركتي الرعشة والبطش (٢)، وحركتي (قال) والوجدان يشهد بها وبكونها صفة غير المزاج وسلامة البنية توجد في بعض الذوات دون البعض أو على بعض الأفعال دون (البعض). تنبيه على أن طريق معرفة القدرة هو الوجدان على ما هو رأي الأشارعة فإن العاقل يجد من نفسه أن له صفة بها يتمكن من حركة البطش وتركها دون الرعشة لا العلم بتأتي الفعل من بعض الموجودين وتعذره على الغير على ما ذهب إليه بعض المعتزلة لأن الممنوع قادر عندهم مع تعذر الفعل، إلا أن يقال : الفعل يتأتى منه على تقدير ارتفاع المنع، فإن قيل : ويتأتى من العاجز بتقدير ارتفاع العجز قلنا : يتأتى من الممنوع وهو بحالة في ذاته وصفاته، وإنما التغير في أمر من خارج بخلاف العاجز فإنه يتغير من صفة إلى صفة ولا العلم بصحة الشخص وانتفاء الآفات منه على ما ذهب إليه الجبائي لأن النائم كذلك وليس بقادر ، إلا أن يقال : النوم ،آفة، ثم الوجدان كما يدل عليها يدل على أنها صفة زائدة على المزاج الذي هو وآثارها من الكيفيات المحسوسة، وليست بطريق القصد ،والاختيار، وعلى سلامة البنية، وليست من قبيل الأجرام على ما نسب إلى ضرار و هشام (٣) من أن القدرة على البطثر هي اليد السليمة، وعلى المشي هي الرجل السليمة، وهذا ما قالا القدرة بعض القادر، وإن فشر بأنها صفة في القادر فهو مذهب الجمهور.
With Ibn Safwan: ascent and descent. The result is that we are certain of the existence of a quality whose function is weighting, specifying, and effecting, and there is no impossibility in its not effecting actually due to an obstacle, and the dispute is whether without actual effect it is called power is verbal. The statement that the power of God is eternal with the temporal occurrence of what is empowered—as is our view—and the affirmation of temporal power before the act—as is the view of the Mu'tazila—supports what we have mentioned. The necessary distinction between the movements of tremor and force (batash). And (it is said) inner sense (wijdan) testifies to it and to its being a quality distinct from temperament and soundness of constitution, existing in some individuals without others or in some acts without (others). A notice: that the way of knowing power is inner sense — according to the view of the Ash'aris. For the rational person finds in himself a quality by which he is able to perform the movement of force and to leave it, not the movement of tremor. Not (he knows it by) knowledge that the act is possible from some existing beings and impossible for others—as some Mu'tazila held—because the one hindered is powerful in their view despite the impossibility of the act, unless it is said: the act is possible from him on the assumption of removal of the hindrance. If it is said: it is possible from the incapable on the assumption of removal of incapacity, we say: it is possible from the hindered while he remains in his essence and attributes, and change only occurs in an external matter, contrary to the incapable, for he changes from one attribute to another. Nor (by) knowledge of the soundness of the person and absence of defects—as al-Jubba'i held—because the sleeper is likewise and is not powerful, unless it is said: sleep is a defect. Then inner sense, as it indicates this, also indicates that it is an attribute additional to temperament, which with its effects are sensible qualities, and not by way of intention and choice, and upon soundness of constitution, and it is not of the category of bodies—as attributed to Dirar and Hisham: that power over force (batash) is the sound hand, and over walking is the sound leg. This is what they said: power is a part of the powerful. If it is interpreted as an attribute in the powerful, it is the view of the majority.
Source pages p. 350

Commentary
This passage defines power (qudra) and establishes its nature according to the Ash'ari school. It begins by asserting certainty in a quality whose function is weighting (tarjih), specifying (takhsis), and effecting (ta'thir). The author notes that the dispute over whether this quality is called power even when not actually effecting is verbal. He then points to the necessary distinction between two types of movement: tremor (ra'sha) and force (batash). The former is involuntary, the latter voluntary. Inner sense (wijdan) testifies to a quality distinct from temperament and bodily soundness that varies among individuals and acts. The author then clarifies the Ash'ari method: knowledge of power comes from inner sense, not from theoretical reasoning or external observation. He refutes the Mu'tazili view that power is known by seeing that some can perform an act while others cannot, because the Mu'tazila consider the hindered person powerful even when the act is impossible. He then addresses potential objections: if the act becomes possible when the obstacle is removed, does not the same apply to the incapable? The answer is that the hindered person's essence and attributes remain unchanged, whereas the incapable would need a change of attribute. He also refutes al-Jubba'i's view that power is known by soundness of body, using the example of a sleeper who is sound but not powerful. Finally, he rejects the corporealist view of Dirar and Hisham that power is a part of the body (e.g., the hand for force), affirming instead that power is an attribute in the powerful, which is the position of the majority of theologians.

التخصيص
التأثير
الرعشة
البطش
الوجدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power is the same as physical ability or the absence of defects.
Correction: Power is a distinct quality known through inner sense, separate from temperament and bodily soundness, as shown by the difference between voluntary and involuntary movements.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the function of the quality called power?
A) Enabling all physical movements
B) Weighting, specifying, and effecting
C) Providing soundness of constitution
D) Removing obstacles from actions

Key Takeaway
Power is a quality with the functions of weighting, specifying, and effecting, and it is distinct from involuntary bodily movements, as known through inner sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a voluntary act—like raising your hand or speaking—do you sense a quality within you that is distinct from your physical body? If that quality were absent, would you still be able to act? How does this awareness affect your gratitude to the One who gave you that power?
?
You experience both involuntary movements (like shivering from cold) and voluntary actions. In light of this lesson, where do you draw the line between what your body does on its own and what your soul directs through power? Are you living as if your actions are merely physical, or do you acknowledge a deeper, God-given attribute at work?
?
The author says the dispute over naming power without actual effect is merely verbal. In your own life, have you ever excused yourself from a good deed by claiming you lack the 'power' to do it? Reflect: Is the obstacle truly in your power, or is it in your will? What would it mean to take full responsibility for the power you do have?

Knowing Power through Inner Sense and Refutations
Author explains the Ash'ari epistemological method and refutes alternative views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Ash'aris know power through inner sense (wijdan), not through external observation or bodily soundness, and how they refute alternative views.

Core Idea
Inner sense alone reveals power as a distinct attribute, not external appearances.

Having defined power and distinguished it from involuntary movement, the author now addresses the epistemological question: how do we know that we have power? He asserts that the way of knowing power is inner sense (wijdan), a direct awareness. He then refutes three alternative views: the Mu'tazili view that power is known by observing ability in others, al-Jubba'i's view that it is known by soundness of body, and the corporealist view that power is a part of the body. Each refutation reinforces the Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ash'ari method of knowing power
Read the statement: 'طريق معرفة القدرة هو الوجدان' (the way of knowing power is inner sense). Understand that this is a direct finding, not a logical inference.
The rational person finds in himself a quality enabling voluntary force and its abandonment.
2
Examine the refutation of the Mu'tazili view
The Mu'tazila claimed power is known by seeing that some can do an act and others cannot. The author counters that they still call the hindered person powerful, so it cannot be about external possibility.
A key point: the hindered person's essence remains unchanged, unlike the incapable.
3
Consider the refutation of al-Jubba'i's view
Al-Jubba'i thought power is known by soundness of body. The author provides the counterexample of a sleeper: the sleeper is sound but not powerful because sleep is a defect.
This shows power requires more than just absence of physical defects.
4
Understand the rejection of corporealist views
Dirar and Hisham claimed power is a part of the body (e.g., the hand for force). The author states this is false; power is an attribute in the powerful, which is the majority view.
The passage ends with the clarification that if interpreted as an attribute, it is the view of the majority.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can know that they have power by observing that others cannot perform an act, or by having a sound body.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the way of knowing power according to the Ash'aris?
A) Knowledge that the act is possible from some and impossible for others
B) Knowledge of soundness of the person and absence of defects
C) Inner sense (wijdan)
D) Observing that one can move their limbs freely

Key Takeaway
Inner sense (wijdan) is the only reliable way to know power, as it reveals a distinct attribute beyond bodily soundness or external possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you measure your own capabilities by what you can actually do (like the Mu'tazila), or by an inner sense of what Allah has placed in you? When you fail to perform a task, do you immediately conclude you are incapable, or do you examine whether a hindrance (like fear, laziness, or lack of opportunity) is blocking your power—which remains intact? This lesson demands you stop using external outcomes as the measure of your inner reality. Will you begin to trust the wijdān Allah gave you, even when no result shows?
?
You may have a sound body and mind, yet feel powerless to pray, to repent, to control your anger. According to this lesson, power is an attribute beyond physical soundness—the sleeper has a sound body but is not powerful. Are you spiritually asleep? What 'sleep' (neglect, distraction, sin) has made you powerless despite being physically and mentally intact? Today, acknowledge one area where you are 'asleep' and commit to waking it through sincere reliance on Allah, who grants the attribute of power.
?
The author refutes the idea that power is just a body part. But have you reduced your spiritual strength to your physical abilities? You might think you can't be patient because you are weak, or you can't give charity because you have little. This is the same error as Dirar and Hisham. Your power to choose obedience comes from an inner attribute, not from your hand or wallet. Are you making excuses based on what you lack physically, while ignoring the qudra Allah placed in your soul? What is one action you have been avoiding because you felt 'not strong enough'—and will you attempt it now, trusting His attribute of power within you?

Definition of Qudra and the Two Views
Sanusi defines qudra and presents Bishr's alternative view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mainstream Sunni definition of qudra as an attribute in the capable, and the Mu'tazili alternative view of Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir.

Core Idea
Qudra is an attribute in the capable, not merely soundness of constitution.

The author begins by clarifying the correct definition of qudra. He contrasts the position of the majority, who consider it a real attribute subsisting in the capable, with the view of Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir and Imam al-Razi, who reduce it to bodily soundness. Understanding this difference is crucial for the subsequent objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage that defines qudra according to the majority. Notice the clarification that 'qudra' in the sense of 'maqduriyya' refers to the act being possible to do or leave.
2
Identify the two views
Find the sentence where Bishr's view is presented. Contrast it with the majority view. Ask yourself: what is the core difference?
Bishr says qudra is 'salamat al-binya' (soundness of constitution).
3
Reflect on why this matters
Consider why defining qudra as a real attribute vs. bodily soundness has implications for God's power and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السليمة، وهذا ما قالا القدرة بعض القادر، وإن فسر بأنها صفة في القادر فهو مذهب الجمهور. وما قيل : إنها بعض المقدور فإنما يصح في القدرة بمعنى المقدورية، أي كون الفعل بحيث يتمكن الفاعل منه ومن تركه. وذهب بشر بن المعتمر إلى أنها عبارة عن سلامة البنية من الآفات وإليه مال الإمام الرازي. واعترض على ما ذكره القوم من أنا نميز بالضرورة بين حركتي البطش والرعشة، وما ذاك إلا بوجود صفة غير سلامة البنية توجد لبعض الأفراد دون البعض كالقدرة على الكتابة لزيد دون عمرو، وعلى بعض الأفعال دون البعض كقدرة زيد على القراءة دون الكتابة بأن الاختيار قبل الفعل باطل عندكم ومعه ممنوع لامتناع العدم حال الوجود. وأيضاً حصول الحركة حال ما خلقها الله تعالى ضروري وقبله محال فأين الاختيار ؟ وأيضاً حصول الفعل عند استواء الدواعي محال وعند عدم الاستواء يجب الراجح ويمتنع المرجوح فلا تثبت الممكنة. والجواب أن الضروري هو التفرقة بمعنى التمكن من الفعل والترك بالنظر إلى نفس حركة البطش مع قطع النظر عن الأمور الخارجية بخلاف حركة المرتعش. وحاصله : أن الوجوب أو الامتناع بحسب أخذ الفعل مع وصف الوجود أو العدم، أو بحسب أن الله تعالى خلقه، أو لم يخلقه . أو بحسب ترجح دواعي الفعل أو الترك لا ينافي تساوي الطرفين بالنظر إلى نفس القدرة .
The healthy [view]? And this is what both said: qudra (power) is a part of the capable? And if it is interpreted as an attribute in the capable, then it is the school of the majority. And what is said: that it is part of the empowered (al-maqdur) is only valid for qudra in the sense of al-maqduriyya (the state of being an object of power), i.e., the act being such that the agent is able to perform it and to abandon it. And Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir held that it is an expression of soundness of constitution from defects, and Imam al-Razi inclined towards it. And an objection was made against what the people mentioned: that we necessarily distinguish between the motion of striking (al-batsh) and trembling (al-ri'sha), and that is only due to the existence of an attribute other than soundness of constitution, existing in some individuals and not others, like the power [qudra] for writing in Zayd but not in Amr, and for some acts but not others, like Zayd's ability to read but not write, because choice before the act is invalid according to you, and with it is prohibited due to the impossibility of non-existence at the time of existence. Also, the occurrence of motion at the moment God creates it is necessary, and before it is impossible, so where is the choice? Also, the occurrence of the act when motives are equal is impossible, and when they are not equal, the preponderant is necessary and the outweighed is impossible, so the possible (al-mumkina) is not established. And the answer: that the necessary is the distinction in the sense of ability to do and abandon with consideration of the motion of striking itself, disregarding external matters, unlike the motion of a trembler. And its summary: that necessity or impossibility with respect to taking the act with the description of existence or non-existence, or with respect to God creating it or not creating it, or with respect to the preponderance of motives for the act or abandonment, does not negate the equality of the two sides with respect to the power itself.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a definition-and-defense section on the attribute of qudra (divine power). The author presents the mainstream Sunni view (مذهب الجمهور) that qudra is an attribute subsisting in the capable (القادر), distinguishes it from the sense of 'being empowered' (المقدورية), then reports the Mu'tazili view of Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir that qudra is merely 'soundness of constitution' (سلامة البنية) from defects, a view that Imam al-Razi inclined to. The objection then raises a crucial argument: we necessarily distinguish between voluntary motion (like striking) and involuntary motion (like trembling), and this distinction cannot be explained by soundness of constitution alone; rather it requires an additional quality (صفة) that varies among individuals and acts. Three specific difficulties are then raised against the Ash'ari position if they accept this additional quality: (1) choice before the act is impossible because non-existence at the time of existence is absurd; (2) motion at the moment God creates it is necessary and before it is impossible, so choice is eliminated; (3) when motives are equal, the act is impossible, and when motives are unequal, the stronger motive makes the act necessary, thus leaving no room for contingency (الإمكان). The author's answer is that the necessary distinction is based on the ability to perform or abandon the act (التمكن من الفعل والترك) when considering the motion itself, without regard to external factors—unlike the trembling motion which lacks such ability. The final summary clarifies that necessity or impossibility relative to existence/non-existence, divine creation, or preponderance of motives does not nullify the equality of the two sides (i.e., contingency) from the perspective of the power itself. In other words, the attribute of qudra grounds a real possibility that is not negated by external determinants.

Key Terms
قدرة
مقدورية
سلامة البنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think qudra is just physical ability or soundness of body.
Correction: The text shows the majority view is that qudra is an attribute distinct from bodily soundness, as it is a real quality in the capable.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of qudra according to the majority (madhhab al-jumhur)?
A) It is soundness of constitution from defects.
B) It is an attribute in the capable (al-qadir).
C) It is the act itself being possible.
D) It is the preponderance of motives.

Key Takeaway
The correct definition of qudra is that it is an attribute subsisting in the capable, not merely bodily integrity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Qadir (able),' do you truly believe His power is unlimited, or do you unconsciously restrict it by your own understanding of capability? What aspect of your life reveals a lack of trust in His complete power, and what will you change today to rely on His qudra?
?
Bishr reduced qudra to mere physical soundness. Have you ever reduced your faith to outward rituals or intellectual assent, neglecting the inner attribute of reliance on Allah? In what specific situation today will you act as if Allah's qudra is real and active, not just a theoretical belief?

The Objection and Its Answer
Sanusi presents the objection and answers it by clarifying the nature of capability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three difficulties raised against the Ash'ari position on qudra and the author's response that distinguishes between capability and external determinants.

Core Idea
Capability (tamakkun) is independent of necessity or preponderance of motives.

After presenting the mainstream definition, the author reports a powerful objection from those who hold that qudra is merely bodily soundness. The objection raises three specific difficulties concerning choice, necessity, and motives. The author's answer clarifies that the capability to perform or abandon an act is independent of these external factors.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection part
Read the Arabic text starting from 'Wa'turada...' to the end of the objection. Notice the three 'aydan' points: choice, necessity of motion, and preponderance of motives.
2
Identify the three sub-objections
List the three difficulties: 1) choice before act is impossible, 2) motion at creation is necessary, 3) equal motives make act impossible, unequal make it necessary.
3
Read the answer
Read the answer starting from 'Wal-jawab...'. Note the key phrase 'al-tamakkun min al-fi'l wal-tark' and the final summary about equality of two sides.
The answer says the distinction is about ability to do/leave, ignoring external factors.
4
Summarize the core insight
The answer shows that necessity or impossibility caused by external conditions does not negate the real possibility (imkan) grounded in the power itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السليمة، وهذا ما قالا القدرة بعض القادر، وإن فسر بأنها صفة في القادر فهو مذهب الجمهور. وما قيل : إنها بعض المقدور فإنما يصح في القدرة بمعنى المقدورية، أي كون الفعل بحيث يتمكن الفاعل منه ومن تركه. وذهب بشر بن المعتمر إلى أنها عبارة عن سلامة البنية من الآفات وإليه مال الإمام الرازي. واعترض على ما ذكره القوم من أنا نميز بالضرورة بين حركتي البطش والرعشة، وما ذاك إلا بوجود صفة غير سلامة البنية توجد لبعض الأفراد دون البعض كالقدرة على الكتابة لزيد دون عمرو، وعلى بعض الأفعال دون البعض كقدرة زيد على القراءة دون الكتابة بأن الاختيار قبل الفعل باطل عندكم ومعه ممنوع لامتناع العدم حال الوجود. وأيضاً حصول الحركة حال ما خلقها الله تعالى ضروري وقبله محال فأين الاختيار ؟ وأيضاً حصول الفعل عند استواء الدواعي محال وعند عدم الاستواء يجب الراجح ويمتنع المرجوح فلا تثبت الممكنة. والجواب أن الضروري هو التفرقة بمعنى التمكن من الفعل والترك بالنظر إلى نفس حركة البطش مع قطع النظر عن الأمور الخارجية بخلاف حركة المرتعش. وحاصله : أن الوجوب أو الامتناع بحسب أخذ الفعل مع وصف الوجود أو العدم، أو بحسب أن الله تعالى خلقه، أو لم يخلقه . أو بحسب ترجح دواعي الفعل أو الترك لا ينافي تساوي الطرفين بالنظر إلى نفس القدرة .
The healthy [view]? And this is what both said: qudra (power) is a part of the capable? And if it is interpreted as an attribute in the capable, then it is the school of the majority. And what is said: that it is part of the empowered (al-maqdur) is only valid for qudra in the sense of al-maqduriyya (the state of being an object of power), i.e., the act being such that the agent is able to perform it and to abandon it. And Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir held that it is an expression of soundness of constitution from defects, and Imam al-Razi inclined towards it. And an objection was made against what the people mentioned: that we necessarily distinguish between the motion of striking (al-batsh) and trembling (al-ri'sha), and that is only due to the existence of an attribute other than soundness of constitution, existing in some individuals and not others, like the power [qudra] for writing in Zayd but not in Amr, and for some acts but not others, like Zayd's ability to read but not write, because choice before the act is invalid according to you, and with it is prohibited due to the impossibility of non-existence at the time of existence. Also, the occurrence of motion at the moment God creates it is necessary, and before it is impossible, so where is the choice? Also, the occurrence of the act when motives are equal is impossible, and when they are not equal, the preponderant is necessary and the outweighed is impossible, so the possible (al-mumkina) is not established. And the answer: that the necessary is the distinction in the sense of ability to do and abandon with consideration of the motion of striking itself, disregarding external matters, unlike the motion of a trembler. And its summary: that necessity or impossibility with respect to taking the act with the description of existence or non-existence, or with respect to God creating it or not creating it, or with respect to the preponderance of motives for the act or abandonment, does not negate the equality of the two sides with respect to the power itself.
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a definition-and-defense section on the attribute of qudra (divine power). The author presents the mainstream Sunni view (مذهب الجمهور) that qudra is an attribute subsisting in the capable (القادر), distinguishes it from the sense of 'being empowered' (المقدورية), then reports the Mu'tazili view of Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir that qudra is merely 'soundness of constitution' (سلامة البنية) from defects, a view that Imam al-Razi inclined to. The objection then raises a crucial argument: we necessarily distinguish between voluntary motion (like striking) and involuntary motion (like trembling), and this distinction cannot be explained by soundness of constitution alone; rather it requires an additional quality (صفة) that varies among individuals and acts. Three specific difficulties are then raised against the Ash'ari position if they accept this additional quality: (1) choice before the act is impossible because non-existence at the time of existence is absurd; (2) motion at the moment God creates it is necessary and before it is impossible, so choice is eliminated; (3) when motives are equal, the act is impossible, and when motives are unequal, the stronger motive makes the act necessary, thus leaving no room for contingency (الإمكان). The author's answer is that the necessary distinction is based on the ability to perform or abandon the act (التمكن من الفعل والترك) when considering the motion itself, without regard to external factors—unlike the trembling motion which lacks such ability. The final summary clarifies that necessity or impossibility relative to existence/non-existence, divine creation, or preponderance of motives does not nullify the equality of the two sides (i.e., contingency) from the perspective of the power itself. In other words, the attribute of qudra grounds a real possibility that is not negated by external determinants.

Misconception: Some may think that external necessity (e.g., from motives) negates the possibility of the act itself.
Correction: The answer shows that necessity from external factors does not negate the equality of the two sides (i.e., real possibility) with respect to the power itself.

Checkpoint

B) The ability to perform or abandon the act when considering the motion itself, disregarding external matters.
C) The preponderance of motives in striking motion over trembling.
D) The fact that striking motion is created by God directly while trembling is caused by illness.

Key Takeaway
Capability (al-tamakkun) is independent of external necessity or preponderance of motives.

What would it mean for you to act on that truth today?

The Ash'ari Proof that Originated Power is Concurrent with the Act
Ash'ari proof with two objections and answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that originated power must exist simultaneously with the act, not prior to it, and how they answer objections.

Core Idea
Originated power is an accident that cannot precede its act.

The Ash'ari school holds that human power to act is created at the moment of the act, not before. This is a key point of difference from the Mu'tazila. In this passage, the author presents the rational proof for this position and tackles two objections that might seem to undermine it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall argument. Notice the structure: thesis, proof, objections, refutations.
The style is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the thesis and proof
The thesis is that originated power does not exist before the act. The proof has two parts: (1) power is an accident and accidents do not endure; (2) the act before existence is impossible due to contradiction.
3
Examine the first objection and refutation
The first objection claims accidents can endure by renewal, like knowledge. The refutation concedes that power can be prior in that sense but then it still exists at the time of the act.
The key is that 'prior' here is not the same as 'existing before and then disappearing.'
4
Examine the second objection and refutation
The second objection points to eternal power as a counterexample. The refutation distinguishes between impossible existence (with condition of non-existence) and possible prior existence (in a state of non-existence).
The distinction is subtle: the act cannot exist simultaneously with its non-existence, but it can be possible before it exists.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters: it clarifies that human agency is real but created, and that power does not linger before the act. This preserves divine omnipotence while accounting for human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني : القدرة الحادثة على الفعل لا توجد قبله خلافاً للمعتزلة . لنا أنها عرض فلا يبقى إلى زمان الفعل بخلاف القديمة، ولأن الفعل قبل وجوده ليس بممكن لامتناع الوجود مع العدم واستلزام فرض وقوعه الخلف ورد الأول بعدم تسليم امتناع بقاء العرض بأن المراد القدرة السابقة المستمرة بتجدد الأمثال كالعلم والميل والتمني، ونحو ذلك مما هو قبل الفعل وفاقاً . والثاني بالنقض بالقدرة القديمة، والحل بأن الممتنع والمستلزم للخلف هو وجود الفعل بشرط عدمه لا حال عدمه بأن يفرض بدل العدم الوجود .
The second section: The originated power concerning the act does not exist before it, contrary to the Mu'tazila. For us, it is an accident, so it does not remain until the time of the act, unlike the eternal power. And because the act before its existence is not possible, due to the impossibility of existence with non-existence and the entailment of its occurrence being a counterfactual. The first [objection] was refuted by not conceding the impossibility of the remaining of the accident, meaning the prior power continuing by the renewal of like instances, like knowledge, inclination, desire, and the like, which are before the act, by agreement. And the second [objection] is by the counterexample of eternal power, and the solution is that the impossible and what entails a counterfactual is the existence of the act with the condition of its non-existence, not in the state of its non-existence, by assuming existence in place of non-existence.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari position on originated power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) being simultaneous with the act, not prior. The author begins by stating the thesis: power does not exist before the act, contrary to the Mu'tazila. The proof is twofold. First, power is an accident, and accidents do not endure for two moments; hence it cannot remain from before the act to the time of the act. Second, the act before its existence is not possible, because existence and non-existence are contradictory, and assuming its occurrence before existence would entail a counterfactual. The author then addresses two objections. The first objection claims that accidents can endure by renewal of similar instances, like knowledge and desire, which can precede the act. The answer concedes that power can be prior in this sense—by continuously renewing similar instances—but then it still exists at the time of the act. The second objection points to eternal power (al-qudra al-qadīma) as a counterexample, which is not subject to non-endurance. The answer clarifies that the impossible is an act existing with the condition of its non-existence (i.e., simultaneously being and not being), not an act existing in the state of its non-existence (i.e., prior to it). The proof stands that originated power must be concurrent with the act.

Key Terms

العرض
القدرة القديمة
الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari denial of prior power negates human agency entirely.
Correction: The passage argues for concomitant power, meaning the power to act is created exactly when the act occurs, preserving both divine creation and human acquisition.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari proof, why can originated power not exist before the act?
A) Because power is an accident and accidents do not endure for two moments.
B) Because the Mu'tazila forbid it.
C) Because eternal power requires it.
D) Because the act is impossible before existence.

Key Takeaway
Originated power must be simultaneous with the act because it is an accident that does not endure, and the act cannot be possible before existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refutation of Mu'tazila Arguments for Prior Power
The author refutes three Mu'tazila proofs for prior power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three Mu'tazila arguments for prior power and the Ash'ari responses to each, particularly the concepts of possibility (imkān) and denial of similarity between divine and human power.

Core Idea
Prior power is not needed for obligation because capacity attaches to possibility, not the act itself.

The Mu'tazila argued that power must exist before the act to avoid logical absurdities. In this passage, al-Sanusi records their three main arguments and the concise Ash'ari rebuttals. These rebuttals hinge on key distinctions in the theology of obligation and divine power.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then compare it with the literal English translation. Notice the structure: a conditional statement with three consequences, then three short refutations.
The Arabic is dense; take your time to parse each clause.
2
Identify the three Mu'tazila arguments
From the phrase 'لزم إيجاد الموجود وامتناع التكليف وقدم آثار القدرة القديمة' (it would entail bringing into existence what exists, impossibility of obligation, and pre-eternity of effects), extract each of the three claims the Mu'tazila use to prove that power must precede the act.
These are all supposed consequences of power being simultaneous with the act.
3
Examine the Ash'ari response to each argument
For each objection, note the Ash'ari answer: (1) refers to a previous discussion, (2) clarifies that obligation requires only possibility (imkān) not actual prior power, (3) denies the analogy between originated and eternal power.
Pay attention to the key term 'الإمكان' (possibility) in the second response.
4
Understand the broader significance
Reflect on how these refutations defend the Ash'ari position that acquired power (istita'a) is simultaneous with the act, while preserving the coherence of divine obligation and the uniqueness of God's power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجت المعتزلة بأنها لو لم تتعلق إلا حال الفعل لزم إيجاد الموجود وامتناع التكليف وقدم آثار القدرة القديمة . وأجيب عن الأول بما سبق . وعن الثاني : بأنا لا نشترط في المكلف به أن يكون متعلق القدرة بالفعل، بل بالإمكان كإيمان الكافر دون خلق الأجسام. وعن الثالث بمنع تماثل القدرتين .
And the Mu'tazila argued that if it [power] were not attached except at the moment of the act, it would entail bringing into existence what already exists, the impossibility of obligation (taklīf), and the pre-eternity of the effects of eternal power. And the first was answered by what preceded. And the second: we do not stipulate in the obligated (mukallaf) that the object of power be attached to the act, but rather to possibility, like the faith of the disbeliever, not the creation of bodies. And the third by denying the similarity of the two powers.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage records three arguments of the Mu'tazila for their view that originated power must precede the act, and then gives the Ash'ari refutations. First, the Mu'tazila argue that if power were attached only at the moment of the act, it would involve bringing into existence something already existent (since the act would be simultaneous with power, it would be like creating the already created). Second, it would make obligation (taklīf) impossible, because obligation requires the capacity to act before acting. Third, it would entail that the effects of eternal power become pre-eternal. The answers are concise. For the first, the author refers to what was previously said (likely the argument about endurance). For the second, the Ash'ari respond that in obligation, we do not require the power to be attached to the actual act (bi al-fiʿl) but rather to possibility (bi al-imkān), like the disbeliever's capacity to have faith, not the creation of bodies (which is impossible for him). For the third, they deny the similarity (tamāthul) between originated and eternal power, so the reasoning does not transfer.

Key Terms
التكليف
الإمكان
تماثل القدرتين
إيجاد الموجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'imkān' in the second refutation refers to logical possibility in general.
Correction: In this context, 'imkān' means the capacity to act is coincident with the act, not that the act is logically possible. The Ash'ari hold that the ability (istita'a) is simultaneous with the act itself.

Checkpoint
How do the Ash'aris answer the Mu'tazila argument that prior power is needed for obligation?
A) They deny that obligation requires power.
B) They say the obligated person needs only the possibility of the act, not the actual power before the act.
C) They claim obligation is impossible.
D) They say the act is created by God alone.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari stance on power is that it is simultaneous with the act, yet obligation remains sound because it is based on possibility (imkān), not actual prior capacity.

6 min
The Simultaneity of Power and Act
Restates the debate and gives the detailed proof with a response about renewed accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument that originated power must be simultaneous with the act, and how the response about renewed accidents addresses objections.

Core Idea
Power must be present at the moment of the act, whether by endurance or renewal.

The debate over whether power (istiṭāʿa) precedes the act or coincides with it is a classic disagreement between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila. In this passage, the author restates the dispute and presents the first proof from the Ash'ari tradition, along with a sophisticated response that shows how the position can accommodate alternative theories of accidents. Understanding this argument clarifies a key principle in Islamic theology about the relationship between divine power and created acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the argument's flow. Focus on the key terms: الاستطاعة, القدرة, تجدد الأمثال.
The passage ends mid-sentence; that's fine.
2
Identify the two opposing positions
Notice how the author frames the disagreement: Ash'aris say power is with the act, Mu'tazila say it precedes. Then note the additional dispute about whether power must remain until the act.
3
Follow the first proof
Trace the logic: power is an accident → accidents don't endure → if before the act, it would be absent at the act → act without power is impossible. Also see why the eternal power counterexample is dismissed.
4
Understand the response
Read the critique and the rejoinder: even if we accept that accidents don't endure, the effect can still have a cause at the same moment. And if necessary, power can be renewed moment by moment, like knowledge or desire.
This response shows flexibility in the Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلفوا في أن الاستطاعة أي القدرة الحادثة على الفعل تكون قبله، أو معه . فذهبت الأشاعرة وغيرهم من أهل السنة إلى أنها مع الفعل لا قبله، وأكثر المعتزلة إلى أنها قبل الفعل ثم اختلفوا في أنه هل يجب بقاؤها إلى حالة المقدور، لنا وجوه : الأول : أن القدرة عرض والعرض لا يبقى زمانين، فلو كانت قبل الفعل لانعدمت حال الفعل فيلزم وجود المقدور بدون المقدرة والمعلول بدون العلة ، وهو محال ولا يرد النقض بالقدرة القديمة لأنها ليست من قبيل الأعراض، وأجيب بعد تسليم امتناع بقاء العرض بأن المحال هو وجود المعلول بدون أن يكون له علة أصلا، واللازم هو وجوده بدون مقارنة العلة بل مع وإستحالة ذلك نفس المتنازع، ولو سلم فيجوز أن تنعدم القدرة ويحدث مثلها فيكون لها بقاء بتجدد الأمثال على الاستمرار في حال الفعل كما هو شأن العلم والميل والتمني، ونحو ذلك مما لا نزاع في جواز سبقها على
They differed concerning whether ability (al-istiṭāʿa), i.e., originated power over the act, exists before it or with it. The Ashʿarīs and others of Ahl al-Sunna held that it is with the act, not before it; most of the Muʿtazila held that it is before the act. Then they differed as to whether it must remain until the state of the object of power. For us there are proofs: the first: that power is an accident, and an accident does not remain for two moments. So if it were before the act, it would be non-existent at the moment of the act, and that would entail the existence of the object of power without the power, and the effect without the cause, which is impossible. And the counterexample of eternal power does not apply because it is not of the category of accidents. And it was answered, after conceding the impossibility of the remaining of the accident, that the impossible is the existence of an effect without having any cause at all, whereas the entailment is its existence without the concomitant cause, but rather with it, and the impossibility of that is what is disputed. And if it is conceded, then it is possible that the power perishes and a like one comes into being, so it has a continuance by the renewal of like instances continuously at the moment of the act, as is the case with knowledge, inclination, desire, and the like, concerning which there is no dispute over the permissibility of their being prior
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage is a detailed restatement and expansion of the Ash'ari proof for the simultaneity of power and act. The author begins by noting the disagreement: Ash'aris and other Sunnis hold that originated power (istiṭāʿa/qudra) is with the act, not before; most Mu'tazila hold it is before. Then he presents a first argument: power is an accident, and accidents do not endure for two moments. If power were before the act, it would cease before the act occurs, so the act would exist without power—an effect without a cause, which is impossible. The objection that eternal power (qudra qadīma) is also an accident is dismissed because eternal power is not an accident. Then a response to a possible concession: even if we admit that accidents cannot endure, the impossible is an effect with no cause at all, but here the effect (act) would still have a cause (power) at the moment, just not preceding it. The impossibility of absence of concomitance is precisely what is disputed. Finally, if this is conceded, it is possible for power to perish and an identical power to arise at the moment of the act, giving continuity through renewal of like instances (tajaddud al-amthāl), as in the cases of knowledge, inclination, desire, etc., which can be prior without controversy. This response refines the position: the crucial point is that power must be present at the moment of the act, whether by endurance or renewal.

Key Terms
الاستطاعة
تعلق القدرة
تجدد الأمثال
المقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument only works if one accepts that accidents cannot endure; otherwise it fails.
Correction: The response shows that even if accidents cannot endure, power can be renewed moment by moment, so it can still be present at the act.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument given for power being simultaneous with the act?
A) Power is eternal.
B) Power is an accident and accidents do not remain for two moments.
C) The Mu'tazila agree.
D) The act is impossible without prior power.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position that power is simultaneous with the act is defended by the argument that power is an accident that cannot persist, but even if persistence is impossible, renewal of identical powers at each moment provides continuity.

If your ability to act is not a stored resource but a gift renewed at every moment, how would your sense of self-reliance shift? In what specific situation today can you consciously acknowledge your dependence on Allah for that very ability?
?
The passage argues that power must accompany the act. When you commit a sin, do you try to excuse yourself by saying you 'couldn't help it'? Reflect honestly: at the moment of sin, did you have the power to choose otherwise, and did you use it? What does that imply about your responsibility?
?
Consider the renewal of power each moment: your past good deeds relied on power that no longer exists. How does this truth affect your attitude toward relying on past achievements? What will you seek from Allah right now to perform a good deed in this very moment?

Three Mu'tazilite Arguments for Power with the Act and Ash'ari Responses
Three Mu'tazilite arguments and Ash'ari responses on power and act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three Mu'tazilite arguments that power must precede the act and the Ash'ari rebuttals to each.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites argue that power must be before the act, but Ash'aris show that power can be simultaneous.

This passage presents three distinct arguments from the Mu'tazilites against the Ash'ari view that power (qudra) is concurrent with the act. The Ash'ari responses highlight key distinctions about the nature of bringing into existence, the conditions of obligation, and the dissimilarity between eternal and created power. Let's read the arguments and their answers carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite arguments
Identify the three impossibilities they claim follow from power being simultaneous with the act: (1) bringing into existence what exists, (2) invalidating obligation, (3) making all possibilities eternal.
Notice the logical structure of each argument.
2
Examine the first answer
The Ash'ari response concedes the definition but distinguishes: bringing into existence what already exists is impossible only if the existence comes from another cause; with this very act of bringing into existence, it is not impossible.
This answer saves the Ash'ari position from the charge of tautology.
3
Examine the second answer
The Ash'aris clarify that obligation does not require the act to be actually within power at the moment of command; it suffices that it is generally possible. They give the example of a disbeliever's faith and note that the opposite (refraining) can be within power.
This rebuts the Mu'tazilite claim that taklīf would be invalid.
4
Analyze the third answer
The Ash'ari response denies the necessary similarity between eternal and originated power. The fact that originated power is with the act does not imply that eternal power is also with the act. The response is incomplete but the point is clear.
This guards against the argument that God's power would be temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجت المعتزلة بوجوه : الأول : أن القدرة لو لم تتعلق بالفعل إلا حال وجوده وحدوثه لزم محالات : الأول : إيجاد الموجود وتحصيل الحاصل لأن هذا معنى تعلق القدرة . الثاني : بطلان التكليف لأن التكليف بالفعل إنما يكون قبل حصوله ضرورة أنه لا معنى لطلب حصول الحاصل فإذا كان الفعل قبل الوقوع غير مقدور كان جميع التكاليف الواقع تكليف ما لا يطاق، وهو باطل بالاتفاق لأن القائل بجوازه لم يقل بوقوعه فضلا عن عمومه . الثالث : كون جميع الممكنات الواقعة بقدرة الله تعالى قديمة لأن المقارن للأزلي أزلي بالضرورة. فإن قيل : المعتزلة لا يقولون بالقدرة القديمة . قلنا لا بل إنما ينازعون في كونها صفة زائدة على الذات ولو سلم فيكون إلزامياً. والجواب عن الأول بعد تسليم أن معنى تعلق القدرة الحادثة بالفعل إيجاده هو أن المستحيل إيجاد الموجود بوجود حاصل بغير هذا الإيجاد، وأما بهذا الإيجاد فلا. وعن الثاني أن من يقول بكون القدرة مع الفعل لا يشترط في المكلف به أن يكون مقدوراً بالفعل حال التكليف بل أن يكون جائز الصدور للمكلف مقدوراً له في الجملة كإيمان الكافر ، بخلاف خلق الأجسام ونحوه مما لا يصح تعلق قدرة العبد به أصلاً، وقريب من هذا ما يقال إن معنى كون المكلف به مشروطاً بالقدرة أن يكون هو أو ضده متعلق القدرة، وهاهنا قد تعلقت القدرة بترك الإيمان. وعن الثالث بمنع الملازمة، وإنما يتم لو كانت القدرة القديمة والحادثة متماثلتين ليلزم من كون الثانية مع الفعل لا قبله كون الأولى كذلك. وقد يجاب بأن الكلام إنما هو في تعلق القدرة، والأزلي إنما هو نفس
And the Mu'tazilites argued with several aspects: The first: that if power were not attached to the act except at the time of its existence and coming-to-be, then impossibilities would follow: first, bringing into existence what already exists and obtaining what is already obtained, because this is the meaning of the attachment of power. Second, the invalidity of obligation because obligation to an act is only before its occurrence, necessarily, since there is no meaning to requesting what is already obtained; so if the act before its occurrence is not within power, then all the obligations that have occurred are obligating what is unbearable, which is invalid by agreement, because the one who permits it does not say it occurs, let alone its universality. Third, all possibilities that occur by the power of God the Exalted would be eternal, because that which is simultaneous with the eternal is necessarily eternal. If it is said: the Mu'tazilites do not affirm eternal power. We say: No, rather they only dispute over it being an attribute superadded to the essence, and if it is conceded, then it is obligatory [to accept the consequence]. The answer to the first, after conceding that the meaning of the attachment of originated power to the act is its bringing into existence, is that it is impossible to bring into existence what exists with an existence obtained by other than this bringing into existence; but with this bringing into existence, it is not [impossible]. And to the second, that the one who says that power is with the act does not require that the obligated act be actually within power at the time of obligation, but rather that it be possible of occurrence for the obligated one and within power in a general sense, like the faith of the disbeliever, unlike the creation of bodies and similar things that cannot be within the power of the servant at all. And close to this is what is said: the meaning of the obligated act being conditional upon power is that it or its opposite is that to which power is attached, and here power has been attached to the abandonment of faith. And to the third, by denying the concomitance; it would only hold if eternal and originated power were similar, so that from the second being with the act and not before it, the first would also be so. And it may be answered that the discourse is only about the attachment of power, and the eternal is itself...
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage presents three Mu'tazilite arguments that power must precede the act, followed by Ash'ari responses. The first argument: if power only attaches to the act at its existence, this would entail bringing into existence what already exists. The answer concedes the meaning but distinguishes: it is impossible to bring into existence what exists from another cause, but with this very act of bringing into existence it is not. The second argument: obligation would be invalid because the act is not yet within power. The answer: the Ash'ari view does not require the act to be actually within power at the time of obligation; it suffices that it is generally possible from the servant, like the faith of a disbeliever—it is not actually within his power now but he can choose it. Also, the condition of power can be satisfied by either the act or its opposite being within power; here, refraining from faith is within power. The third argument: if power is with the act, then all things done by God's power would be eternal because what accompanies the eternal is eternal. The answer denies the concomitant similarity between eternal and originated power; the requirement that power come before the act for the eternal does not apply to originated power. The response ends mid-sentence, but the gist is that the discussion is about the attachment of power, not the nature of the eternal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilites argue that if power is with the act, then obligation (taklīf) would be invalid because the act is not within the servant's power at the time of command.
Correction: The Ash'aris respond that it is enough that the act is generally possible for the servant; it does not need to be actually within his power at the moment of obligation.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazilite claim that power with the act would make all things done by God's power eternal?
A) They deny that God has power
B) They assert that God's power is eternal but originated power is not
C) They deny the concomitance because eternal and originated power are not similar
D) They accept the conclusion that the universe is eternal

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite arguments against power with the act are met by Ash'ari distinctions about the nature of bringing into existence, the scope of obligation, and the dissimilarity between eternal and created power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your daily actions, do you assume that your power to act exists before you act, or do you recognize that your ability is granted by God at each moment? This lesson challenges you to see your power as simultaneous with the act—how does that change your reliance on God in your next decision?
?
The Ash'ari response to the obligation argument says that it is enough for the act to be generally possible, not actually in your power at the moment of obligation. Think of a command from Allah that you find difficult—like guarding your tongue. Does this teaching make you feel more hopeful or more accountable? Will you turn to Him for that ability in the very moment of the test?
?
The third argument warns against comparing God's power to human power. Where in your life have you been measuring God's ability by your own limited experience? What would it look like to trust that His power is unlike anything you know, especially in a situation where you feel powerless?

Eternal Power and Temporal Acts: A Refinement
Author asserts that eternal power's relation to acts is temporal and responds to al-Amidi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the eternity of divine power does not require the act to be eternal, and how al-Amidi's objection is answered.

Core Idea
Eternal power can relate to temporal acts without making them eternal.

The author begins by addressing a potential misunderstanding: if God's power is eternal, does that mean His acts are also eternal? He clarifies that the relation of power to an act can be temporal even if power itself is eternal. This sets the stage for engaging with al-Amidi's objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening assertion
Read the first part of the passage where the author states that eternal power does not preclude a temporal relation to acts. Note the key terms: قدرة (power), قديمة (eternal), تعلق (relation), مقارن (concomitant), حادث (originated).
2
Examine al-Amidi's objection
The author paraphrases al-Amidi's argument: eternal power cannot relate to an act in pre-eternity because the act was impossible then; it only relates in the future. Identify the logical structure of this objection.
3
Analyze the author's rebuttal
The author notes that the objection from al-Mawaqif does not apply to al-Amidi's view. Then he refines the point about time: even if the act was impossible in pre-eternity, it became possible later, so the relation can be established. This refutation preserves the distinction between eternal power and temporal acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدرة، وكونها قديمة سابقة لا ينافي كون تعلقها مقارناً حادثاً، فلا يلزم من كون تعلق القدرة القديمة مع الفعل قدم الحادث، أو حدوث القديم، ولو حمل ما قال الآمدي : إن القدرة القديمة وإن كانت متقدمة على جميع المقدورات فهي إنما تتعلق بالأفعال الممكنة، والفعل في الأزل غير ممكن، فلا تتعلق به في الأزل، بل فيما لايزال على هذا المعنى لم يرد عليه اعتراض المواقف بأن فيه التزام ما التزمه المعلل مع بيان سبب له في القدرة القديمة، فليجز في الحادثة أيضاً سبب آخر وبأن الفعل في الأزل وإن امتنع لكنه أمكن فيما لا يزال في زمان سابق على الزمان الذي وجد فيه فيجاز تعلق القدرة به، فلو لزمت المقارنة لزم كون الفعل في الزمان السابق دون اللاحق، نعم : يرد أن الكلام في تعلق القدرة بالمعنى الذي يصح قولنا فلان قادر على كذا متمكن من وتركه، وهو لا يتأخر عن نفس القدرة لا بالمعنى الذي إذا نسب إلى المقدور كان صدوره عن القادر، وإذا نسب إلى القدرة كان إيجابها للمقدور، وإذا نسب إلى القادر كان خلقه وإيجاده له فإن هذا مقارن بلا نزاع حادث في حق القديم أيضاً . فعله
Power, and its being eternal and prior does not contradict its relation being concomitant and originated. So it does not follow from the relation of eternal power with the act that the originated is eternal or that the eternal becomes originated. And if one understands what al-Amidi said: that eternal power, even though it precedes all possible objects, relates only to possible acts, and the act in pre-eternity is not possible, so it does not relate to it in pre-eternity, but only in what never ceases to be – in this sense, the objection of al-Mawaqif is not directed at him: that [this view] entails what the arguer already committed himself to, along with explaining a cause for it in eternal power, so let there be another cause also for originated [power]; and that the act in pre-eternity, though impossible, was possible in what never ceases to be, in a time prior to the time in which it existed, so the relation of power to it is permissible; and if concomitance were required, then the act would have to be in the earlier time without the later. Yes, it is objected: the discussion is about the relation of power in the sense that it is correct for us to say 'so-and-so is capable of such-and-such, able to do it and leave it' – and this does not lag behind power itself, not in the sense that when it is related to the object, it is its emanation from the capable agent; when related to power, it is power's necessitation of the object; when related to the capable agent, it is His creation and bringing into existence. For this is concomitant without dispute, originated in the case of the Eternal as well. His act.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage refines the concept of divine power (qudra) by addressing an objection from al-Amidi. The author first asserts that the eternal nature of power does not preclude its relation to originated acts. He then paraphrases al-Amidi's argument: eternal power cannot relate to an act in pre-eternity because the act was impossible then; it relates only in the future. The author notes that the objection from al-Mawaqif—that this view leads to self-commitment and could be applied to originated power—does not apply to al-Amidi's. The author then presents a stronger objection of his own: the relevant sense of 'power' is the capacity to do or leave an act, which does not lag behind power itself. Another sense—where the act is the emanation from the agent—is concomitant and originated even for the Eternal. Thus the discussion is about the first meaning, and the author's position stands.

قديمة
تعلق
مقارن
حادث
الآمدي
مقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal power must have eternal effects.
Correction: The passage shows that the relation of power to an act can be temporal even if power itself is eternal.

Checkpoint

A) Because al-Amidi's view is about eternal power only.
B) Because the act is impossible in pre-eternity but possible later, so the relation is permissible.
C) Because al-Mawaqif's objection only applies to originated power.
D) Because the author agrees with al-Mawaqif.

Key Takeaway
Eternal power relates to acts in time without making them eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's eternal power relates to your every action only when that action becomes possible, does that change how you view your own choices? Will you now see each moment as a unique opportunity granted by His precise timing?
?
The author argues that eternal power does not make your acts eternal. How does this affect your understanding of accountability? Are you ready to take full responsibility for your actions, knowing they are temporally distinct from God's eternal decree?
?
Al-Amidi thought power could not relate to an act in pre-eternity because it was impossible then. Is there an area of your life where you have assumed change is impossible because you think God's power is fixed? How will you begin to act on the possibility that He relates to you in time?

Two Senses of Divine Power
Author distinguishes two meanings of power and clarifies the debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will distinguish between two meanings of 'power' (qudra) and understand which one is relevant to the debate.

Core Idea
Power as capacity is eternal; power as actualization is temporal.

The author concludes by sharpening the terminology. He argues that the sense of power relevant to the discussion is the capacity to do or leave an act, which does not lag behind power itself. The other sense—power as the actual bringing into existence—is temporal even for God. This refines the argument against al-Amidi.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying two senses
Read the final part of the source where the author explicitly distinguishes two meanings of 'power' (قدرة). Pay attention to the phrases: 'الذي يصح قولنا فلان قادر على كذا متمكن من فعله وتركه' and other descriptions.
2
Understand the first sense: capacity to do or leave
The author states that the relevant sense is when we say someone is capable of doing or leaving an act. This sense does not lag behind power itself and is eternal for God.
3
Understand the second sense: actualization
The author presents another sense where power is related to the act as its emanation from the agent, or as power's necessitation, or as creation. This sense is concomitant and originated even for God.
4
See how this resolves the debate
The author concludes that the discussion is about the first sense, which is eternal, while the second sense is temporal. This clarifies that eternal power does not force eternal acts.
