Two Consequences of Power with the Act and the Mu'tazila Objection
Sanusi states two consequences of power with act and records the Mu'tazila's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two logical consequences of the thesis that power is with the act, and the Mu'tazila's objection based on the distinction between constrained and paralyzed.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that prevention does not negate capability.

The author has established that power is inseparable from the act. Now he draws two implications: first, that someone prevented from acting is not capable; second, that one power cannot relate to two objects. The Mu'tazila immediately object, claiming a necessary distinction between the constrained and the paralyzed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the two consequences and the objection. Focus on the terms 'mamnu' (prevented) and 'zamin' (paralyzed).
2
Identify the two consequences
List them: (1) the prevented person is not capable, like the paralyzed; (2) one power does not relate to two objects.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila objection
They distinguish between constrained (muqayyad) and paralyzed (zamin) - prevention does not imply change in essence or attribute.
This is a key point of contention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتفرع على كون القدرة مع الفعل أن الممنوع عن الفعل لا يكون قادراً عليه كالزمن وإن القدرة الواحدة لا تتعلق بمقدورين وإن لم يكونا ضدين . وقالت المعتزلة : الفرق بين المقيد والزمن ضروري كيف وليس فيه تبدل ذات أو صفة ولا طريان ضد للقدرة . واتفقوا على أنها تتعلق بالمتماثلات لكن على تعدد الأوقات . وجوز بعضهم تعليها بالضدين على البدل وتردد أبو هاشم فجوز تارة أن تعلق كل من القلبية والعضوية بمتعلقاتها دون متعلقات الأخرى، وتارة لمتعلقاتها من غير تأثير في متعلقات الأخرى، وتارة خص الحكمين بالقلبية والحق أنه إن أريد بالقدرة القوة التي هي مبدأ الأفعال بطريق الإيجاد، وتسمى القدرة المؤثرة أو بطريق جري العلة، وتسمى الكاسبية فهي قبل الفعل ومعه وبعده وتتعلق بالمقدورين، ونسبتها إلى الضدين على السواء، وإن أريد القوة المستجمعة لجميع شرائط التأثير على أحد الوجهين فهي مع الفعل، ولا تتعلق بمقدورين لاختلاف الشرائط بالنسبة إلى المقدورات. أي الذي منع من فعل من يصح صدوره عنه في الجملة لا يكون قادراً عليه حال المنع كالزمن الذي هو عاجز عن الفعل وأن القدرة الواحدة لا تتعلق بمقدورين سواء كانا ضدين أو مثلين أو مختلفين فإن ما نجده في نفوسنا عند صدور أحد المقدورين غير ما نجده عند صدور الآخر.
And it branches from the fact that power is with the act: that the one prevented from an act is not capable of it, like the paralyzed; and that one power does not relate to two objects even if they are not opposites. And the Mu'tazila said: the distinction between the constrained and the paralyzed is necessary – how so? For there is in it no change of essence or attribute, nor occurrence of an opposite to power. And they agreed that it [power] relates to similar [acts] but over multiple times. And some allowed its relation to two opposites on alternate occasions, and Abu Hashim hesitated: sometimes he allowed that each of the cardiac and muscular [powers] relates to its own objects without affecting the other's objects; sometimes [he allowed] to their objects without effect on the other's objects; and sometimes he specified the two judgments to the cardiac. And the truth is: if by 'power' is meant the faculty that is the principle of acts by way of bringing into existence – called 'effective power' – or by way of the course of habit – called 'acquisitive power' – then it is before the act, with it, and after it, and relates to both objects, and its relation to opposites is equal. But if by 'power' is meant the faculty that has gathered all conditions of effect in one of the two ways, then it is with the act, and does not relate to two objects because the conditions differ with respect to the objects. That is: the one prevented from an act that could in general issue from him is not capable of it during prevention, like the paralyzed who is incapable of the act; and that one power does not relate to two objects whether they are opposites, similars, or different – for what we find in ourselves at the occurrence of one of the two objects is other than what we find at the occurrence of the other.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage presents the author's two branching consequences (تفرع) from the thesis that power is with the act: (1) someone prevented from an act is not capable of it, and (2) one power cannot relate to two objects. The Mu'tazila counter that there is a necessary distinction between a person who is constrained (مقيد) and one who is paralyzed (زمن); prevention does not involve change in essence or attribute. The author then reviews Mu'tazila positions: they agree power relates to similar acts over time; some allow relation to two opposites on alternate occasions; Abu Hashim was uncertain. The author gives the correct view: if power is the effective principle (مبدأ الإيجاد) or the acquisitive power (الكاسبية), it exists before, with, and after the act and relates to objects equally. But if power means the faculty with all conditions satisfied for one of two ways, then it is with the act and cannot relate to two objects due to differing conditions. The last paragraph clarifies: the prevented person is like the paralyzed, incapable during prevention, and one power cannot relate to two objects because our inner experience shows different states for different acts.

Key Terms
قدرة
ممنوع
زمن
مقيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a prevented person is still capable because they could act if prevention were removed.
Correction: The author argues that during prevention, the person is not capable; capability requires absence of prevention. The Mu'tazila object that this conflates constrained (external prevention) with paralyzed (internal incapacity).

Checkpoint
What is the first consequence the author derives from power being with the act?
A) The prevented person is not capable.
B) Power is before the act.
C) Power relates to opposites equally.
D) Power is effective or acquisitive.

Key Takeaway
The author asserts that prevention negates capability, but the Mu'tazila challenge this by distinguishing external constraint from internal paralysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an obstacle in obeying Allah, do you truly examine whether the prevention is external (like circumstances) or internal (like laziness or weak īmān)? In light of this lesson, what one area of your life are you using external excuses for, when the real barrier is inside you? Commit to one specific change this week.
?
The Mu'tazila argued that external prevention does not change your essence or ability. Have you ever excused yourself from a duty by saying 'I can't' when deep down you know you have the capacity? Write down one duty you've avoided and ask: Is this a real prevention or a self-imposed excuse? Decide today to act on it.
?
The author states that a single power cannot relate to two objects simultaneously. In your daily life, do you try to serve multiple priorities at once, diluting your devotion to Allah? Which one object does your power need to focus on right now to draw closer to Him? Make a concrete plan to eliminate distractions.

Mu'tazila Positions on Power's Objects and the Author's Resolution
Sanusi reports Mu'tazila views on power's relation to objects and gives his decisive resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila views on power's relation to similar and opposite acts, and the author's final resolution distinguishing two meanings of power.

Core Idea
Power can relate to two objects equally if it is the effective principle, but only to one if it has all conditions for a specific act.

After the initial objection, the author surveys Mu'tazila positions on whether one power can relate to similar acts or opposites. He then resolves the issue by distinguishing two senses of 'power': as the simple principle of acts, and as the fully conditioned faculty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read from 'واتفقوا' to the end, noting the various opinions.
2
List the Mu'tazila positions
They agree on similar acts over time; some allow opposites on alternate; Abu Hashim hesitated.
Notice the three views of Abu Hashim.
3
Understand the author's resolution
He distinguishes two meanings of power and shows how each leads to different rulings.
This resolves the apparent contradictions.

Objection to Author's View on Capability
Objection to the author's view and clarification of 'difference'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised against the author's view that prevention negates capability and the clarification of what is meant by 'difference' in inner states.

Core Idea
The objection hinges on whether 'difference' refers to relation or essence.

In the previous lesson, the author argued that prevention negates capability. Here, an objection is raised: if by 'difference' in states we mean a relational difference, it is not problematic; but if it means an essential difference, then the word 'power' would be homonymous. This objection forces precision in defining capability.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage carefully to understand the objection and its two scenarios.
The objection begins with 'واعترض بأنه'.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The objection presents two interpretations of 'difference': relational and essential. Notice the consequences for each.
3
Understand why relational difference is harmless
If 'difference' refers only to the relation between capability and its object, it does not undermine the author's position.
This is based on the Imam's statement.
4
Understand why essential difference leads to homonymy
If the difference is essential, then 'power' would be a homonym, which is not the common usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض بأنه إن أريد المغايرة والاختلاف بحسب التعلق على ما قال الإمام: إن مفهوم التمكن من هذا غير مفهوم التمكن من ذاك فغير قادح، وإن أريد تغاير الحالتين بالذات والمفهوم، أو كون القدرة اسماً بمجموع التمكن مع ما به الاختلاف كان لفظ القدرة مقولاً بالاشتراك، ولم يقل به المشترك أحد . ذهبت المعتزلة إلى أن الممنوع قادر والمنع لا ينافي القدرة، وإنما ينافي المقدور سواء كان المنع بل ما به المنع عدمياً كانتفاء شرط وقوع المقدور، أو وجودياً ضداً له كالسكون للحركة، أو مولداً للضد كالثقل المولد للحركة السفلية المضادة للحركة العلوية . واستدلوا بأنا نفرق بالضرورة بين المقيد الممنوع من المشي والزمن
And it is objected: if by 'difference and variation' is meant with respect to relation, as the Imam said: 'the concept of ability for this is not the concept of ability for that' – then it is not damaging. And if by it is meant the two states differ in essence and concept, or that power is a name for the totality of ability with that by which difference occurs, then the word 'power' would be said by homonymy, and none of the common people have said that. The Mu'tazila held that the one prevented is capable, and prevention does not negate capability; it only negates the actual object, whether the prevention is by a privative [cause] like absence of a condition for the occurrence of the object, or by an existential opposite like rest for movement, or by a generator of the opposite like heaviness which generates downward motion opposite to upward motion. And they argued: we necessarily distinguish between the constrained person prevented from walking and the paralyzed.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage contains an objection to the author's stance and the Mu'tazila's own position. The objection is: if the difference in inner states is merely relational (i.e., the concept of ability for one act differs from that for another), it is not a problem; if it is a difference in essence and concept, then the word 'power' would be homonymous, which is not the common usage. Then the author presents the Mu'tazila view: the prevented person is still capable (قادر) because prevention, whether privative or existential or generative, only prevents the act, not the capability itself. Their proof is the necessary distinction between a constrained person (who is prevented) and a paralyzed person (who lacks capability). This distinction shows that prevention does not remove capability.

Key Terms
مغايرة
تمكن
قدرة
مشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think any difference in states of capability undermines the author's view.
Correction: The objection shows that only an essential difference is problematic, not a relational one.

Checkpoint
What does the objection say about a relational difference in capability?
A) It is damaging to the author's view.
B) It is not damaging.
C) It proves the capability is the same.
D) It shows power is a homonym.

Key Takeaway
The objection distinguishes between relational and essential differences in capability, affirming that only the latter would challenge the author's position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily struggles with obedience, have you ever justified failure by saying 'I can't' when really you meant 'I won't'? How does the distinction between relational and essential capability expose the difference between a genuine weakness and a hidden excuse?
?
If Allah's power is absolute and relational differences in your ability are no obstacle, what is one specific area where you have blamed prevention for something that was actually a matter of your will? What will you do differently tomorrow?
?
When you consider the concept of 'power' as it applies to your actions, do you treat it as a fixed essence or as something that shifts with your relationship to the act? How does your understanding of capability need to change to make you more accountable before Allah?

Mu'tazila Proof that the Prevented is Capable
Mu'tazila view and proof that the prevented is capable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila doctrine that prevention does not negate capability and their proof from the necessary distinction between the constrained and the paralyzed.

Core Idea
Prevention negates the act, not the power itself.

The Mu'tazila held a distinctive view: a person who is prevented from acting is still capable of that act. Prevention, whether from lack of conditions or from an opposing force, only prevents the actual occurrence, not the underlying power. They prove this by pointing out that we naturally distinguish between someone tied up (prevented) and someone paralyzed (lacking power).

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila position
Read the Arabic passage stating that the prevented is capable and prevention does not negate capability.
The passage begins with 'ذهبت المعتزلة إلى أن الممنوع قادر'.
2
Identify the three types of prevention
The passage lists three kinds: privative (absence of condition), existential opposite, and generative opposite. Understand each example.
3
Analyze the proof
The Mu'tazila argue from the necessary distinction between the constrained and the paralyzed. This shows prevention does not remove capability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it is objected: if by 'difference and variation' is meant with respect to relation, as the Imam said: 'the concept of ability for this is not the concept of ability for that' – then it is not damaging. And if by it is meant the two states differ in essence and concept, or that power is a name for the totality of ability with that by which difference occurs, then the word 'power' would be said by homonymy, and none of the common people have said that. This passage contains an objection to the author's stance and the Mu'tazila's own position. The objection is: if the difference in inner states is merely relational (i.e., the concept of ability for one act differs from that for another), it is not a problem; if it is a difference in essence and concept, then the word 'power' would be homonymous, which is not the common usage. Their proof is the necessary distinction between a constrained person (who is prevented) and a paralyzed person (who lacks capability). This distinction shows that prevention does not remove capability.

Checkpoint
Why do the Mu'tazila claim that the constrained person is still capable despite prevention?
A) Because the constrained person can still move other limbs.
B) Because we necessarily distinguish between the constrained and the paralyzed.
C) Because prevention is always privative.
D) Because the power is in the soul, not the body.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila teach that prevention does not remove capability, as shown by the necessary distinction between the constrained and the paralyzed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

According to this lesson, is that prevention actually removing your ability, or is it merely blocking the action? What would it look like to acknowledge your capability and take one small step despite the barrier?
?
Are you currently using a privative prevention (e.g., 'I don't have the right environment') as an excuse for not drawing closer to Allah? How can the Mu'tazila distinction between prevention and incapability challenge you to act now rather than waiting for conditions to be perfect?
?
Consider a time you confused being prevented with being paralyzed in your spiritual journey. How does the necessary distinction between the constrained and the paralyzed force you to stop labeling yourself as incapable? What specific prevention are you ready to confront today, knowing your power remains?

Two Meanings of Power (Qudra)
Defines two senses of qudra and their relation to objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct definitions of qudra (power) and how they differ in relation to actions and objects.

Core Idea
Power can mean either a general capacity or a full-conditioned ability — each has different implications.

This passage clarifies that the term 'qudra' is used in two distinct senses. Understanding this distinction is crucial for later discussions about divine attributes and human capacity. Let's examine how the author defines each sense and what it entails for the relation to actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the two definitions
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that the author presents two definitions of power, introduced by 'إن أريد بالقدرة'.
The first definition uses 'القوة التي هي مبدأ الأفعال'; the second uses 'القوة المستجمعة لجميع شرائط التأثير'.
2
Compare the two senses
Identify the key differences: first sense is general, exists before/during/after, relates to two objects; second sense is conditioned, exists only with act, relates to one object.
3
Reflect on implications
Ask yourself why this distinction matters for theological discussions about divine power and human actions.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لمتعلقاتها من غير تأثير في متعلقات الأخرى، وتارة خص الحكمين بالقلبية والحق أنه إن أريد بالقدرة القوة (۱) التي (۲) هي مبدأ الأفعال بطريق الإيجاد، وتسمى القدرة المؤثرة أو بطريق جري العلة (۳) ، وتسمى الكاسبية فهي قبل الفعل ومعه وبعده وتتعلق بالمقدورين، ونسبتها إلى الضدين على السواء، وإن أريد القوة المستجمعة لجميع شرائط التأثير على أحد الوجهين فهي مع الفعل، ولا تتعلق بمقدورين لاختلاف الشرائط بالنسبة إلى المقدورات. أي الذي منع من فعل من (٤) يصح صدوره عنه في الجملة لا يكون قادراً عليه حال المنع كالزمن الذي هو عاجز عن الفعل وأن القدرة الواحدة لا تتعلق بمقدورين سواء كانا ضدين أو مثلين أو مختلفين فإن ما نجده في نفوسنا عند صدور أحد المقدورين غير ما نجده عند صدور الآخر. واعترض بأنه إن أريد المغايرة والاختلاف بحسب التعلق على ما قال الإمام: إن مفهوم التمكن من هذا غير مفهوم التمكن من ذاك فغير قادح، وإن أريد تغاير الحالتين بالذات والمفهوم ، أو كون القدرة اسماً بمجموع التمكن مع ما به الاختلاف كان لفظ القدرة مقولاً بالاشتراك، ولم يقل به أحد.
To its attachments without affecting the other attachments, and sometimes he specified the two judgments as heart-based. The truth is that if by power is meant the force (qudra) which is the principle of actions by way of creation, called effective power, or by way of habit (jary al-‘āda), called acquired (kasbiyya), then it is before the action, with it, and after it, and it relates to two objects (maqdūrayn), and its relation to two opposites is equal. If by power is meant the force that gathers all conditions of effect in one of the two ways, then it is with the action and does not relate to two objects because the conditions differ with respect to the objects. That is, whoever is prevented from an action that could be validly performed by him in general is not capable of it at the time of prevention, like the chronically ill person who is incapable of the action, and that one power does not relate to two objects whether they are opposites, similars, or different; for what we find in our souls at the occurrence of one object is not what we find at the occurrence of another. It was objected that if by difference is meant difference in relation as al-Imām said: the concept of ability for this is different from the concept of ability for that, then it is not fatal; and if by difference is meant the two states differ in essence and concept, or that power is a name for the combination of ability and the factor of difference, then the term power would be homonymous (mushtarak), and no one has said that.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'qudra' (power) in two distinct senses and then addresses an objection regarding whether one power can relate to two objects (maqdūrayn). The author explains that if power is understood as a force that produces actions by creation (effective power) or by habit (acquired power), it exists before, during, and after the act and can relate to two objects equally, even opposites. However, if power is understood as the force that has gathered all conditions of effect in one specific way, it exists only with the act and cannot relate to two objects because the conditions differ for different objects. The author then presents an objection: if the difference between the two states of power applying to two objects is merely a difference of relation (the concept of ability for one is different from ability for the other), then this is not problematic; but if the two states differ in essence or concept, or if 'power' is taken as the combination of ability and the differentiating factor, then the term would be ambiguous (mushtarak) by homonymy, and no one holds that view. The objection is thus answered by showing that the intended difference is the first meaning, which is acceptable.

Key Terms
القدرة (qudra)
المقدور (maqdūr)
الكاسبية (kasbiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power (qudra) can only relate to one object at a time.
Correction: The author explains that in the first sense (effective/acquired power), it relates to two objects equally, even opposites.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about the first sense of power?
A) It exists only at the moment of the action.
B) It relates to two objects equally.
C) It is called conditioned power.
D) It does not relate to opposites.

Key Takeaway
The term 'qudra' has two meanings: a general capacity before/during/after the act relating to multiple objects, and a conditioned ability existing only with the act and specific to one object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on your own abilities, which meaning of qudra are you assuming—the general capacity that exists before, during, and after, or the fully conditioned ability that requires all conditions to be met? How does that assumption affect your tawakkul on Allah?
?
Think of a time you made a plan assuming you had the power to execute it. Did you consider that the power to act is conditioned on factors beyond your control? How can you practically acknowledge the second meaning of qudra in your daily plans?
?
The first meaning of qudra relates to both opposites equally. In moments of temptation, do you recognize that you have both the capacity to sin and the capacity to refrain? How does this awareness change your sense of personal responsibility before Allah?

Objection: One Power and Two Objects
Addresses objection about one power relating to two objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised against the idea that one power can relate to two objects and the author's defense that the difference is only in relation.

Core Idea
The objection fails because the difference between relating to two objects is merely in the relation, not in the essence of power.

After defining two senses of power, the author addresses a potential objection: if one power can relate to two objects, then there must be some difference between its states. The author explains that the difference is only in relation, which is acceptable, and if it were more, the term would be ambiguous, which no one holds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the objection: someone argues that one power cannot relate to two objects because there must be a difference.
2
Analyze the two options
The author presents two possible interpretations of what the difference could be: difference in relation or difference in essence. Understand each.
The first option is attributed to al-Imām.
3
Understand the resolution
The author dismisses the essence difference as leading to homonymy, which is unacceptable, and accepts the relation difference as unproblematic.
This resolves the objection.

Mu'tazila on Capability of the Prevented and Its Refutation
Presents the Mu'tazila claim and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila claim that a prevented person is capable, their two arguments, and the Ash'ari refutation based on divine habit.

Core Idea
Prevention does not negate capability, only the object of power.

In this passage, al-Sanusi presents the Mu'tazila position that a person who is prevented from acting is still capable, and contrasts it with the Ash'ari view that true capability requires the absence of prevention. The Mu'tazila rely on an intuitive distinction and the stability of the soul's attributes, while the Ash'ari response appeals to God's ordinary habit. Understanding this debate clarifies key differences in how capability is conceived.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: first the Mu'tazila claim, then their two arguments, then the two refutations.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila arguments
Find the two arguments: (1) the intuitive distinction between a fettered person and a chronically ill person, (2) the lack of change in the fettered person's essence or attributes.
These arguments aim to show capability remains despite prevention.
3
Analyze the Ash'ari refutations
Examine how each argument is answered: the first by appealing to divine habit, the second by denying the premise of no change in attribute.
The refutation does not deny the possibility but reinterprets the basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهبت المعتزلة إلى أن الممنوع قادر والمنع لا ينافي القدرة، وإنما ينافي المقدور سواء كان المنع بل ما به المنع عدمياً كانتفاء شرط وقوع المقدور، أو وجودياً ضداً له كالسكون للحركة، أو مولداً للضد كالثقل(٥) المولد للحركة السفلية المضادة للحركة العلوية. واستدلوا بأنا نفرق بالضرورة بين المقيد الممنوع من المشي (٦) والزمن (۷) العاجز عنه وما ذاك إلا بوجود القدرة في المقيد دون العاجز، وبأن المقيد لم يلحقه تغير في ذاته ولا صفاته، ولم يطرأ عليه ضد من أضداد القدرة وقد كان قادراً حال المشي ، فكذا مع القيد لأن القدرة من صفات النفس. وأجيب عن الأول بأن الفرق عندنا عائد إلى جري العادة بخلق القدرة في المقيد بارتفاع القيد بخلاف الزمن العاجز فإنه وإن كان ارتفاع العجز ممكناً لكن لم تجر العادة بذلك. وعن الثاني : يمنع عدم التغير في الصفة.
The Mu'tazila held that the prevented person is capable, and prevention does not negate capability, but only negates the object of power (maqdūr); whether the prevention, or rather that by which prevention occurs, is non-existent like the absence of a condition for the object, or existent as an opposite like rest to movement, or something generating an opposite like heaviness generating downward motion opposite to upward motion. They argued: we necessarily distinguish between the fettered person prevented from walking and the chronically ill person incapable of it, and this is due only to the existence of power in the fettered person but not in the incapable person; and because the fettered person undergoes no change in his essence or attributes, and no opposite of power occurs to him, and he was capable at the time of walking, so likewise he is capable with the fetter because power is an attribute of the soul. The answer to the first argument: the difference according to us is due to God's habit (jary al-‘āda) of creating power in the fettered person upon removal of the fetter, unlike the chronically ill incapable person, for even though removal of incapacity is possible, it is not the habit. To the second: we deny that there is no change in the attribute.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila position that a person who is prevented from an action (e.g., a fettered person) is still capable (qādir) because prevention does not negate capability but only the actual performance. They support this with two arguments: first, we intuitively distinguish between a fettered person who is prevented from walking and a chronically ill person who is incapable; the difference is the presence of power in the fettered person. Second, the fettered person undergoes no change in essence or attributes, and no opposite of power appears, so he remains capable. The response refutes both. To the first, the answer is that the distinction is based on God's ordinary habit (jary al-‘āda): He creates power in the fettered person when the fetter is removed, but He does not habitually do so for the chronically ill, even though it is possible. To the second, the Ash'arī denies the premise that there is no change in the attribute, implying that the fetter does affect the attribute of power. The passage thus defends the Ash'arī view that true capability requires the absence of prevention.

Key Terms
الممنوع (al-mamnū‘)
القدرة (qudra)
المنع (man‘)
جري العادة (jary al-‘āda)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prevention and incapacity are the same thing, so a prevented person is incapable.
Correction: The Mu'tazila distinguish between absence of a condition (incapacity) and presence of an obstacle (prevention). A prevented person has power but lacks the conditions for action.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari response, why is the fettered person considered capable?
A) Because power is an attribute of the soul that cannot be negated.
B) Because God habitually creates power in him when the fetter is removed.
C) Because he has no change in his essence.
D) Because he can still move the fetter.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila argue that prevention does not remove capability, but the Ash'ari respond that capability in such cases is due to divine habit, not inherent attribute stability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an obstacle to obeying Allah—like a difficult task or temptation—do you assume you lack the capability entirely, or do you recognize that Allah's habit is to grant you power when the barrier is removed? How can you actively rely on His custom rather than your own perceived inability?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that a fettered person remains capable because power is an attribute of the soul. But the Ash'ari redirect your focus to Allah's habitual creation. In your daily struggles, do you treat your spiritual capabilities as fixed attributes of your soul, or do you constantly depend on Allah's fresh creation of ability in each moment? What would change if you truly believed that every capacity is a divine gift?
?
This passage shows that even a simple distinction like the difference between a fettered person and a sick person can lead to deep theological disagreement. Are there areas in your life where you assume a natural distinction (like 'I can't do this because of my circumstances') without considering that Allah could change the habit? How can you cultivate a mindset that sees obstacles as opportunities for divine intervention rather than fixed limitations?

Mu'tazila on One Power: Similars and Opposites
Consensus on similars and majority view on opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila consensus that one power can relate to similars over time and their majority view that it can relate to opposites by substitution.

Core Idea
One power can handle many similar actions over time, and opposites too—but only by taking turns.

The Mu'tazila engaged in detailed discussions about the scope of a single power (qudra). They agreed that one power can produce multiple similar actions, but only successively, not simultaneously. However, they split on whether it can also produce opposites. This lesson explains their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
Notice the two distinct topics: similars and opposites.
2
Understand the consensus on similars
Identify that the Mu'tazila all agree that one power relates to similars only over successive times. Note the reason: two similars cannot occur at the same time in the same locus.
3
Understand the majority view on opposites
Most Mu'tazila allow one power to relate to opposites via substitution (badal). Their argument: if not, the agent would be compelled to act, which is absurd.
Substitution means one opposite after the other, not simultaneously.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this discussion matters for understanding divine and human capability.
The issue touches on free will and determinism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واتفقت المعتزلة على أن القدرة الواحدة تتعلق بالمتماثلات لكن على مرور الأوقات إذ يمتنع وقوع مثلين في محل واحد بقدرة واحدة في وقت واحد. واختلفوا في تعلقها بالضدين، فجوز أكثرهم تعليقهما بهما على سبيل البدل (۱) ، إذ لو لم يمكن القادر على الشيء قادراً على ضده لكان مضطراً إلى ذلك المقدور حيث لم يتمكن من تركه هذا خلف. وتردد أبو هاشم فزعم تارة أن كلاً من القدرة القائمة بالقلب، والقدرة القائمة بالجوارح تتعلق أفعال محلها دون محل الأخرى، بمعنى أن القائمة بالقلب تتعلق بالإرادات والاعتقادات مثلاً دون الحركات والاعتمادات والقائمة بالجوارح على العكس. وتارة أن كلا منهما يتعلق بالجميع إلا أنها لا تؤثر إلا في أفعال محله مثلاً.
The Mu'tazila agreed that one power relates to similars (mutamāthilāt) but over successive times, because it is impossible for two similars to occur in one locus with one power at one time. They differed concerning its relation to opposites; most of them allowed their relation to them by way of substitution (badal), for if the capable person were not capable of the opposite, he would be compelled to that object (maqdūr) since he could not leave it—this is absurd. Abu Hashim hesitated: he sometimes claimed that each of the power residing in the heart and the power residing in the limbs relates to actions of its own locus and not the other, meaning the heart-power relates to volitions and beliefs for example, not to movements and pressures, and the limb-power is the opposite. And sometimes that each relates to all but only affects actions of its own locus.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage explains the Mu'tazila consensus and disagreement regarding the scope of a single power (qudra). They unanimously agree that one power can relate to multiple similar actions (mutamāthilāt) but only over successive moments; two similars cannot occur simultaneously in the same subject with a single power. However, they disagreed about whether one power can relate to two opposites. Most Mu'tazila permitted it on the basis of alternation (badal), arguing that if a person capable of one action were incapable of its opposite, he would be compelled to perform the first action, which contradicts the notion of capability. Then the author notes the hesitation of Abu Hashim, who wavered between two positions: first, that the power in the heart (for volitions) and the power in the limbs (for movements) each have their own domain and do not interfere; second, that each power relates to all actions but only affects those in its own locus. This shows the subtlety of the Mu'tazila discussion on the scope of power.

Key Terms
المتماثلات (mutamāthilāt / similars)
الضدان (ḍiddān / opposites)
البدل (badal / substitution)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a single power can relate to opposites, it can do so simultaneously.
Correction: The Mu'tazila majority allow only substitution (badal)—one opposite after the other, not at the same time.

Checkpoint
Why do most Mu'tazila say one power can relate to opposites via substitution?
A) To explain the creation of opposites simultaneously.
B) To avoid concluding that a capable agent is compelled to act.
C) Because the power is divided into parts.
D) Because opposites are actually similars.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila agree that one power can produce similars over time, and most hold it can also produce opposites in succession (badal) to avoid compulsion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I had no choice,' are you denying your own power? This lesson shows that even the Mu'tazila argue that true capacity includes the ability to do the opposite. What would change if you reminded yourself that you are capable of the opposite in a difficult situation?
?
The Mu'tazila use the concept of 'badal' to avoid compulsion. Do you live as if you are compelled by your habits or circumstances, or do you affirm your power to substitute one action for another? Which area of your life have you given up on the possibility of change?
?
Think of a time you felt 'stuck' doing something because you thought you couldn't do the opposite. How does the argument from this lesson challenge that feeling? What would it look like for you to exercise your power to substitute today?

Abu Hashim's Position on the Scope of Powers
Abu Hashim's hesitation on the scope of heart and limb powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's two competing theories about the scope of heart-power and limb-power.

Core Idea
Abu Hashim couldn't decide whether each power is restricted to its own locus or relates to all actions but only affects its own.

Within the Mu'tazila, Abu Hashim took a nuanced position on the scope of the two powers: the power residing in the heart (for volitions, beliefs) and the power residing in the limbs (for movements). He wavered between two views, which shows the complexity of the problem. This lesson examines his hesitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on Abu Hashim
Read the Arabic carefully and the translation. Notice the key phrase 'taraddada' (hesitated).
The passage presents two views introduced by 'tāratan...' (sometimes).
2
Understand the two types of power
Identify the two powers: heart-power (qudrat al-qalb) and limb-power (qudrat al-jawāriḥ). Know what each normally causes.
Heart-power: volitions, beliefs. Limb-power: movements, pressures.
3
Examine the first view (domain restriction)
Each power relates only to actions of its own locus and not the other.
4
Examine the second view (universal relation, specific effect)
Each power relates to all actions but only affects those in its own locus.
This allows broader potential but actual specialization.
5
Reflect on the hesitation
Consider why Abu Hashim could not settle on one view. What implications does each view have?
The debate touches on the nature of power and causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واتفقت المعتزلة على أن القدرة الواحدة تتعلق بالمتماثلات لكن على مرور الأوقات إذ يمتنع وقوع مثلين في محل واحد بقدرة واحدة في وقت واحد. واختلفوا في تعلقها بالضدين، فجوز أكثرهم تعليقهما بهما على سبيل البدل (۱) ، إذ لو لم يمكن القادر على الشيء قادراً على ضده لكان مضطراً إلى ذلك المقدور حيث لم يتمكن من تركه هذا خلف. وتردد أبو هاشم فزعم تارة أن كلاً من القدرة القائمة بالقلب، والقدرة القائمة بالجوارح تتعلق أفعال محلها دون محل الأخرى، بمعنى أن القائمة بالقلب تتعلق بالإرادات والاعتقادات مثلاً دون الحركات والاعتمادات والقائمة بالجوارح على العكس. وتارة أن كلا منهما يتعلق بالجميع إلا أنها لا تؤثر إلا في أفعال محله مثلاً.
The Mu'tazila agreed that one power relates to similars (mutamāthilāt) but over successive times, because it is impossible for two similars to occur in one locus with one power at one time. They differed concerning its relation to opposites; most of them allowed their relation to them by way of substitution (badal), for if the capable person were not capable of the opposite, he would be compelled to that object (maqdūr) since he could not leave it—this is absurd. Abu Hashim hesitated: he sometimes claimed that each of the power residing in the heart and the power residing in the limbs relates to actions of its own locus and not the other, meaning the heart-power relates to volitions and beliefs for example, not to movements and pressures, and the limb-power is the opposite. And sometimes that each relates to all but only affects actions of its own locus.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage explains the Mu'tazila consensus and disagreement regarding the scope of a single power (qudra). They unanimously agree that one power can relate to multiple similar actions (mutamāthilāt) but only over successive moments; two similars cannot occur simultaneously in the same subject with a single power. However, they disagreed about whether one power can relate to two opposites. Most Mu'tazila permitted it on the basis of alternation (badal), arguing that if a person capable of one action were incapable of its opposite, he would be compelled to perform the first action, which contradicts the notion of capability. Then the author notes the hesitation of Abu Hashim, who wavered between two positions: first, that the power in the heart (for volitions) and the power in the limbs (for movements) each have their own domain and do not interfere; second, that each power relates to all actions but only affects those in its own locus. This shows the subtlety of the Mu'tazila discussion on the scope of power.

Key Terms
القدرة القلبية (qudrat al-qalb / heart power)
القدرة الجوارحية (qudrat al-jawāriḥ / limb power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abu Hashim held a single definitive position on the scope of powers.
Correction: He famously hesitated (taraddada) between two positions, both recorded here.

Checkpoint
According to one of Abu Hashim's views, what is the difference between the heart-power and limb-power?
A) The heart-power is stronger.
B) The heart-power only relates to internal actions, limb-power only to external actions.
C) They are identical in scope.
D) Only the heart-power relates to opposites.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim wavered between two theories: either each power is restricted to its own locus, or each relates to all but only affects its own.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Abu Hashim hesitated between two explanations of human power. When you face deep uncertainty about your own abilities or limits, do you honestly admit confusion or do you force a conclusion to avoid discomfort? What might this hesitation teach you about intellectual humility before Allah?
?
In the second view, the power relates to all actions but only affects its own locus. In your life, do you ever assume you have more influence over certain areas than you actually do, or do you underestimate your potential? How can you align your self-awareness with the reality of your God-given capacities?
?
This passage shows a respected scholar openly wavering. Are you willing to sit with uncertainty in your faith or do you rush to answers? What would change if you embraced hesitation as part of sincere seeking?

Mutazila on Power Over Similars and Opposites
Mutazila consensus and dispute on divine power's relation to similar and opposite acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mutazila position on the scope of divine power: their agreement that one power relates to similar acts over different times, and their disagreement (with the majority allowing relation to opposites via replacement).

Core Idea
The Mutazila agreed on power over similars over time, but disagreed on opposites—most allowed replacement.

Before we examine specific attributes, al-Sanusi presents the Mutazila perspective on how divine power relates to possible acts. This passage establishes their consensus on one point and their dispute on another, introducing a key concept—badal (replacement)—that will be central to later discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mutazila agreement
Read the first sentence: the Mutazila agreed that one power relates to similar acts but over time, because two similars cannot occur in one locus at one time.
Notice the reasoning: impossibility of simultaneous similars.
2
Identify the disagreement
Read the next part: they differed on relating power to opposites. The majority allowed it on the basis of badal (replacement).
Some Mutazila (like Abu Hashim) did not allow it.
3
Understand the argument for badal
Read the reasoning: if the agent could not do the opposite, he would be compelled to do the act, contradicting ability (qudra).
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واتفقت المعتزلة على أن القدرة الواحدة تتعلق بالمتماثلات لكن على مرور الأوقات إذ يمتنع وقوع مثلين في محل واحد بقدرة واحدة في وقت واحد. واختلفوا في تعلقها بالضدين، فجوز أكثرهم تعليقهما بهما على سبيل البدل، إذ لو لم يمكن القادر على الشيء قادراً على ضده لكان مضطراً إلى ذلك المقدور حيث لم يتمكن من تركه هذا خلف.

(۱) البدل لغة العوض، وبدل الشيء غيره، والخلف منه قال سيبويه : إن بدلك زيد أي بديلك زيد، والأصل في التبديل تغيير الشيء عن حاله. والأصل في الإبدال جعل الشيء مكان شيء آخر. والبدل في اصطلاح الفلاسفة : الشيء الذي تجعله مكان غيره، أو تأخذه عوضاً عنه، وقد استعمل الفيلسوف (تين) لفظ الإبدال في كتاب العقل باعتبار أن البدل إشارة أو علامة تساعدك على إجراء أعمال ذهنية مختلفة من دون أن تحتاج إلى التفكير في الشيء المدلول عليه والحروف التي نستعملها في علم الجبر أبدال تقوم مقام الكميات والألفاظ أبدال تنوب عن الصور الذهنية.
The Mutazila agreed that the one power relates to similar things but over the passage of times, because the occurrence of two similars in one locus by one power at one time is impossible. And they differed on its relation to opposites: most of them allowed their relation to both on the basis of replacement (badal), for if the one capable of a thing were not capable of its opposite, he would be compelled to that object of power since he cannot abandon it—this is a contradiction.

(1) Badal linguistically means compensation, and the badal of a thing is its substitute, and its replacement. Sibawayh said: 'Inna badlaka Zaidun' i.e., your substitute is Zaid. The origin of tabdil is changing a thing from its state. The origin of ibdal is making a thing in the place of another. And badal in the terminology of the philosophers: the thing that you put in place of another, or take in exchange for it. The philosopher (Taine) used the word ibdal in his book 'The Intellect' considering that badal is a sign or symbol that helps you perform different mental operations without needing to think about the signified thing; the letters we use in algebra are substitutes (abdal) standing in for quantities, and words are substitutes representing mental images.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage defines the Mutazila position on the scope of divine power. It begins by stating their agreement: one power can relate to similar acts (muthilat) over different times, because two similar acts cannot occur in one locus (i.e., the same subject) at the same time by one power. Then it notes their disagreement on whether one power can relate to two opposites (diddan). The majority (aktharuhum) allowed it on the basis of badal (replacement), meaning the power can produce one opposite at a time, alternatively. Their reasoning: if the capable agent (al-qadir) could not do the opposite of what he does, he would be compelled to do that act, contradicting his ability to leave it. The passage then gives a detailed linguistic and philosophical definition of 'badal', tracing its usage from Sibawayh's grammar to the philosopher Taine's epistemology, illustrating that letters in algebra are substitutes for quantities, and words are substitutes for mental images.

Key Terms
القدرة الواحدة
المتماثلات
الضدان
البدل
القادر
المقدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mutazila unanimously allowed power over opposites.
Correction: Only the majority allowed it; some (like Abu Hashim) dissented.

Checkpoint
What is the Mutazila position on one power relating to opposites?
A) All agreed it relates to both simultaneously.
B) All agreed it relates to neither.
C) Majority allowed it on the basis of badal (replacement).
D) Majority denied it entirely.

Key Takeaway
The Mutazila majority held that one power can relate to opposites alternately via badal, to avoid compulsion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe you have the power to choose both good and evil, or do you sometimes feel compelled by your desires? How would your life change if you fully owned that power?
?
The Mutazila argued that without the ability to choose the opposite, you would be compelled. In what area of your life are you avoiding the hard choice, pretending you have no alternative?
?
If God holds you accountable for your actions, are you willing to admit that your ability to sin is part of your real capacity? Does that make you more or less grateful for His guidance?

The Concept of Badal: Linguistic and Philosophical Definitions
Detailed linguistic and philosophical definition of badal with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and philosophical meanings of badal, including its use as a substitute in grammar, algebra, and epistemology.

Core Idea
Badal means substitution, allowing one thing to stand for another without direct presence.

The previous lesson introduced badal as a key theological term for Mutazila. Now the author provides a detailed definition, tracing it from its linguistic roots in Arabic grammar to its philosophical usage in logic and epistemology. This clarifies how the concept of replacement works in both everyday language and abstract thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the linguistic definition
Read the first part: badal linguistically means compensation or substitute. Note the example from Sibawayh.
Sibawayh's example: 'Inna badlaka Zaidun' means 'your substitute is Zaid'.
2
Distinguish tabdil and ibdal
Notice the two derived forms: tabdil (changing a thing from its state) and ibdal (making a thing in place of another).
Tabdil is transformation, ibdal is replacement.
3
Explore the philosophical application
Read the philosophers' definition and Taine's use: badal is a sign or symbol that helps perform mental operations without thinking of the signified.
Examples: letters in algebra as substitutes for quantities, words as substitutes for mental images.

The Ash'ari View on Inability and Its Critique
Taftazani presents and critiques the Ash'ari view on inability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that inability relates only to the existent, and Taftazani's critique of this view.

Core Idea
Inability can relate to the non-existent, just like knowledge and will.

Taftazani examines the Ash'ari claim that inability (al-'ajz), as the opposite of power, only relates to existent things. He argues that this position is obstinate and contradicts common understanding. In this passage, he presents the Ash'ari view, then offers a vigorous critique, pointing out that rational people agree that inability can be about bringing about something that does not yet exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: first a distinction, then the Ash'ari view, then a critique.
Taftazani uses a very direct and critical tone.
2
Identify the Ash'ari view
Find where al-Ash'ari's position is stated: inability only relates to the existent, because an existent attribute cannot relate to the non-existent.
This is a core point of disagreement.
3
Understand Taftazani's first objection
Taftazani calls this 'mukabara' (obstinacy). He argues that even if inability were existential, it could relate to the non-existent, like knowledge and will.
This is a direct refutation of the Ash'ari's reasoning.
4
Examine the Quranic challenge example
Taftazani uses the example of those who challenged the Quran. Their inability was to produce something like it (non-existent), not to remain silent (existent). This shows common usage supports his view.
This example appeals to rational consensus.
5
Reflect on the linguistic argument
Taftazani rejects the idea that 'ajz is homonymous; he says it is univocal in referring to inability to do something, not absence of power.
He insists on the customary and common meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدرة ، بل الفرق أن الزمن ليس بقادر والممنوع قادر بالفعل، أو من شأنه القدرة بطريق جري العادة على ما سبق . ويتفرع على كون العجز ضد القدرة ما ذهب إليه الشيخ الأشعري من أنه إنما يتعلق بالموجود كالقدرة لأن تعلق الصفة الموجودة بالمعدوم خيال محض، فعجز الزمن يكون عن القعود الموجود لا عن القيام المعدوم . ولا خفاء في أن هذا مكابرة، وأن العجز على تقدير أن يكون وجودياً وإن لم يقم عليه دليل فلا امتناع في تعقله بالمعدوم كالعلم والإرادة، ولهذا أطبق العقلاء على أن عجز المتحدين لمعارضة القرآن إنما هو عن الإتيان بمثله، لا عن السكوت وترك المعارضة والقول باشتراك لفظ العجز بين عدم القدرة فيكون عدمياً يتعلق بالمعدوم دون الموجود، وبين صفة تستعقب الفعل لا عن قدرة فيكون وجودياً يتعلق بالموجود دون المعدوم خلاف العرف واللغة. ولو سلم فالكلام فيما هو المتعارف الشائع الاستعمال.
Power; rather the difference is that the paralyzed person is not capable, while the prevented person is capable actually, or by his nature power happens by way of customary occurrence as previously. And it branches from the fact that inability is the opposite of power what Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari held, that it (inability) only relates to the existent like power, because the relation of an existent attribute to the non-existent is a pure illusion; so the inability of the paralyzed person is about sitting which is existent, not about standing which is non-existent. And it is not hidden that this is obstinacy, and that inability, even if it were existential—though no proof has been established for it—there is no impossibility in conceiving it in relation to the non-existent like knowledge and will. For this reason, the rational people have agreed that the inability of those who challenged (the Quran) to produce its like is only about bringing something like it, not about silence and leaving off opposition. And the claim that the word 'inability' is homonymous between absence of power (so it is non-existential relating to the non-existent not the existent) and an attribute that necessitates action not from power (so it is existential relating to the existent not the non-existent) contradicts custom and language. And even if conceded, the discussion is about what is customary and common usage.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the Ash'ari position on the nature of inability ('ajz) as the opposite of power (qudra). Taftazani first states a preliminary distinction: the paralyzed person (zaman) is not capable, while the prevented person (mamnu') is capable in actuality or by habit. Then he presents the consequence of considering inability as the opposite of power: al-Ash'ari held that inability, like power, only relates to the existent, because an existent attribute cannot relate to the non-existent—that would be a pure illusion. Thus, the inability of the paralyzed person is about his existent sitting, not about his non-existent standing. Taftazani immediately rejects this as 'obstinacy' (mukabara). He argues that even if inability were considered existential (which has not been proven), there is no impossibility in conceiving it relating to the non-existent, just like knowledge and will relate to the non-existent. As evidence, he notes that all rational people agree that the inability of those who challenged the Quran to produce its like is about bringing something like it (which is non-existent at the time of challenge), not about silence or leaving off opposition (which are existent). He then criticizes the claim that the word 'ajz is homonymous between 'absence of power' (non-existential, relating to non-existent) and 'an attribute necessitating action not from power' (existential, relating to existent). He says this contradicts custom and language. Even if one conceded this homonymy, the discussion is about the common and customary usage of the term, which is univocal in referring to the inability to do something, not a mere absence of power.

Key Terms
قدرة
عجز
وجود
عدم
صفة
معدوم
موجود
مكابرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that inability must be non-existential because it is the opposite of power.
Correction: Taftazani clarifies that inability can be conceived as existential relating to the non-existent, just like knowledge and will.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, why does the Ash'ari view on inability being related only to the existent constitute 'mukabara' (obstinacy)?
A) Because inability is always non-existential and cannot relate to anything.
B) Because rational people agree that the inability of the Quran's challengers was about producing its like, which is non-existent.
C) Because al-Ash'ari denied the existence of inability altogether.
D) Because the paralyzed person can still stand.

Key Takeaway
Inability, as commonly understood, is not limited to existent actions but can refer to the non-existent, as seen in the Quranic challenge and supported by analogies with knowledge and will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself unable to obey Allah in a certain situation, do you secretly believe that your inability is just an excuse—a non-entity that doesn't count—or does Taftazani's argument force you to admit that your inability is a real attribute attached to a specific action you have not done? What would change if you saw your inability as a concrete barrier that you must genuinely overcome with Allah's help?
?
The Quran's challengers were unable to produce anything like it. You have never been called to produce a Quran, but you are called to live by it every day. In what specific area of your life have you convinced yourself you are 'unable' to obey, when in fact your 'inability' is merely a refusal to try? Will you now name that area and commit to seeking the power that Allah has already granted you?
?
If inability can relate to the non-existent, then your failure to perform a good deed in the future is already a real inability present with you now. How does this accountability before Allah humble you? What is one action you have been postponing that you must now confront as an inability you are responsible for?

The Doubt about Sleep Opposing Power
Taftazani discusses the doubt about sleep opposing power, presenting three views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological debate about whether sleep (nawm) is opposed to power (qudra), and the three main positions held by scholars.

Core Idea
Sleep's relationship to power is a case study in how theologians define attributes.

This lesson examines a specific doubt: does sleep negate a person's ability to act? Taftazani presents three views from different schools, showing that even seemingly obvious phenomena require careful theological reasoning. By analyzing this passage, you will see how the concept of opposition (tadadd) is applied to divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the doubt
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the matn states there is hesitation (taraddud) because some actions are possible from a sleeper while most are impossible. The commentary explains the implications.
2
Distinguish the three positions
Identify the views of the Mu'tazila, Abu Ishaq, and al-Qadi. Note how each position relates to the concept of tadadd (opposition).
3
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this debate matters for understanding power (qudra) as an attribute of Allah by analogy to human power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وفي تضاد النوم للقدرة تردد، إذ قد يصدر عن النائم بعض الأفعال، ويمتنع الأكثر). لا خفاء في جواز بعض الأفعال عن النائم وامتناع الأكثر، واختلفوا فيما يصدر. فذهب المعتزلة وبعض أصحابنا إلى أنه مقدور له، وأن النوم لا يضاد القدرة، ونفاه الأستاذ أبو اسحاق ذهاباً إلى التضاد كالعلم والإدراك . وتوقف القاضي وبعض الأصحاب، وللمعتزلة في القدرة تفريعات وتفاصيل لا نطول الكتاب بذكرها .
He said: And regarding the opposition of sleep to power there is hesitation, for some actions may occur from the sleeper, but most are impossible. There is no hiddenness in the permissibility of some actions from the sleeper and the impossibility of most, and they differed over what occurs. So the Mu'tazila and some of our companions held that it is within his power, and that sleep does not oppose power; and al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq negated that, going to the view of opposition like knowledge and perception. And al-Qadi and some companions paused. And for the Mu'tazila there are subdivisions and details which we will not lengthen the book by mentioning.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of whether sleep (nawm) is opposed to power (qudra). It begins with a quotation: 'He said: And regarding the opposition of sleep to power there is hesitation, for some actions may occur from the sleeper, but most are impossible.' The commentator then explains that it is obvious that some actions are possible from a sleeper while most are impossible. The scholars differed over which actions occur. The Mu'tazila and some of the author's companions (i.e., Ash'aris?) held that such actions are within the sleeper's power, meaning sleep does not oppose power. Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq (al-Isfarayini) denied this, asserting that sleep does oppose power, similar to how knowledge opposes perception (i.e., they are mutually exclusive). Al-Qadi (Abu Bakr al-Baqillani) and some companions suspended judgment. Taftazani then notes that the Mu'tazila have further subdivisions and details on power, which he declines to elaborate to avoid lengthening the book.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume sleep absolutely prevents all actions.
Correction: The passage clarifies that some actions are possible from a sleeper, leading to doubt about whether sleep opposes power.

Checkpoint
What was the view of al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq regarding sleep and power?
A) Sleep does not oppose power.
B) Sleep opposes power like knowledge opposes perception.
C) Sleep is irrelevant to power.
D) Sleep only opposes power in the case of voluntary actions.

Key Takeaway
The debate over whether sleep opposes power reveals that theologians carefully analyze even everyday phenomena to understand the consistent application of attribute definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that your ability to act is entirely under your control, forgetting that even in sleep, some actions proceed while others are blocked? How does this debate challenge your confidence in your own power?
?
When you face a difficult question about your faith or actions, do you rush to take a definite stance like the Mu'tazila or Abu Ishaq, or do you have the humility to pause like al-Qadi? Which disposition does your soul truly need right now?
?
If even a simple state like sleep can raise a deep theological dispute about power, how much more careful should you be when attributing any power to yourself or to others? Will you today consciously return the source of all power to its rightful Owner?

Opposition of Power to Character
Taftazani explains why power opposes character based on ease, spontaneity, and equal relation to opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why power (qudra) is considered opposite to character (khuluq) based on the criteria of ease, deliberation, and equal relation to opposites.

Core Idea
Power and character oppose each other because they cause actions in opposite ways.

In this passage, Taftazani explains a subtle point of kalam: how power and character relate as opposites. He defines character as a stable faculty for easy action without deliberation, and then shows how power fails that definition. This clarification helps us distinguish between two fundamental types of causal properties in the soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Understand the flow before digging into details.
2
Identify the definition of character
Locate the definition of character (khuluq) in the passage. Notice the key conditions: it is a faculty (malaka), actions issue with ease (suhula), and without deliberation (rawiya).
3
Understand what is not character
The passage lists several cases that are not character, such as non-firm qualities, faculties for limb actions with ease, equal relation to opposites (like power), and qualities requiring thought. Focus on why power is singled out.
4
Grasp the opposition
Contrast power with character: power produces action without ease and without need for deliberation, and its relation to action and abandonment is equal. This is the opposite of the definition of character, hence they are opposed.
Taftazani references al-Tajrid for additional support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ويضادها الخلق من جهة أنه ملكة تصدر بها الأفعال عن النفس بسهولة، ومن غير روية، وان نسبته إلى الطرفين لا تكون على السوية . يريد أن من الكيفيات النفسانية الخلق، وفسر بملكة تصدر بها عن النفس أفعال بسهولة من غير تقدم فكر وروية فغير الراسخ من صفات النفس لا يكون خلقاً كغضب الحليم، وكذا الراسخ الذي يكون مبدأ لأفعال الجوارح بسهولة كملكة الكتابة أو يكون نسبته إلى الفعل والترك على السواء كالقدرة، أو يفتقر في صدور الفعل عنه إلى فكر وروية كالبخيل إذا حاول الكرم، وكالكريم إذا قصد بالعطاء الشهرة، ولكا كانت القدرة تصدر عنها الفعل لا بسهولة واستغناء عن روية وكانت نسبتها إلى طرفي الفعل والترك على السوية حكم بأنها تضاد الخلق مضادة مشهورة . وهذا ما قال في التجريد أن القدرة تضاد الخلق لتضاد أحكامها .
He said: And it (power) is opposed by character (khuluq) insofar as it is a faculty (malaka) by which actions issue from the soul with ease and without deliberation, and its relation to the two opposites is not equal. He intends that character is among the psychic qualities, and is explained as a faculty by which actions issue from the soul with ease without prior thought and deliberation. So what is not firmly rooted among the qualities of the soul is not character, like the anger of a forbearing person; likewise, the firmly rooted quality that is a principle for actions of the limbs with ease, like the faculty of writing, or whose relation to action and abandonment is equal like power, or which depends for the occurrence of action on thought and deliberation, like the miser when he attempts generosity, and like the generous when he intends fame through giving. And since power produces action not with ease and without need for deliberation, and its relation to the two sides of action and abandonment is equal, it is judged that it opposes character in a well-known opposition. And this is what was said in al-Tajrid: that power opposes character due to the opposition of their effects.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of why power (qudra) is opposed to character (khuluq). It begins with a quotation: 'He said: And it (power) is opposed by character insofar as it is a faculty (malaka) by which actions issue from the soul with ease and without deliberation, and its relation to the two opposites is not equal.' The commentator explains that character is one of the psychic qualities, defined as a faculty from which actions issue easily without prior thought. He then lists what is not character: qualities not firmly rooted (like anger in a forbearing person) and those that are firmly rooted but serve as a principle for limb actions with ease (like writing faculty), or whose relation to doing and leaving is equal (like power itself), or that require thought and deliberation (like a miser trying to be generous, or a generous person seeking fame). Since power produces action not with ease and without need for deliberation, and its relation to the two sides is equal, it is concluded that power opposes character in a well-known opposition. Taftazani then references al-Tajrid (by Nasir al-Din al-Tusi) which states that power opposes character because their effects (ahkam) are opposed.

Key Terms

قدرة
ملكة
كيفيات نفسانية
تضاد
روية
سهولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that power and character are unrelated qualities, both simply attributes of the soul.
Correction: The passage shows they are opposites: power lacks the ease and spontaneity of character, and its relation to action and abandonment is equal, which is contrary to the definition of character.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is power (qudra) considered opposite to character (khuluq)?
A) Because power is a non-existential quality.
B) Because power produces actions with ease and without deliberation.
C) Because power produces actions without ease and its relation to action and abandonment is equal.
D) Because character is always opposed to power by definition.

Key Takeaway
Power opposes character because it lacks ease and deliberation, and its relation to action and abandonment is equal, which is the opposite of the definition of character.
