Slowness in Motion: Philosophers' Objections and Response
Introduces debate on slowness and presents philosophers' first two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on the cause of slowness in motion between philosophers and theologians, including the two objections raised by philosophers and the response that the correlation is merely customary.

Core Idea
Slowness is explained by theologians as rests intercalated, but philosophers object that this would break necessary correlations.

This passage introduces a key debate in Islamic natural philosophy: what causes slowness in motion? The theologians propose that slowness arises from an increase in moments of rest within motion, while the philosophers argue that this would lead to logical absurdities. We will examine the philosophers' first two objections and the theological response, which redefines correlation as customary rather than necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the contrast between the two schools.
2
Identify the two objections
Find the first and second objections. The first deals with simultaneous motions of different distances; the second deals with correlation (talāzum) using examples.
3
Analyze the examples
Understand how the examples of sun-shadow, millstone, and compass illustrate that correlation can hold despite different distances.
Notice the clarification that the shadow is not actually moving.
4
Understand the response
The answer states that the correlation is not intellectually necessary but merely customary (ʿādī). Reflect on what this means for the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يكون السبب في بطئها نفس الإرادة كما في رمي الحجر وتحريك اليد برفق ولا خفاء في سببية هذه الأمور في الجملة. لكن عند الفلاسفة من جهة أنها تصير سبباً لضعف الميل الذي هو العلة القريبة للحركة، فيضعف المعلول. وعند المتكلمين من جهة أنه يكثر حينئذ تخلل السكنات التي لا تخلو الحركة عن ثبوتها، وتختلف بالسرعة والبطء بحسب قلتها وكثرتها، والفلاسفة نفوا ذلك بوجوه : الأول : أنه لو كان البطء لتخلل السكنات لامتنع تلازم حركتين مع إتحاد الزمان واختلاف المسافة بالطول والقصر، لأن الحركة التي في المسافة الأقصر تكون أبطأ ضرورة اتحاد الزمان، واختلاف المسافة فيكون تخلل السكنات فيها أكثر، فيصدق أنه قد لا يتحرك . الثاني : عند تحرك الأول فلا يصدق أنه كلما تحرك الثاني تحرك الأول وبالعكس على ما هو معنى التلازم هذا خلف لكن اللازم باطل لتحقق التلازم مع تفاوت المسافة في صور كثيرة كحركة الشمس مع ما يتخيل من حركة ظلال الأشخاص وإنما قلنا يتخيل لأن الظل عرض لا حركة له بل إنما يطرأ عليه الضوء الأول، أعني الضوء الحاصل من مقابلة جرم الشمس فيرى كأنه يتحرك إلى الانتقاص، أو يزول الضوء الأول فيحدث الظل شيئاً فشيئاً فيرى كأنه متحرك إلى الازدياد، وكحركة طرفي الرحى أعني الدائرة العظيمة والصغيرة، وكحركتي الشعبتين الخارجة والمتوسطة من الفرجار ذي الشعب الثلاث عند رسمها الدائرة العظيمة والصغيرة . وأجيب بأنا لا نسلم تلازم الحركتين بمعنى امتناع الانفكاك عقلاً، وإنما هو عادي يجوز ارتفاعه بأن تتحرك الشمس مع سكون الظل، وكذا في جميع الصور غايته أنه يلزم انفكاك الرحى والفرجار وهو ملتزم .
And the cause of its slowness may be the will itself, as in throwing a stone and moving the hand gently, and there is no concealment of the causality of these things in general. But according to the philosophers, from the aspect that they become a cause for the weakness of the inclination (mayl) which is the proximate cause of motion, so the effect weakens. And according to the theologians, from the aspect that then the intercalation of rests (sakanāt) increases, which motion is never free from their existence, and motion varies in speed and slowness according to their fewness and abundance. The philosophers denied this by several arguments: First: If slowness were due to the intercalation of rests, then the correlation (talāzum) of two motions would be impossible with unity of time and difference of distance in length and shortness, because the motion in the shorter distance would be slower necessarily due to unity of time and difference of distance, so the intercalation of rests in it would be greater, and so it would be true that it might not move. Second: When the first moves, it is not true that whenever the second moves the first moves, and vice versa, according to the meaning of correlation – this is a contradiction. But the necessary is false because of the actuality of correlation with difference of distance in many cases, like the movement of the sun with what is imagined of the movement of people's shadows – and we only say 'imagined' because the shadow is an accident with no motion; rather the first light, I mean the light obtained from facing the sun's body, only newly occurs to it, so it is seen as if moving to decrease, or the first light disappears and the shadow occurs little by little, so it is seen as if moving to increase – and like the movement of the two ends of the millstone, I mean the large and small circles, and like the movement of the two arms, the outer and middle, of the compass with three arms when drawing the large and small circles. And it was answered: We do not accept the correlation of the two motions in the sense of the impossibility of separation intellectually; rather it is customary, it is permissible that it be removed by the sun moving while the shadow is still, and likewise in all cases, at most it necessitates the separation of the millstone and the compass, and that is accepted.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage begins by introducing a debate about the cause of slowness (بطء) in motion. The author notes that sometimes the cause is the will itself, as in gentle movements. However, the philosophers and theologians differ: the philosophers hold that slowness is due to a weakening of the inclination (mayl), which is the proximate cause of motion. The theologians, on the other hand, argue that slowness results from the intercalation of moments of rest (sakanāt) that always occur within motion, and speed or slowness depends on their fewness or abundance. The philosophers reject this view with several arguments. The first argument is that if slowness were due to rest intercalation, it would be impossible for two motions to be simultaneous (متحدة الزمان) but with different distances, because the shorter motion would have more rests and thus could be considered non-motion. The second argument concerns the correlation (تلازم) of two motions: if one moves, the other should also move simultaneously; but examples like the sun and shadow show correlation holds despite different distances, implying slowness cannot be due to rests. The author explains that the shadow is not actually moving but appears to due to changing light. Examples include the millstone and compass. The answer given is that this correlation is not necessary (عقلي) but merely customary (عادي), and can be broken, e.g., the sun moving while shadow stays still, and such separation is acceptable.

Key Terms
بطء
سكنات
تلازم
حركة
فلاسفة
متكلمون
عادي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Correlation between two motions (e.g., sun and shadow) is a necessary logical relationship that cannot be broken.
Correction: The theologians argue that such correlation is not intellectually necessary but merely customary (ʿādī), and it is possible for one to move without the other.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the theologians' explanation for slowness of motion?
A) Weakness of inclination (mayl)
B) Intercalation of rests (sakanāt)
C) The will itself
D) Lack of causality

Key Takeaway
The theologians maintain that slowness in motion results from the intercalation of rests, and the apparent correlation between motions is merely customary, not necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience slowness in your spiritual growth or in achieving a goal, do you blame a lack of inner drive (like the philosophers' weak inclination) or do you see it as God's wisdom in creating pauses? How does your answer affect your patience and trust in His timing?
?
The passage teaches that correlations like effort and result are merely customary, not necessary. Is there a relationship in your life (e.g., prayer and relief, charity and blessing) that you treat as a guaranteed formula? How would your reliance on God change if you truly internalized that these are habits of His will, not obligations?
?
If the sun could move while the shadow stays still, then the laws you depend on for stability are not absolute. What is one assumption about how your life 'must' work that you need to surrender to God's freedom to alter?
